Definitions of Qadīm and Hadith in Kalam
Defines muḥdath and distinguishes technical and colloquial qadīm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of 'originated' (muḥdath) and the different usages of 'eternal' (qadīm) in theological vs. everyday contexts.

Core Idea
Muḥdath has a beginning; qadīm can be technical or colloquial.

After the verbal disagreement, the author clarifies the precise meanings of key terms. He defines the originated and then distinguishes the theological usage of eternal from the everyday one. This lesson anchors the technical vocabulary of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definitions of muḥdath
Read the Arabic text describing muḥdath. Notice the multiple expressions all pointing to one meaning.
The varied phrasing shows that the concept is clear even if the wording differs.
2
Note the multiple expressions that point to one meaning
The passage gives four ways to define muḥdath: having a beginning, having a first, originated in existence, subsequent to the eternal. All mean the same.
This demonstrates the richness of Arabic expression in kalam.
3
Understand the colloquial usage of qadīm and why it is excluded
Read the part about 'qadīm' being applied to old buildings or old men. This is a different sense, not used in theological discussions about the world's eternity.
This prevents confusion between everyday language and technical kalam terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبين الأشعري خلاف في أن صفات الله تعالى هل توصف بأنها قديمة؟ فالأشعري يصفها بذلك لما أنه لا أول لوجودها، وعبد الله بن سعيد مع قدماء أصحابنا يمتنعون عن ذلك، لاستحالة قيام القدم وهو صفة بما وراءها من الصفات، إذ الصفة لا تقوم بها صفة، وإن كانوا قالوا: لا ابتداء لوجودها، فكأن هذا اختلاف في العبارة. وأما المحدث فهو ما لوجوده ابتداء ويقال ما لوجوده أول، وقيل: هو المبتدأ في الوجود، وقيل: هو ما تأخر بوجوده عن الأزلي. وهذه العبارات كلها تنبئ عن معنى واحد قد يطلق اسم القديم على ما لوجوده ابتداء، فيقال: هذا بناء قديم وشيخ قديم، يراد به التقدم في الوجود على غيره بشرط المبالغة، وهذا النوع ليس بمراد في التكلم في العالم أنه قديم أم محدث، والله الموفق.
And between al-Ashʿarī and [the Māturīdīs] there is a disagreement concerning whether the attributes of Allah—exalted is He—can be described as eternal (qadīm)? Al-Ashʿarī describes them as such because they have no beginning to their existence, while ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd together with the early ones among our companions refrain from that, due to the impossibility of eternity (al-qidam)—which is a attribute—subsisting in attributes beyond itself, since an attribute does not subsist in another attribute, even though they said: there is no beginning to their existence. So this is as if a disagreement in expression. As for the originated (al-muḥdath), it is that which has a beginning to its existence, and it is said: that which has a first to its existence, and it is said: it is the originated in existence, and it is said: it is that which is subsequent by its existence to the eternal (al-azalī). All these expressions indicate a single meaning. The name 'eternal' (qadīm) may be applied to that which has a beginning to its existence, so it is said: this is an ancient building and an old man, intending precedence in existence over others with the condition of exaggeration. And this type is not intended in the discussion of whether the world is eternal or originated. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage begins by noting a disagreement between al-Ashʿarī and the early Māturīdīs (represented by ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd al-Qalānisi and others) over whether God's attributes can be described as 'qadīm' (eternal). Al-Ashʿarī permits it because the attributes have no beginning to their existence. The Māturīdīs refrain, arguing that an attribute cannot subsist in another attribute, and since eternity (qidam) is itself an attribute, it cannot be attributed to other attributes. However, both sides agree that the attributes have no beginning; the disagreement is merely verbal. Next, the passage defines 'muḥdath' (originated): it is something with a beginning to its existence, or something that enters existence after non-existence. It then notes that 'qadīm' can colloquially describe something with a beginning but very old (like an 'ancient building' or 'old man'), but this usage is not meant when discussing the world's eternity or origination in theology. The passage ends with 'And Allah is the giver of success.'

Key Terms
قديم (qadīm)
محدث (muḥdath)
أزلي (azalī)
القدم (al-qidam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'qadīm' always means uncreated, so calling a building 'qadīm' implies it is eternal.
Correction: In everyday language, 'qadīm' can mean very old but still created; in theology it means without beginning and is reserved for Allah.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a definition of 'muḥdath' in the passage?
A) That which has a beginning to its existence
B) That which has a first to its existence
C) That which is eternal
D) That which is originated in existence

Key Takeaway
The term 'muḥdath' consistently means something with a beginning, while 'qadīm' in theology means without beginning, distinct from its everyday usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you casually use the word 'eternal' or 'forever' for created things (e.g., 'this moment is eternal')? How might this blur your sense of the uniqueness of Allah's attribute of qidam? Commit to using precise language today that honors the distinction between Creator and creation.
?
When you hear the phrase 'the world is created', does it truly sink in that everything you see had a beginning? Let that reality humble you—how often do you live as if this world is permanent? What can you do today to remind yourself that it is all muḥdath and will pass away?

The Impossibility of an Eternal Thing Preceded by Temporal Events
The author refutes the claim that an eternal thing could be preceded by temporal events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that an eternal thing cannot be preceded by temporal events, and why a decisive proof outweighs any analogy to possible things.

Core Idea
What is truly impossible cannot be made possible by analogy.

In this compact passage, the author engages with an opponent who might argue that sharing characteristics with temporal things does not prevent something from being eternal. He clarifies that if a decisive proof establishes impossibility, then no amount of analogy to possible things can overturn it. Ignorance of the distinction is permissible for the opponent, but error in a decisive proof is not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full literal translation. Let the argument's flow sink in.
Notice the dense style; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the core claim and refutation
The author states that something that shares the characteristic of being preceded by events cannot be eternal. Find where he refutes the opponent's potential analogy to possible things.
Look for the phrase 'والمستحيل لا يمكن تصحيحه بما يظن أنه نظيره من الممكنات'.
3
Understand the role of decisive proof
The argument hinges on 'دليل مقطوع به' (decisive proof) versus 'ضرورة' (necessary knowledge). Reflect on why error in a decisive proof is impossible, while ignorance is possible for the opponent.
This shows the epistemological hierarchy in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس بقديم لم يوجب استحالة قدمها. بينا استحالة قدم ما لم يسبق الحوادث لمشاركته الحوادث فيما كانت لأجله حادثة. والمستحيل لا يمكن تصحيحه بما يظن أنه نظيره من الممكنات. بل إذا ثبت استحالة هذا بدليل مقطوع به وثبت إمكان ذلك عرف بطريق الضرورة أن بينهما مفارقة وإن جهلها الخصم، لأن الجهل عليه جائز ممكن، والخطأ على الدليل المقطوع به ممتنع، وكذا التسوية بين الممتنع والممكن ممتنعة، فكذا الحكم بجهله بالتفاوت بين الأمرين مع أن الجهل عليه أولى من الحكم ببطلان ما علم بالدليل الضروري.
It is not eternal without necessitating the impossibility of its eternity. We have demonstrated the impossibility of the eternity of what does not precede temporal events because it shares with temporal events the characteristic for which they are temporal. And the impossible cannot be validated by what is thought to be its analogue among possible things. Rather, if the impossibility of this is established by a decisive proof and the possibility of that is established, it is known by necessary means that there is a distinction between them, even if the opponent is ignorant of it. For ignorance is permissible and possible for him, and error with respect to a decisive proof is impossible. Likewise, equating the impossible and the possible is impossible. Thus, the judgment of his ignorance of the difference between the two matters, given that ignorance is more appropriate for him than judging the invalidity of what is known by necessary proof.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational argument in kalam against the possibility of an eternal thing being preceded by temporal events. The author begins by stating that if something is not eternal [qadim], it does not necessitate the impossibility of its eternity—but the opponent claims that something could be eternal despite being preceded by events. The author responds that we have already demonstrated the impossibility of the eternity of anything that does not precede temporal events, because it shares with temporal events the very characteristic (i.e., being preceded) that makes them temporal. He then addresses a possible objection: the opponent might point to something that is possible (i.e., a created thing that is not eternal) and claim it is analogous. The author argues that what is impossible cannot be validated by appealing to what is thought to be its analogue among possible things. Instead, if the impossibility of one case is established by a decisive proof (dalil maqtu' bih), and the possibility of another case is established, then necessary knowledge (darura) tells us there is a distinction (mufaraqa) between them, even if the opponent is ignorant of it. Ignorance is possible for the opponent, but error with respect to a decisive proof is impossible. Therefore, to equate the impossible and the possible is itself impossible. The conclusion is that it is more appropriate to attribute ignorance to the opponent than to reject what is known by necessary proof.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
مستحيل
ممكن
دليل مقطوع به
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if something is similar to possible things in some respect, then it could be possible, even if a decisive proof shows it is impossible.
Correction: The author clarifies that impossibility established by decisive proof cannot be overturned by analogy to possible things. The two cases must be distinguished.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for something that does not precede temporal events to be eternal?
A) Because it is created in time.
B) Because it shares with temporal events the characteristic that makes them temporal.
C) Because there is no proof for its eternity.
D) Because the opponent is ignorant.

Key Takeaway
A decisive proof (dalil maqtu' bih) establishes impossibility, and no analogy to possible things can override that; ultimate knowledge is based on necessary proof, not speculation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever convinced yourself that a sin is acceptable because you saw others doing it, even though you knew it was forbidden? How does this lesson's warning against false analogy challenge that tendency in your own life, and what will you do the next time you feel that pull?
?
When someone presents a seemingly similar case to argue against a clear truth of your faith—like the necessity of prayer or the prohibition of interest—do you find yourself doubting the truth? How can you fortify your heart to trust the decisive proofs of revelation over deceptive analogies, even when you cannot articulate the distinction?
?
The author says it is more appropriate to attribute ignorance to the opponent than to reject the necessary proof. In your daily interactions, are you quick to question the clear commands of Allah when someone challenges them? What would it cost you to firmly trust Allah's decisive proofs and respond with patience and clarity, rather than doubt?

Extending Impossibility from One Event to All Events
Nasafi extends the impossibility of eternity from one event to all events and distinguishes temporality from non-everlastingness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that if a thing cannot be eternal due to one event, it cannot be eternal due to all events, and the distinction between temporality and non-everlastingness.

Core Idea
The impossibility of eternity is based on the characteristic, not the count of events.

This passage builds on the previous argument about temporality. Imam Nasafi uses an analogy to show that if a thing is impossible to be eternal because it is preceded by a single event, it is equally impossible to be eternal if preceded by all events. He then clarifies a potential confusion about persistence. Let's read the passage carefully to grasp these logical extensions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the key analogy
Find the part where the author compares 'not preceding one event' to 'not preceding all events' and notice how he argues they have the same ruling.
The key phrase is 'فكذلك ما لم يسبق جميع الحوادث'.
3
Notice the nuance about persistence
The author then addresses a separate point: being not free from a single thing that cannot persist does not necessitate impossibility of persistence; likewise for all such things.
This is a distinct but related logical point.
4
Understand the distinction
Reflect on the difference between a temporal thing (not eternal) and a thing that is not everlasting. The author clarifies that these are different categories.
This distinction is crucial for the overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: أليس أن ما لا يتقدم على حادث واحد متعين يستحيل أن يكون قديما لمساواته إياه في المعنى الموجب للحدوث، فكذلك ما لم يسبق جميع الحوادث، وما لا يتعرى عن شيء واحد متعين يستحيل بقاؤه لا يوجب أن يستحيل بقاؤه، وكذا ما لا يتعرض عن جميع ما يستحيل بقاؤه لا يوجب استحالة بقائه، وقد ظهر لكم الفرق بين الأمرين عند الاعتبار بواحد معين من كل واحد من الجنسين، أعني ما ليس بقديم بل هو حادث وما ليس بباق.
Then we say: Is it not that whatever does not precede a single predetermined event is impossible to be eternal because it equals it in the meaning that necessitates temporality? Thus, likewise, whatever does not precede all events—and whatever is not free from a single predetermined thing, its remaining is impossible, does not necessitate that its remaining is impossible; and likewise, whatever is not free from all whose remaining is impossible does not necessitate the impossibility of its remaining. And the difference between the two matters has become clear to you when considering a specific individual from each of the two categories, namely, what is not eternal but is temporal, and what is not everlasting.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a logical extension of the previous argument. The author argues by analogy: if something is impossible to be eternal because it is preceded by a single specific temporal event (i.e., it shares the same reason for temporality), then similarly, something that is preceded by all temporal events is also impossible to be eternal. The author then addresses a potential nuance: the fact that something is not free from a single specific thing whose existence is impossible to persist does not imply that its persistence is impossible; likewise, being not free from all things whose persistence is impossible does not imply the impossibility of its persistence. This distinguishes between the case of a temporal thing (which is not eternal) and a thing that is not everlasting (which may still have persistence for a while). The key point is that the impossibility of eternity is determined by the characteristic of being preceded by events, not by the number of events.

Key Terms
حادث
قديم
باق
استحالة
مساواة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is not free from a single thing that cannot persist, then its own persistence is impossible.
Correction: The author argues that being not free from such a thing does not necessitate impossibility of persistence; it is a separate matter.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the author's argument that a thing preceded by all events cannot be eternal?
A) Because it is equal to a thing preceded by one event in the meaning that necessitates temporality.
B) Because it is preceded by more events, making it more impossible.
C) Because it is not free from a single thing.
D) Because it is not everlasting.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of eternity is determined by the characteristic of being preceded by events, not by the number of events; and being not free from transient things does not imply impossibility of persistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that being not free from transient things does not mean your own persistence is impossible. Think of a fear you hold—perhaps failing a task or losing a relationship. How does this logical distinction free you from believing that your stability depends on that one thing? What step can you take today to anchor your persistence in something truly everlasting in your life?
?
Nasafi distinguishes between something that is not eternal (has a beginning) and something that is not everlasting (will have an end). What is one thing in your life that you treat as if it will never end, even though it clearly began? How does the recognition of its temporality change the way you invest your heart and efforts in it starting now?

Refuting Abu al-Hudhayl on the Finiteness of God's Power
Refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl's view that God's power ends, showing it leads to kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu al-Hudhayl's claim that God's power is finite leads to the conclusion that God's essence is finite, and why this is considered clear disbelief in Maturidi theology.

Core Idea
Confusing past and future infinities leads to denying God's power.

One of the most serious errors in kalam is to treat the impossibility of an infinite past regress as equivalent to the impossibility of an infinite future. Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf made this mistake, and it led him to claim that God's power—and therefore reward and punishment—must eventually end. In this lesson, we trace his argument and see how the author of the Tabṣirah exposes its devastating consequence: if power is identical to essence, finiteness of power means finiteness of essence, which is outright kufr.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the author presents al-Allaf's position and then refutes it.
Pay attention to the logical chain: position → argument → refutation.
2
Understand al-Allaf's error
Al-Allaf failed to distinguish between the impossibility of an eternal past (what does not precede events cannot be eternal) and the possibility of an endless future (what is not free from what can endure can endure endlessly). Identify this distinction in your own words.
This is a classic mistake in early kalam; many theologians addressed it.
3
Trace the implication for God's essence
Al-Allaf held that God's power is identical to His essence. If power is finite (because its objects are finite), then His essence must also be finite. Follow this inference in the passage.
The author uses al-Allaf's own premises to derive a conclusion al-Allaf would reject.
4
Reflect on the verdict of kufr
The author concludes that this view amounts to clear disbelief. Ask yourself: why is the implication so severe? How does it contradict the Quran and the consensus of Muslims?
The verse about eternal reward and punishment is directly denied by al-Allaf's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تناهي مقدورات الله عند العلاف ومناقشة ذلك ]: العلاف رئيس المعتزلة إلى القول بتناهي مقدورات الله تعالى وانقطاع ثواب المؤمنين وعذاب الكفرة في الآخرة فإنه لما عجز عن التفرقة سوى بينهما في الاستحالة وقال كما يستحيل قدم ما لا يسبق الحوادث يستحيل بقاء ما لا ينفك عما يستحيل بقاؤه إلى مالا يتناهى وجعل كل واحد منهما متناهيا فأداه جهله بالتفرقة بين الأمرين وعجزاه عن ذلك إلى إنكار نصوص كتاب الله تعلى وإلى تعجيز الله تعالى مع بقاء ذاته مع أنه يقول: الله تعالى يقدر بقدرة هي ذاته، فإذا كانت قدرته ذاته وقد تناهت القدرة التي هي ذاته لما أن تناهى المقدورات بتناهي القدرة فكان هذا تناهي ذاته لا محالة، وهذا كله كفر صريح عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك بفضله.
[The finiteness of God's powers according to al-Allaf and discussion thereof]: al-Allaf, the leader of the Mu'tazila, [was led] to the statement of the finiteness of God's powers and the cessation of believers' reward and disbelievers' punishment in the Hereafter. For when he was unable to distinguish, he made both equivalent in impossibility. He said: just as it is impossible for that which does not precede originated things to be eternal, so it is impossible for that which is not free from what cannot endure to endure endlessly. He made each of them finite. His ignorance of the distinction between the two things and his inability regarding that led him to deny the texts of God's Book and to declare God incapable while His essence remains, despite his saying: 'God is powerful with a power that is His essence.' If His power is His essence, and the power which is His essence has become finite because the objects of power become finite [following] the finiteness of power, then this would be the finiteness of His essence necessarily. All of this is clear disbelief; may God protect us from that by His grace.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf's view that God's power is finite. The author begins by stating al-Allaf's position: God's powers (mighty acts) are finite, and reward and punishment in the afterlife will end. The reason given is that al-Allaf failed to distinguish between the impossibility of an infinite past regress and the possibility of an infinite future regress. He mistakenly argued that just as a being that does not precede events cannot be eternal, so a being that is not free from what cannot endure cannot endure endlessly. The author points out that this confusion led al-Allaf to deny clear Quranic texts about eternal reward and punishment, and to ascribe impotence to God. The author then highlights the contradictory implication: al-Allaf holds that God's power is identical to His essence. If His power is finite (because its objects are finite), then His essence would also be finite. This is outright disbelief (kufr). The author concludes with a prayer for protection.

Key Terms
تناهي المقدورات
تعجيز الله
قدرة هي ذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that finiteness of God's power is a harmless or even reverent position, since it limits what God can do.
Correction: The passage shows that finiteness of power, especially when power is identified with essence, leads to finiteness of God's essence itself. This contradicts the very concept of God and is clear kufr.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim is the consequence of Abu al-Hudhayl's view that God's power is finite?
A) God's mercy ends.
B) God's essence becomes finite.
C) God becomes unable to create.
D) God's knowledge becomes finite.

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Hudhayl's error of conflating past and future infinities leads to the disastrous conclusion that God's essence is finite, which is outright disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that, when logically extended, contradicted a clear Quranic text? Are you willing to abandon your reasoning for the sake of the Book of Allah?
?
If you truly believe that God's power is infinite and unrestricted, how does that belief shape your response to trials where you feel helpless? Where in your life do you act as if His power has limits?
?
Al-Allaf was a respected scholar, yet his error led to kufr. Is there any respected opinion or teacher you follow whose views, if taken to their conclusion, would lead you away from the truth? Are you courageous enough to examine that?

The Eternity of God's Attribute and the Temporality of the World
Nasafi argues that the eternal attribute of bringing into existence does not imply the world's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's eternal attribute of bringing into existence does not necessitate the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
An eternal cause does not require an eternal effect.

This lesson addresses a key objection: if God's attribute of bringing into existence is eternal, then the world should likewise be eternal. Nasafi clarifies that the attribute is eternal but the world is temporal because its existence depends on God's will and action in time.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage carefully, noting the logical flow from the affirmation of the attribute's eternity to the refutation of the implication that the world is eternal.
Pay attention to the two reasons Nasafi gives.
2
Identify the claim and refutation
The claim is that an eternal attribute would necessitate an eternal world. Nasafi refutes this with two arguments: the impossibility of an eternal dependent being and the distinction between the attribute and its effect in time.
3
Understand the first reason
The world's existence is dependent on God (muttaṣil bi-ghayrihi), so it cannot be eternal because an eternal being must be independent.
This is based on the earlier principle that only God is eternal and self-sufficient.
4
Understand the second reason
The attribute of bringing into existence (ījād) does not bring the world into existence at every moment, but only at the time of its existence, which is temporal.
This shows that the attribute's exercise is temporally conditioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : وجود العالم يتعلق بإيجاد الله تعالى إياه، والإيجاد صفة الله تعالى وراء ذاته أزلية قائمة بذات الباري جل وعلا على ما نبين في مسألة التكوين والمكون وقدمه لا يقتضي قدم العالم لما مر من استحالة قدم ما تعلق وجوده بغيره، ولأن الإيجاد ما كان ليوجد المكون للحال بل كان ليوجد وقت وجوده على ما نبين في تلك المسألة إن شاء الله تعالى. وهكذا الجواب عن قولهم : إن وجود الشيء لا من شيء غير معقول فإنا أثبتنا ذلك بالدليل العقلي والمعقول ما يعرف ثبوته بالدليل العقلي، وقد ثبت ذلك بحمد الله تعالى، فكان معقولا غير أنه ليس بموهوم لأن الوهم من انتاج الحس إذ هو انطباع صورة المحسوس في المفكرة بعد زواله عن الحس فمن أراد أن يعرف ما غاب عن الحس بما هو من نتائج الحس وهو الوهم فقد أراد معرفة الشيء بغير ما وضع لمعرفته من أسباب المعارف، فصار كمن أراد أن يميز بين الألوان بسمعه وبين الأصوات ببصره ويعرف طعم الأجسام بيده وهذا جهل مفرط، وهذا لأنه لم ير إيجاد الأجسام ممن له قدرة إيجادها ومن رأى من الفاعلين لم يكن لهم قدرة إيجادها فلم يتصور ذلك في وهمه لبعده عن حسه، فظن أن ما ليس بموهوم ليس بمعقول جهلا منه بالفرق بين المحسوس والمعقول، على أنه رأى حدوث الصنعة في المادة لا عن أصل، إذ الصنعة توجد في المحل أما الصنعة فلا تحدث عن صنعة أخرى تكون مادة للأولى، وكذا الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق فإذا عرف هو وجود شيء لا من شيء فبطل كلامه والله الهادي للعباد إلى سبيل الرشاد.
Then we say: The existence of the world is dependent on God's bringing it into existence (ijād), and bringing into existence is an attribute of God beyond His essence, eternal, subsisting in the essence of the Creator glorified and exalted, as we will explain in the question of takwīn and mukawwan. And its eternity does not necessitate the eternity of the world, due to what has passed of the impossibility of the eternity of that whose existence is dependent on another. And because bringing into existence (al-ījād) was not to bring the brought-into-existence (al-mukawwan) into existence at the moment, but rather to bring it into existence at the time of its existence, as we will explain in that question, God willing. And likewise the answer to their saying: The existence of something from nothing is inconceivable (ghayr ma'qūl). For we have established that by rational proof (dalīl 'aqli), and the rationally conceivable (al-ma'qūl) is that whose existence is known by rational proof. And that has been established by praise of God. So it is rationally conceivable, but it is not imagined by estimation (mawhūm). Because estimation (al-wahm) is a product of sense perception (al-ḥiss), as it is the impression of the image of the sensed in the faculty of imagination (al-mufakkira) after its departure from sense. So whoever wants to know what is absent from the senses by means of what is a product of the senses, namely estimation, wants to know a thing by other than the means of knowledge established for its knowledge. He becomes like one who wants to distinguish colors by his hearing, sounds by his sight, and to know the taste of bodies by his hand—this is extreme ignorance. And this is because he has not seen the bringing-into-existence of bodies from one who has the power to bring them into existence, and those he has seen of agents did not have the power to bring them into existence, so that was not imagined in his estimation due to its remoteness from his sense. So he thought that what is not imagined by estimation is not rationally conceivable, out of ignorance of the difference between the sensed (al-maḥsūs) and the rational (al-ma'qūl). Moreover, he has seen the temporal origination of craftsmanship (al-ṣun'a) in matter not from an origin, for craftsmanship occurs in the locus, but craftsmanship does not occur from another craftsmanship that would be matter for the first. Similarly, motion and rest, conjunction and separation. So if he knows the existence of something from nothing, his speech is invalid. And God is the guide of servants to the path of right guidance.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation by addressing two points: First, the relationship between God's eternal attribute of bringing into existence (ījād) and the temporal world. The world's existence depends on this attribute, but the attribute being eternal does not make the world eternal because the world's existence is dependent on another (God). Second, the objection that 'ex nihilo' (something from nothing) is inconceivable. Nasafi argues it is rationally conceivable (ma'qūl) even though it is not imagined by estimation (wahm). He explains that estimation is based on sense perception, so it cannot grasp things beyond the senses. Those who reject rational truths because they cannot imagine them are like someone trying to distinguish colors by hearing—an extreme ignorance. He also notes that we observe temporal origination of artifacts from matter, but that does not negate absolute origination from nothing, which is rationally established. The passage emphasizes the distinction between the realm of sense and the realm of reason.

Key Terms
إيجاد
أزلية
تكوين
مكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because God's attribute of bringing into existence is eternal, the world must also be eternal.
Correction: Nasafi explains that the attribute is eternal but the world's existence is dependent and temporally conditioned, so it is not eternal.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does the eternity of the attribute of bringing into existence not make the world eternal?
A) The attribute is not real.
B) The attribute is created.
C) The world's existence is dependent on another.
D) The attribute is not eternal.

Key Takeaway
The eternity of God's attributes does not force the world to be eternal because the world's existence depends on God's will and occurs in time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attribute of bringing into existence is eternal, yet He chose to bring the world into being at a specific time, what does that tell you about His sovereignty over time? Do you truly believe that God's timing for events in your life is purposeful, even when you desire immediate answers?
?
When you feel that God is 'delaying' an answer to your prayer, do you slip into thinking that if God were truly powerful, He would have acted instantly? How does this lesson's distinction between an eternal attribute and a temporal effect correct that impatience?
?
Consider that your own existence is entirely dependent on God's bringing into existence at a precise moment. How does that reality affect your attitude toward humility and gratitude today? Will you consciously acknowledge your contingency in your next moment of self-reliance?

The Rational Conceivability of Creation Ex Nihilo
Nasafi defends the rational conceivability of creation ex nihilo by distinguishing between reason and imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creation from nothing is rationally conceivable even though it cannot be imagined through sense perception.

Core Idea
What is rationally conceivable may be beyond sensory imagination.

This lesson addresses the objection that creation from nothing ('ex nihilo') is inconceivable (ghayr ma'qūl). Nasafi distinguishes between rational conceivability (ma'qūl) and sensory estimation (wahm). He explains that wahm is limited to sense impressions, so it cannot grasp things beyond the senses, but rational proof can establish them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the whole passage once, noting the objection and Nasafi's response.
The passage contains an analogy and examples.
2
Understand the definition of ma'qūl
Nasafi defines ma'qūl as that known by rational proof. He asserts that creation ex nihilo is established by reason.
3
Grasp the distinction between ma'qūl and mawhūm
Wahm (estimation) is derived from sense impressions. Because ex nihilo is not sensed, it cannot be imagined, but that does not mean it is irrational.
The analogy of distinguishing colors by hearing illustrates the absurdity.
4
Consider the example of craftsmanship
Even in ordinary experience, we see temporal origination (ḥudūth) of artifacts from matter, but that does not negate absolute origination from nothing, which is a higher rational truth.
Motion and rest are cited as further examples.

The Mu'tazilite Claim and the Trilemma
Imam al-Nasafi presents the Mu'tazilite claim and the three possible interpretations of origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite denial of creation as an attribute and the three possible meanings of 'the world is originated' that frame the refutation.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite position reduces to a trilemma about what 'origination' means.

This lesson introduces the central dispute: the Mu'tazilites claim that bringing into existence (al-ihdath) is not an attribute of God but is identical to the thing brought into existence. Imam al-Nasafi then poses a critical question that exposes three logical options for what 'the world is originated' could mean. By grasping these options, you set the stage for the detailed refutation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the first sentence carefully: the opponent says al-ihdath is not an attribute but 'ayn al-muhdath (the essence of the originated thing). This is the view being refuted.
Note that this denies a real attribute of creation distinct from God.
2
Identify the three options
The question presents three possible answers to 'what caused the world to be originated?': (1) an attribute of God, (2) the world itself, (3) something distinct from God and not an attribute. Find these in the text.
These three options are exhaustive in the context.
3
Reflect on the strategy
Why does Imam al-Nasafi start with this trilemma? Think about how it forces the opponent to choose and then be refuted.
This is a typical dialectical method in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من زعم منهم أن الأحداث ليس بصفة للباري عز وجل بل عين المحدث، فيقال: ما معنى قولكم بأن العالم محدث، أكان محدثا بأحداث هو صفة الله تعالى قائمة به أو بإحداث هو عين المحدث أو بإحداث هو غير المحدث وليس بصفة الله تعالى قائمة به؟ فإن قالوا: حدث بإحداث هو صفة الله تعالى، قيل هذا ترك منكم مذهبكم وإن زعموا أنه حدث بإحداث هو غير الله تعالى، قيل أقديم هو أم محدث؟ فإن قالوا هو قديم فقد تركوا مذهبهم وإن قالوا هو محدث، قيل: إن حدث أيضا بإحداث آخر أم لا بإحداث؟ فإن قالوا: لا بإحداث فقد أحالوا وعطلوا الصانع حيث جوزوا حدوث حادث لا بإحداث، وإن قالوا حدث بإحداث آخر سئلوا عن الإحداث الثاني والثالث فيتسلسل إلى غير نهاية، وفيه تعليق حدوث العالم بما يستحيل ثبوته، وإن قالوا حدث بإحداث هو ذاته، فقد جعلوا ذات العالم في العدم إحداثا وهو في الأزل كان ذاتا وكان جوهرا وأعراضا وأجساما فكانت محدثة في الأزل، والموجود في الأزل لن يكون محدثا بل يكون قديما، فإذا كان العالم قديما موجودا في الأزل، فاعترضوا على هذا وزعموا أن معنى قولنا: إن العالم حدث أي حصل على حالة لم يكن عليها قبل هذه الحالة، معنى راجع إلى ذات العالم أم هي معنى وراء ذات العالم فبأي الجواب أجابو فقد أبطلنا، وتبين أنه حصل بهذا
Then among them is one who claims that bringing into existence [al-ahdath] is not an attribute of the Exalted and Glorious Creator, but rather it is the very thing brought into existence [al-muhdath]. So he asks: What is the meaning of your statement that the world is originated? Was it originated by a bringing into existence that is an attribute of God, may He be exalted, subsisting in Him, or by a bringing into existence that is identical to the originated thing, or by a bringing into existence that is other than the originated thing and not an attribute of God, may He be exalted, subsisting in Him? If they say: It occurred by a bringing into existence that is an attribute of God, it is said: This is an abandonment of your school. And if they claim that it occurred by a bringing into existence that is other than God, it is said: Is it eternal or originated? If they say it is eternal, they have abandoned their school. And if they say it is originated, it is said: Did it occur by another bringing into existence or not by a bringing into existence? If they say: Not by a bringing into existence, then they have committed an absurdity and rendered the Creator idle, by allowing the occurrence of an originated thing without a bringing into existence. And if they say it occurred by another bringing into existence, they are asked about the second and third bringings into existence, leading to an infinite regress, and in that is the suspension of the occurrence of the world upon what is impossible to establish. And if they say it occurred by a bringing into existence that is identical to its essence, then they have made the essence of the world in non-existence a bringing into existence, and it was in pre-eternity an essence and a substance and accidents and bodies, so they were originated in pre-eternity, and what exists in pre-eternity cannot be originated but rather is eternal. So if the world is eternal, existing in pre-eternity, then they objected to this and claimed that the meaning of our statement that the world is originated means it came to be in a state it was not in before that state—does this meaning refer to the essence of the world or is it a meaning beyond the essence of the world? Whichever answer they give, we have refuted it, and it is made clear that by this they have obtained...
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical refutation of a Mu'tazilite position that denies that the act of bringing into existence (al-ahdath) is an attribute of God and instead identifies it with the originated thing itself (al-muhdath). The refutation proceeds by asking a series of questions about the meaning of 'the world is originated' (al-‘alam muhdath). The opponent is given three options for the cause of the world’s origination: (1) an attribute of God, (2) something identical to the world itself, or (3) something distinct from God but not an attribute. Each option is then examined. If they choose (1), they abandon their own school (since Mu'tazila deny eternal attributes). If they choose (3), the questioner asks whether that cause is eternal or originated; if eternal, the world would be co-eternal; if originated, they are asked whether it originated by another cause, leading either to an infinite regress or to the absurdity of an originated thing without a cause. If they choose (2), the refutation argues that if the originated thing itself is its own cause, then in non-existence it would have been a cause, implying pre-eternal origination, which makes the world eternal—contradicting their claim. Finally, the Mu'tazilites are shown an alternative definition of 'originated' as 'coming to be in a state it was not in before', but the refutation asks whether this meaning is identical to the essence or distinct, and whichever they choose, the position is refuted. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the refutation is complete in its logic.

Key Terms
الإحداث
المحدث
الصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite denial of an attribute of creation is a straightforward position without consequences.
Correction: The passage shows it leads to a trilemma, each option of which is shown to be problematic.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilite claim about al-ihdath (bringing into existence)?
A) It is an attribute of God.
B) It is a created thing.
C) It is identical to the originated thing (al-muhdath).
D) It is a separate entity from God.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite position forces a choice between three interpretations of 'originated', each of which will be refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God created the world,' do you unconsciously separate the act from God Himself? How does the Mu'tazilite identity claim challenge you to think more deeply about the connection between God and His action? Will you now pause and examine your own mental picture of creation?
?
Have you ever considered that your default understanding of 'origination' might be shaped by a specific theological assumption? Which of the three options in the trilemma feels most intuitive to you, and why? Are you willing to test that intuition against the arguments that follow in this lesson?
?
The Mu'tazilites reduced God's act to the effect itself. Do you ever treat God's attributes as mere labels for His actions in your life, rather than real aspects of His being? What would change in your worship if you truly affirmed that His attributes are real and distinct from creation? Write down one concrete step you will take to honor God's attributes in your next prayer.

Refuting the First and Third Options
Imam al-Nasafi refutes the first and third options from the trilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the options of attributing origination to a divine attribute or to a separate external cause both fail, leading to either abandoning the Mu'tazilite school or infinite regress.

Core Idea
Claiming origination by an external cause leads to either pre-eternity or infinite regress.

In this lesson, we examine the refutation of the first and third options from the trilemma. If the Mu'tazilites say the world was originated by an attribute of God, they contradict their own denial of attributes. If they say it was by something other than God, that external cause must be either eternal or originated, leading to absurdities. Imam al-Nasafi's argument is sharp and concise.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of Option A
Read how the author dismisses the first option (attribute of God) with a single sentence: it abandons the Mu'tazilite school.
This shows the inconsistency of the opponent.
2
Analyze Option C
Now follow the chain of questions for the third option: is the external cause eternal or originated? If eternal, again against their school. If originated, how did it originate? This leads to either no cause (absurd) or infinite regress.
Note the terms: ahadath (made it happen), ihdath (bringing into existence), tasalsul (regress).
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
The key conclusion: if each cause requires another, the series goes on infinitely, which is impossible and suspends the origination of the world on an impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من زعم منهم أن الأحداث ليس بصفة للباري عز وجل بل عين المحدث، فيقال: ما معنى قولكم بأن العالم محدث، أكان محدثا بأحداث هو صفة الله تعالى قائمة به أو بإحداث هو عين المحدث أو بإحداث هو غير المحدث وليس بصفة الله تعالى قائمة به؟ فإن قالوا: حدث بإحداث هو صفة الله تعالى، قيل هذا ترك منكم مذهبكم وإن زعموا أنه حدث بإحداث هو غير الله تعالى، قيل أقديم هو أم محدث؟ فإن قالوا هو قديم فقد تركوا مذهبهم وإن قالوا هو محدث، قيل: إن حدث أيضا بإحداث آخر أم لا بإحداث؟ فإن قالوا: لا بإحداث فقد أحالوا وعطلوا الصانع حيث جوزوا حدوث حادث لا بإحداث، وإن قالوا حدث بإحداث آخر سئلوا عن الإحداث الثاني والثالث فيتسلسل إلى غير نهاية، وفيه تعليق حدوث العالم بما يستحيل ثبوته، وإن قالوا حدث بإحداث هو ذاته، فقد جعلوا ذات العالم في العدم إحداثا وهو في الأزل كان ذاتا وكان جوهرا وأعراضا وأجساما فكانت محدثة في الأزل، والموجود في الأزل لن يكون محدثا بل يكون قديما، فإذا كان العالم قديما موجودا في الأزل، فاعترضوا على هذا وزعموا أن معنى قولنا: إن العالم حدث أي حصل على حالة لم يكن عليها قبل هذه الحالة، معنى راجع إلى ذات العالم أم هي معنى وراء ذات العالم فبأي الجواب أجابو فقد أبطلنا، وتبين أنه حصل بهذا
Then among them is one who claims that bringing into existence [al-ahdath] is not an attribute of the Exalted and Glorious Creator, but rather it is the very thing brought into existence [al-muhdath]. So he asks: What is the meaning of your statement that the world is originated? Was it originated by a bringing into existence that is an attribute of God, may He be exalted, subsisting in Him, or by a bringing into existence that is identical to the originated thing, or by a bringing into existence that is other than the originated thing and not an attribute of God, may He be exalted, subsisting in Him? If they say: It occurred by a bringing into existence that is an attribute of God, it is said: This is an abandonment of your school. And if they claim that it occurred by a bringing into existence that is other than God, it is said: Is it eternal or originated? If they say it is eternal, they have abandoned their school. And if they say it is originated, it is said: Did it occur by another bringing into existence or not by a bringing into existence? If they say: Not by a bringing into existence, then they have committed an absurdity and rendered the Creator idle, by allowing the occurrence of an originated thing without a bringing into existence. And if they say it occurred by another bringing into existence, they are asked about the second and third bringings into existence, leading to an infinite regress, and in that is the suspension of the occurrence of the world upon what is impossible to establish. And if they say it occurred by a bringing into existence that is identical to its essence, then they have made the essence of the world in non-existence a bringing into existence, and it was in pre-eternity an essence and a substance and accidents and bodies, so they were originated in pre-eternity, and what exists in pre-eternity cannot be originated but rather is eternal. So if the world is eternal, existing in pre-eternity, then they objected to this and claimed that the meaning of our statement that the world is originated means it came to be in a state it was not in before that state—does this meaning refer to the essence of the world or is it a meaning beyond the essence of the world? Whichever answer they give, we have refuted it, and it is made clear that by this they have obtained...
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical refutation of a Mu'tazilite position that denies that the act of bringing into existence (al-ahdath) is an attribute of God and instead identifies it with the originated thing itself (al-muhdath). The refutation proceeds by asking a series of questions about the meaning of 'the world is originated' (al-‘alam muhdath). The opponent is given three options for the cause of the world’s origination: (1) an attribute of God, (2) something identical to the world itself, or (3) something distinct from God but not an attribute. Each option is then examined. If they choose (1), they abandon their own school (since Mu'tazila deny eternal attributes). If they choose (3), the questioner asks whether that cause is eternal or originated; if eternal, the world would be co-eternal; if originated, they are asked whether it originated by another cause, leading either to an infinite regress or to the absurdity of an originated thing without a cause. If they choose (2), the refutation argues that if the originated thing itself is its own cause, then in non-existence it would have been a cause, implying pre-eternal origination, which makes the world eternal—contradicting their claim. Finally, the Mu'tazilites are shown an alternative definition of 'originated' as 'coming to be in a state it was not in before', but the refutation asks whether this meaning is identical to the essence or distinct, and whichever they choose, the position is refuted. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the refutation is complete in its logic.

Key Terms
الإحداث
الصفة
القديم
المحدث
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the external cause could be a self-subsisting entity that does not require further causation.
Correction: The argument shows that if it is originated, it must have a cause, leading to regress; if it is eternal, it contradicts the oneness of God.

Checkpoint
What happens if the Mu'tazilites say the external cause of the world's origination is itself originated and came about by another cause?
A) That leads to an infinite regress.
B) That leads to an eternal cause.
C) That leads to the world being eternal.
D) That leads to the cause being an attribute of God.

Key Takeaway
Both the first and third options lead to contradictions or infinite regress, showing the Mu'tazilite position is untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you trace the causes of your existence back, do you stop at Allah as the First Cause, or is there something in your life you still rely on as an independent source? Be honest: do you ever feel you can manage without Him?
?
The argument shows that without an eternal Creator, everything collapses into absurdity. Where in your daily thoughts do you try to keep God at a distance, as if the world could run on its own? What would it cost you to bring Him into every detail right now?
?
The Mu'tazilites could not accept attributes because they feared compromising God's oneness. Ask yourself: Are there aspects of Allah's perfection you avoid contemplating because they make you uncomfortable? Do you reduce God to fit your reason instead of letting His revelation shape your mind?

6 min
Refuting the Option: The World as Its Own Cause
Imam al-Nasafi refutes the second option: that the cause of the world's origination is identical to the world itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why saying the world was originated by itself leads to the conclusion that the world is eternal, contradicting the Mu'tazilite claim.

Core Idea
Identifying the cause of the world with the world itself implies the world was always originated, hence eternal.

The second option from the trilemma is that the world was originated by something identical to itself. Imam al-Nasafi presents a powerful refutation: if the world's own essence was the cause of its origination, then in non-existence it was already an 'ihdath' (bringing into existence), meaning it was always originated, which implies it is eternal. This refutation targets the core of the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of Option B
Read the passage carefully. The key is that if the world's essence (dhat) is itself the cause, then even before existence, that essence was an 'ihdath', meaning the world was 'originated' in pre-eternity.
The terms: dhat (essence), 'adam (non-existence), azal (pre-eternity).
2
Understand the consequence
If the world was originated in pre-eternity, it cannot be originated (since what exists in pre-eternity is eternal). This is a contradiction.
This is a classic argument against self-causation.
3
Connect to the larger argument
This refutation shows that the world cannot be its own cause, reinforcing the need for a distinct divine attribute of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If the world caused itself, it would have been originated eternally, making it eternal—contradicting its own temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage argues that claiming self-causation leads to logical contradiction. In your spiritual life, are there beliefs or actions that contradict your profession of tawhid? Ask yourself: where am I attributing causality to myself or creation rather than Allah? Identify one such area and commit to a concrete step to realign your heart.
?
If the world's existence depends entirely on an external cause (Allah), how should that reshape your daily routine? Name one action this week that will physically manifest your reliance on Allah—such as a specific du'a, a moment of reflection, or an act of worship you will perform with full presence.

Refuting the Alternative Definition of Origination
Imam al-Nasafi refutes a final redefinition of 'originated' as a mere change of state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi refutes a redefinition of 'originated' as merely 'coming to be in a new state', whether that meaning is considered identical to the essence or distinct.

Core Idea
Redefining 'originated' as a change of state does not escape the problems; it still leads to contradictions.

After refuting the three options, Imam al-Nasafi anticipates a potential objection: the Mu'tazilites might reinterpret 'the world is originated' to mean 'it came to be in a state it was not in before'. This final move asks whether that meaning (al-ma'na) is identical to the world's essence or distinct. Whichever answer is given, the position is refuted. This lesson closes the argument decisively.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite redefinition
Read the passage where the opponent redefines 'originated' as a change of state (hassala 'ala halatin lam yakun 'alayha qabla hadhihi al-hala).
This is a common move: redefine the term to avoid earlier problems.
2
Understand the dilemma
The author then asks: is this 'meaning' (that change) identical to the essence of the world or additional to it?
The opponent must choose one.
3
Why both answers fail
If identical, then the essence itself changes, leading to same problems as before. If distinct, then origination is an additional attribute, which they deny. Whichever choice, they are refuted.
The conclusion: 'bi-ayyi al-jawab ajabu faqad abtalna' (whichever answer they give, we have invalidated it).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
What dilemma does Imam al-Nasafi present after the Mu'tazilites redefine 'originated' as a change of state?
A) Is the change of state eternal or originated?
B) Is the change of state identical to the essence or something beyond?
C) Is the change of state caused or uncaused?
D) Is the change of state an attribute of God or not?

Key Takeaway
Redefining 'originated' as a change of state fails because it either identifies with essence (leading to pre-eternal change) or adds an attribute (which Mu'tazilites deny).

?
When you encounter a difficult truth in Islam, do you ever try to 'redefine' it to make it more comfortable? How does this lesson warn you against that temptation, and what will you do instead?
?
If you were faced with a clear contradiction between your own reasoning and the teachings of the Quran and Sunnah, would you force your reasoning to fit or submit to revelation? What step can you take today to strengthen your submission?
?
This lesson exposes a flaw in human reasoning when it tries to escape a necessary conclusion. Where in your own life are you avoiding a necessary conclusion about your relationship with Allah? What would it cost you to face it head-on?

beginner
4 min
Refutation of al-Jubba'i on God's Will Without an Agent
Nasafi refutes al-Jubba'i's view and praises Allah for protection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God's will is originated without an agent is inconsistent with proving a Creator, as argued by Imam Nasafi.

Core Idea
God's will cannot be an effect without a cause—it must be attributed to God as its agent.

Imam Nasafi concludes his refutation of deviant views on God's will by addressing a final claim from al-Jubba'i and his son. They held that God's will is an originated accident not subsisting in a locus and without any originating agent. Nasafi shows that this self-contradictory position actually undermines the very proof of a Creator, and he ends with praise for divine protection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the two sentences of Arabic aloud or silently. Then read the English translation. Notice how Nasafi first states the claim, then refutes it, then concludes with praise.
The passage is concise but dense.
2
Identify al-Jubba'i's claim
What exactly do al-Jubba'i and his son assert about God's will? They say it is originated (hadith), not in a locus (la fi mahall), and without an originator (la bi ihdath ahad).
This is the view being refuted.
3
Understand Nasafi's critique
Nasafi says that this view incapacitates its proponents from proving the existence of a Creator (al-sani'). Why? Because if something can exist without an agent, then the argument from contingency collapses.
The critique is not elaborated here but is part of the broader context.
4
Reflect on the concluding praise
Nasafi thanks Allah for protecting the People of Truth from such errors. Reflect on how theological errors can lead to undermining the foundations of religion.
This is a doxological conclusion.

Source Text

وكذا الجبائي وابنه (۱) يزعمان أن إرادة الله تعالى حادثة لا في محل لا بإحداث أحد وإيجاده. فهؤلاء كلهم عجزوا أنفسهم عن إثبات الصانع على أصولهم الفاسدة. والله المحمود على ما عصمنا معاشر أهل الحق عن الوقوع فيما يهدم قواعد الدين والتمسك بما يفضي بنا إلى قياده إلى مذاهب الملحدين(۲)(۳).
And likewise al-Jubba'i and his son (1) claim that the will of Allah is originated, not in a locus, without anyone originating it or bringing it into existence. So all of these have incapacitated themselves from proving the Creator upon their corrupt principles. And Allah is praised for having protected us, the community of the People of Truth, from falling into what destroys the foundations of religion and from adhering to what leads us to the paths of the atheists (2)(3).
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Jubba'i and his son (Abu Hashim), who held that God's will (irada) is a hadith (originated) that exists not in a locus (i.e., not in a substrate) and has no originating agent. Nasafi includes them in the same critique: their view also undermines the proof for a Creator. He then concludes by praising Allah for protecting the Ahl al-Haqq (People of Truth, i.e., the Sunnis) from such errors that destroy the foundations of religion and lead to atheism. The passage serves as a summary rebuke of all the previously mentioned deviant views and an expression of gratitude for orthodox guidance.

Misconception: Some might think that denying an agent for God's will implies that God's will is uncreated.
Correction: The issue is about the will being originated without an agent, not about its pre-eternality. Even if originated, it must have an agent, otherwise the argument for a Creator fails.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the view that God's will is an uncaused originated accident, showing it undermines the proof for a Creator, and thanks Allah for preserving orthodox belief.

Consider the fragility of faith: one wrong principle can unravel everything. How will you actively safeguard your creed from corrupting influences starting now?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought their rationalism was superior, yet it rendered them unable to prove the Creator. Have you ever trusted your own reasoning over revelation? In what area of your life are you tempted to rationalize away clear teachings? Commit to humbling your intellect before the Qur'an and Sunnah, even when it feels uncomfortable.

Refutation of Mu'tazilite Claims on Takwin and Irada
Author refutes Mu'tazilite claims about God's attributes of takwin and irada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position on takwin and irada and why the Maturidi author considers it self-defeating for proving the existence of the Creator.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilite views on takwin and irada undermine the very proof of the Creator.

This passage shows how a seemingly technical debate about divine attributes has serious consequences for establishing God's existence. The author argues that the Mu'tazilite claim that takwin (bringing into existence) and irada (will) are originated without an originator leads to an absurdity that would apply to all creation, thus incapacitating any proof for the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Notice the structure: first takwin, then irada, then the conclusion.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claims
Find the two claims: (1) takwin is originated without originator, (2) God's will (irada) is originated without originator. Note that the Mu'tazilites agree on takwin but differ on its locus.
Al-Jubba'i and his son are mentioned specifically for irada.
3
Understand the author's objection
The author argues that if takwin can occur without an originator, then any part of the world could also occur without an originator. This leads to the inability to prove the Creator, which the author says shows the fallacy of the Mu'tazilite position.
The praise at the end emphasizes the protection of Ahl al-Haqq from such errors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التكوين حادث حدث لا بإحداث أحد، فإنهم اتفقوا على هذا، وإن اختلفوا في محل التكوين على ما نبين ذلك في تلك المسالة، ولو جاز ذا في التكوين لجاز في جميع أجزاء العالم. وكذا الجبائي وابنه يزعمان أن إرادة الله تعالى حادثة لا في محل لا بإحداث أحد وإيجاده. فهؤلاء كلهم عجزوا أنفسهم عن إثبات الصانع على أصولهم الفاسدة. والله المحمود على ما عصمنا معاشر أهل الحق عن الوقوع فيما يهدم قواعد الدين والتمسك بما يفضي بنا إلى قياده إلى مذاهب الملحدين.
Al-takwin (bringing into existence) is an originated thing that occurs without any originator bringing it about, for they (the Mu'tazilites) agreed upon this, though they differed regarding the locus of takwin, as we will explain in that issue. And if this were permissible for takwin, it would be permissible for all parts of the world. Likewise, al-Jubba'i and his son claim that the will (irada) of God Most High is originated, not in a locus, without anyone originating it or bringing it into existence. All of these people have incapacitated themselves from proving the Creator (al-sani') on the basis of their corrupt principles. And praise be to God for protecting us, the community of the people of truth, from falling into what destroys the foundations of religion and from adhering to what leads us to the ways of the atheists.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position regarding two of God's attributes: takwin (bringing into existence) and irada (will). The author begins by stating that the Mu'tazilites agree that takwin is an originated thing that occurs without any originator ('adam ihdath ahad). He notes that they differ on the locus of takwin, which he says will be explained later. The author then argues that if this were allowed for takwin, it would be allowed for all parts of the world, implying an absurdity. Next, he mentions that al-Jubba'i and his son claim that God's will (irada) is also originated, not in a locus, and without anyone originating it. The author concludes that all these thinkers have incapacitated themselves from proving the existence of the Creator (al-sani') due to their corrupt principles. He ends with a praise to God for protecting the Ahl al-Haqq (people of truth) from falling into such destructive views. The argment is that denying that God's attributes are eternal and self-subsisting leads to an inability to establish the existence of God as the Creator.

Misconception: This discussion is purely speculative and unrelated to proving the Creator's existence.
Correction: The author treats it as a foundational issue: if divine attributes like takwin and irada can be originated without an originator, then the world could also exist without a creator, making it impossible to prove God's existence.

Conflicting Divine Wills: A Logical Proof for One God
Nasafi proves necessity of one god by conflicting volitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical necessity of one God based on the impossibility of conflicting wills from two gods regarding the same body.

Core Idea
Two gods with opposite wills cannot coexist without contradiction or incapacity.

Nasafi presents a tight logical argument that forces a conclusion: if two gods exist and will opposite things for the same body, the only possible outcomes all lead to impossibility or to one god being incapable. This proof shows that the very concept of multiple gods is rationally untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the logical flow emerge before dissecting it.
Notice the compact style: the argument covers all possibilities in a few lines.
2
Identify the initial scenario and the first branch
The proof begins with two gods possessing all divine attributes. One wills a body to move. The first question: can the second god will the opposite (rest) or not?
If the second god cannot will the opposite, he is incapable, which disproves his divinity.
3
Consider the three sub-possibilities if the second god can will the opposite
If the second god can will the opposite, there are three outcomes: both wills executed, neither executed, or only one executed. Each must be examined.
These three exhaust all logical possibilities.
4
Observe the logical impossibility in each sub-case
Both executed: opposites (motion and rest) would coexist in the same body, impossible. Neither executed: the body would be devoid of both motion and rest, also impossible. Only one executed: the other god's will fails, so he is incapable.
In every possible scenario, either an impossibility occurs or at least one god is incapable.
5
Conclude the necessity of one God
Since all alternatives lead to contradiction or incapacity, the only consistent conclusion is that there is only one God capable of executing His will.
This proof reinforces the Islamic doctrine of tawhid (oneness of God).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خامسا: لو وجد إلهان متصفان بكل صفات الإلوهية وتعلقت إرادة أحدهما بتحريك جسم فإن لم يتمكن الآخر من إرادة ضده يكون عاجزا أو إن تمكن فإن نفذ مرادهما معا لزم اجتماع الضدين وهو محال وإن لم ينفذ مرادهما لزم خلو الجسم عن الحركة والسكون وهو محال ولزم عجزهما، وإن نفذ مراد أحدهما دون الآخر ثبتت الوحدانية وكان الآخر عاجزا.
Fifth: If there were two gods described with all attributes of divinity, and the will of one of them was related to moving a body, then if the other is not able to will its opposite, he is incapable. Or if he is able, then if both of their wills are executed together, the conjunction of two opposites would be necessary, which is impossible. And if neither of their wills is executed, the body being devoid of motion and rest would be necessary, which is impossible, and their incapacity would be necessary. And if the will of one is executed without the other, then oneness is established and the other is incapable.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This is the fifth proof, based on conflicting divine wills. Suppose two gods each have all divine attributes, including will. If one wills a body to move, the other can either be able to will its opposite (rest) or not. If not, he is incapable. If yes, then either both wills are actualized (impossible because opposites cannot coexist in the same locus), or neither is actualized (body would be devoid of both motion and rest, impossible), or only one is actualized (the other is incapable). In every scenario, at least one god is incapable or an impossibility occurs, so two gods cannot coexist.

Key Terms
إرادة
ضد
حركة
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could have complementary wills and thus avoid conflict.
Correction: The argument considers all possibilities, including complementary wills: if one wills motion and the other wills rest, they are opposite; if they both will the same, then the second god's will is redundant and he lacks independent power. But the proof assumes they can will opposites, covering the case of conflict.

Checkpoint
In Nasafi's proof, what happens if the second god is able to will the opposite of the first god's will, but only the first god's will is executed?
A) Both gods are proven capable.
B) The second god is proven incapable.
C) The body becomes devoid of motion and rest.
D) The two opposites coexist in the body.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of two conflicting divine wills leads to logical impossibility or incapacity in every scenario, proving that only one God can exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that two independent wills cannot govern the same reality without contradiction. Are there areas in your life where you act as if there are multiple ultimate authorities—your own desires, society, or others—that compete with Allah’s will? What would it mean to truly submit to His sole authority in one specific decision you are facing today?
?
The argument forces you to choose: either Allah’s will is effective and others’ are not, or He is not truly God. Do your actions and trust reflect this absolute reliance on Allah alone, or do you sometimes panic because you fear other forces can overrule His decree? What step can you take this week to deepen your trust in His exclusive control?

Refuting the Objection: Single Creator's Power Ceasing Is Not Disabling
Nasafi responds to an objection by distinguishing types of disabling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single creator's power ceasing due to creating an opposite is not a sign of incapacity, and how Nasafi distinguishes this from the case of two creators.

Core Idea
A single creator's power ceases by his own choice—that is mastery, not weakness.

Nasafi concludes his proof by addressing a subtle objection. The opponent tries to draw an analogy between a single creator's power ceasing after creating an opposite and the hypothetical case of two creators. Nasafi carefully distinguishes them, showing that intention and the ability to substitute make all the difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage once through. Notice the opponent's argument: if a single creator's power ceases after creating an opposite, that is not disabling, so why would it be disabling for two creators?
2
Identify Nasafi's response
Nasafi distinguishes two ways one can be disabled: directly (abolishing power) and indirectly (preventing action by creating an opposite in the same locus).
3
Understand the substitution concept
The single creator, when creating motion, still has the power to create rest alternately (badal). His power ceases only because opposites cannot coexist, not because another agent overpowers him.
4
Contrast with two creators
In the two-creator scenario, one creator's act prevents the other from acting entirely, which is genuine disabling and subjugation. That leads to impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول بثبوت صانعين يؤدي إلى هذا، وما أفضى إلى المحال فهو محال، ولا يقال: إن الواحد إذا أثبت فيه حركة زالت قدرته عن تخليق السكون فيه، ولم يكن ذلك تعجيزا لنفسه ولم يكن ذلك محالا ، فكذا في الاثنين لأنا نقول: إن من زالت قدرته عن إثبات شيء في محل إثبات غيره ضد ذلك الشيء كان عاجزا بتعجيز غيره إياه عن إثبات ما كانت قدرته ثابتة عليه بإثبات عليه ذلك الغير ضده لأن هذا منه تصرف في المحل ليمنع صاحبه عن التصرف فيه وتعجيز غيره تارة يكون بمنعه عن تنفيذ قدرته بتصرفه في ذاته بإبطال قدرته وتارة يكون بفعل في محل تصرف بإثبات ضد ما يريد صاحبه إثباته فيه وإخراجه من أن يكون قابلا لتصرفه بإثبات ضد تصرفه فيه، فأما من أثبت تصرفا في محل مع قدرته على إثبات ضده فيه على طريق البدل لن يعد عاجزا عن التصرف فيه وإن زالت قدرته لاستحالة الجمع بين الضدين إذا كان هو الذي أثبت هذا الضد فيه باختياره مع قدرته على إثبات ضده على البدل بل هو آية كمال القدرة وعلاقة نفاذ المشيئة، فأما في الاثنين فذلك علامة العجز والقهر على ما قدرنا، والله الموفق.
And the statement affirming two creators leads to this, and what leads to impossibility is impossible. And it cannot be said: When the one (i.e., a single creator) establishes motion in a thing, his power ceases from creating rest in it, and that is not a disabling of himself nor is it impossible. So likewise in the case of two. For we say: Whoever's power ceases from establishing a thing in a locus due to another's establishing its opposite in that locus is made incapable by the other's disabling him from establishing what his power was established upon, by the other establishing its opposite. Because this (the other's action) is a disposition in the locus to prevent the first from disposing in it. And disabling another is sometimes by preventing him from executing his power through disposing in his essence by abolishing his power, and sometimes by an act in the locus of disposition by establishing the opposite of what the other wants to establish, and removing it from being receptive to his disposition by establishing the opposite of his disposition. As for one who establishes a disposition in a locus while having the power to establish its opposite in it by way of substitution, he is not considered incapable of disposition in it even though his power ceases due to the impossibility of combining opposites, since he is the one who established this opposite in it by his choice while having the power to establish its opposite by substitution. Rather, it is a sign of the perfection of power and the connection of the will's effectiveness. As for in the case of two, that is a sign of incapacity and subjugation according to what we have detailed. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer, concluding the proofs. The opponent argues that a single creator's power to create rest ceases after he creates motion, but this is not disabling; so why would it be disabling for two? The author responds by distinguishing two types of disabling: (1) direct abolition of power, and (2) indirect prevention by creating an opposite. In the single creator case, he himself chooses to create motion while having the power to create rest alternatively; his power ceasing is due to the impossibility of opposites, not because of another's action. Thus, it is a sign of power, not weakness. In the two-creator case, one creator's act prevents the other from acting, which is genuine disabling and subjugation. Therefore, the objection fails.

Key Terms
تعجيز
ضد
تصرف
قدرة
بدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any cessation of divine power indicates weakness, regardless of context.
Correction: The passage shows that when the cessation is due to the creator's own choice and with the ability to substitute, it actually demonstrates the perfection of power.

Checkpoint
Why is a single creator's power ceasing after creating an opposite not considered disabling?
A) Because his power does not actually cease.
B) Because he himself chose to create that opposite while having the ability to create the alternative.
C) Because it is impossible for power to cease.
D) Because the opposite is not really created.

Key Takeaway
The cessation of a single creator's power due to his own choice and with the ability to substitute demonstrates the perfection of his power, not weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where your plans are thwarted, do you ever feel that God's power is somehow limited? How does this lesson's distinction between self-chosen cessation and external disabling reshape your understanding of God's absolute control?
?

?
If you truly believed that God's ceasing to create an opposite is a sign of perfect power and the efficacy of His will, how would that change your response when your prayers seem unanswered or your desires are not fulfilled?
