Opponents' Proofs that God is Jawhar
Opponents present two arguments for God being a substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments used by opponents to claim that God is a substance (jawhar) based on the classification of existents and the nature of action.

Core Idea
Opponents argue God must be a substance because all existents are either substance or accident, or because He acts.

Some theological groups argued that God must be a substance (jawhar) in order to exist or to act. This passage presents two of their arguments, which the author will later refute. Let's examine their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow. Then read the translation to understand the arguments.
Notice that the opponents present two distinct arguments in sequence.
2
Identify the first argument
The first argument uses the dichotomy of substance and accident: all existents are either substance or accident; God is not an accident, so He must be a substance.
The argument concludes that if God were neither, He would not exist.
3
Identify the second argument
The second argument uses the concept of action: everything that performs action in the witnessed world is a substance; God performs actions, so He is a substance.
This argument relies on analogical reasoning from observable things.
4
Reflect on the logic
Consider the assumptions each argument makes about the nature of existence and action. Are they valid for God?
The next lesson will show how the author refutes these assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يتعلقون به أن الله تعالى موجود والموجود إما أن يكون جوهرا وإما أن يكون عرضا والله تعالى ليس بعرض فيكون جوهرا إذ لو لم يكن جوهرا مع أنه ليس بعرض لما كان موجودا لأن الموجود ينقسم إلى قسمين وما انقسم إلى قسمين فما خرج عن قسميه جميعا لا يكون من جملته فلو خرج الباري جل وعلا عن القسمين جميعا لما كان موجودا ويقولون أيضا إن الله تعالى فاعل وما يجوز منه الفعل في الشاهد فهو جوهر وما يستحيل منه الفعل فهو عرض وقد تحقق من الله تعالى الفعل فكان جوهرا.
وحجتنا أن الجوهر في اللغة عبارة عن الأصل يقال لمن اشتهر بالإحسان من أبناء الشرف والسيادة فلان يجري في الإحسان على شاكلة جوهره الشريف ومقتضى حسبه العالي المنيف ويقال للثوب إذا كان محكم الصنعة جيد الأصل إنه ثوب جوهري فعلى هذا سموا ما لا يتجزأ من أجزاء الجسم جوهرا لكون البسائط التي يتركب منها المتركبات جارية مجرى الأصول لها لا يعنون به أنه أصله القديم كما يذهب إليه أهل الدهر بل يريدون أن كل متركب يقدر في الوهم أنه من الأفراد تركب فيكون الأفراد أصولا وأركانا في المركبات ولتصوير الأفراد بدون التركب واستحالة المتركبات بدون الأفراد التي تركت هي منها والله سبحانه وتعالى يستحيل تركيبه إلى غيره ليصير أجساما فلم يكن أصلا يتركب منه الجسم فلم يكن جوهرا.
And among what they cling to is that God the Exalted is existent, and the existent is either a substance (jawhar) or an accident ('arad), and God the Exalted is not an accident, so He is a substance. For if He were not a substance despite not being an accident, He would not exist, because the existent is divided into two parts, and whatever is divided into two parts, what departs from both of its parts is not of its entirety. So if the Glorious and Exalted departed from both parts, He would not exist. And they also say: God the Exalted is an agent, and whatever allows action from it in the witnessed world is a substance, and whatever action is impossible from it is an accident. And action from God the Exalted has been verified, so He is a substance. And our proof: that 'jawhar' in language is an expression for the origin (al-asl). It is said of one who is renowned for beneficence among people of nobility and leadership: 'So-and-so flows in beneficence upon the pattern of his noble jawhar and the requirement of his high and exalted lineage.' And it is said of a garment, if it is well-woven and of good origin, 'It is a jawhari garment.' Accordingly, they called what is indivisible from the parts of a body a jawhar, because the simple elements from which compounds are composed are like origins for them. They do not mean by it that it is the ancient origin as the materialists hold, but rather they mean that every compound, it is imagined in the mind that it is composed of individuals, so the individuals are origins and pillars in the compounds, and because of the conceivability of individuals without composition and the impossibility of compounds without the individuals of which they are composed. And God the Exalted and Glorious, composition into other things to become bodies is impossible for Him, so He was not an origin from which the body is composed, so He is not a jawhar.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from the opponents (likely Christians and the Karramiyya) that God is a jawhar (substance), followed by the author's rebuttal based on the linguistic meaning of jawhar. The first argument: all existent things are either substances or accidents. God is not an accident, so He must be a substance; otherwise, if He were neither, He would not exist. The second argument: anything that performs action in the witnessed world is a substance; God performs actions, so He is a substance. The author then refutes by investigating the linguistic origin of the word 'jawhar'. He shows that in Arabic, jawhar means 'origin' (asl), used for noble lineage or high-quality fabric. From this, scholars applied the term to the indivisible parts of a body because those simple parts serve as the origins for composite bodies. They do not mean it as an eternal origin (like materialists claim), but that every composite is mentally composed from individuals, these individuals being the origins. Since God cannot be composed into bodies, He cannot be an origin for composition, and thus He is not a jawhar.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
عرض (accident, 'arad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God must fit into human categories of substance and accident.
Correction: The opponents assume that 'existent' is exhaustively divided into substance and accident, which the author will later challenge by examining the linguistic meaning of 'jawhar'.

Checkpoint
According to the opponents' first argument, why must God be a jawhar?
A) Because He performs actions.
B) Because He is not an accident and the existent is only substance or accident.
C) Because He is the origin of all things.
D) Because He is composed of parts.

Key Takeaway
Opponents use the dichotomy of substance/accident and the attribute of action to argue that God is a substance, but these arguments rely on assumptions that may not apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unknowingly limited God by forcing Him into your own categories of thought? What would it mean for you to let go of that mental framework in your worship?
?
The opponents' arguments seem logical until you realize they assume God is like created things. What assumptions about God do you hold that might actually be based on your own experiences rather than revelation?
?
If God truly transcends the categories of substance and accident, how should that change your reliance on human reasoning when approaching Him? Commit to one specific step you will take this week to submit your logic to His revelation.

Linguistic Rebuttal: Jawhar Means Origin
Author provides linguistic analysis of 'jawhar' to refute opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that God is a jawhar by analyzing the linguistic meaning of the term as 'origin' and showing it cannot apply to God.

Core Idea
Jawhar linguistically means 'origin' and applies only to composite things, not to God.

The author now turns to the Arabic language to show that 'jawhar' does not mean what the opponents think. He demonstrates its original meaning as 'origin' and how it came to be used for indivisible parts of bodies, then concludes that God, being beyond composition, cannot be called a jawhar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Pay attention to the examples given.
2
Understand the linguistic definition
The author states that 'jawhar' in Arabic means 'origin' (asl), citing examples from noble lineage and fine fabric.
This shows the term is used for something fundamental or high-quality.
3
See how it applies to bodies
Scholars applied 'jawhar' to indivisible parts of bodies because those parts are the 'origins' of composite bodies.
This is a technical usage, not the original meaning.
4
Apply the definition to God
Since God cannot be composed or serve as an origin for composition, He cannot be called a jawhar.
The author refutes the opponents by showing the term's proper meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يتعلقون به أن الله تعالى موجود والموجود إما أن يكون جوهرا وإما أن يكون عرضا والله تعالى ليس بعرض فيكون جوهرا إذ لو لم يكن جوهرا مع أنه ليس بعرض لما كان موجودا لأن الموجود ينقسم إلى قسمين وما انقسم إلى قسمين فما خرج عن قسميه جميعا لا يكون من جملته فلو خرج الباري جل وعلا عن القسمين جميعا لما كان موجودا ويقولون أيضا إن الله تعالى فاعل وما يجوز منه الفعل في الشاهد فهو جوهر وما يستحيل منه الفعل فهو عرض وقد تحقق من الله تعالى الفعل فكان جوهرا.
وحجتنا أن الجوهر في اللغة عبارة عن الأصل يقال لمن اشتهر بالإحسان من أبناء الشرف والسيادة فلان يجري في الإحسان على شاكلة جوهره الشريف ومقتضى حسبه العالي المنيف ويقال للثوب إذا كان محكم الصنعة جيد الأصل إنه ثوب جوهري فعلى هذا سموا ما لا يتجزأ من أجزاء الجسم جوهرا لكون البسائط التي يتركب منها المتركبات جارية مجرى الأصول لها لا يعنون به أنه أصله القديم كما يذهب إليه أهل الدهر بل يريدون أن كل متركب يقدر في الوهم أنه من الأفراد تركب فيكون الأفراد أصولا وأركانا في المركبات ولتصوير الأفراد بدون التركب واستحالة المتركبات بدون الأفراد التي تركت هي منها والله سبحانه وتعالى يستحيل تركيبه إلى غيره ليصير أجساما فلم يكن أصلا يتركب منه الجسم فلم يكن جوهرا.
And among what they cling to is that God the Exalted is existent, and the existent is either a substance (jawhar) or an accident ('arad), and God the Exalted is not an accident, so He is a substance. For if He were not a substance despite not being an accident, He would not exist, because the existent is divided into two parts, and whatever is divided into two parts, what departs from both of its parts is not of its entirety. So if the Glorious and Exalted departed from both parts, He would not exist. And they also say: God the Exalted is an agent, and whatever allows action from it in the witnessed world is a substance, and whatever action is impossible from it is an accident. And action from God the Exalted has been verified, so He is a substance. And our proof: that 'jawhar' in language is an expression for the origin (al-asl). It is said of one who is renowned for beneficence among people of nobility and leadership: 'So-and-so flows in beneficence upon the pattern of his noble jawhar and the requirement of his high and exalted lineage.' And it is said of a garment, if it is well-woven and of good origin, 'It is a jawhari garment.' Accordingly, they called what is indivisible from the parts of a body a jawhar, because the simple elements from which compounds are composed are like origins for them. They do not mean by it that it is the ancient origin as the materialists hold, but rather they mean that every compound, it is imagined in the mind that it is composed of individuals, so the individuals are origins and pillars in the compounds, and because of the conceivability of individuals without composition and the impossibility of compounds without the individuals of which they are composed. And God the Exalted and Glorious, composition into other things to become bodies is impossible for Him, so He was not an origin from which the body is composed, so He is not a jawhar.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from the opponents (likely Christians and the Karramiyya) that God is a jawhar (substance), followed by the author's rebuttal based on the linguistic meaning of jawhar. The first argument: all existent things are either substances or accidents. God is not an accident, so He must be a substance; otherwise, if He were neither, He would not exist. The second argument: anything that performs action in the witnessed world is a substance; God performs actions, so He is a substance. The author then refutes by investigating the linguistic origin of the word 'jawhar'. He shows that in Arabic, jawhar means 'origin' (asl), used for noble lineage or high-quality fabric. From this, scholars applied the term to the indivisible parts of a body because those simple parts serve as the origins for composite bodies. They do not mean it as an eternal origin (like materialists claim), but that every composite is mentally composed from individuals, these individuals being the origins. Since God cannot be composed into bodies, He cannot be an origin for composition, and thus He is not a jawhar.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
أصل (origin, asl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'jawhar' in kalam refers to God's essence or is simply a synonym for 'substance'.
Correction: The author shows that jawhar is a linguistic term meaning 'origin' and applies only to something that can be a compositional part; God is beyond composition, so the term does not apply.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does 'jawhar' mean in the Arabic language?
A) A substance that exists independently.
B) An indivisible part of a body.
C) The origin (asl) of something.
D) An accident that does not endure.

Key Takeaway
The term jawhar, by its linguistic origin, cannot be applied to God because He is not an origin for composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God, do you ever use terms that imply He is like created things, even unintentionally? How does this lesson's insistence on avoiding 'jawhar' because of its connection to composition challenge you to be more careful with your words about Him?
?
The author says that calling someone 'jawhari' means they have a noble origin. Do you seek to base your identity on something other than God—like your lineage, character, or achievements? How does the fact that only God is truly without origin (not jawhar) reorient your sense of worth?
?
If you were to apply the logic of this lesson to other attributes—like saying God is 'light' or 'spirit'—how would you test whether the linguistic and technical meanings actually apply to Him without reducing Him to creation? What concrete steps will you take to guard against such misapplications in your own understanding?

Refutation of Trinitarian Hypostases from Divine Attributes
Nasafi refutes Trinitarian hypostases via attribute analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi exposes the logical flaws in the Christian Trinitarian claim that God has three hypostases, using the Islamic concept of divine attributes.

Core Idea
If each attribute is a distinct meaning, the Trinity logically expands to eight hypostases, not three.

Nasafi opens a devastating critique of the Christian Trinity by taking their own logic seriously. If knowledge, power, will, life, hearing, and sight are each distinct 'meanings' (maʿānī) beyond the divine essence, then consistency demands not three hypostases but eight. This forces the Trinitarian into a series of absurd positions about whether attributes are identical to or distinct from the essence—each leading to self-contradiction or rational impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let Nasafi's argumentative structure emerge naturally.
Pay attention to how he moves from counting attributes to testing the opponent's positions.
2
Identify the key moves in the refutation
Notice that Nasafi first points out an inconsistency in the number of hypostases, then examines possible answers the opponent might give about the relationship between attributes and essence.
There are three possible positions he considers, each shown to be problematic.
3
Reflect on the Islamic doctrine of attributes
Consider how Nasafi's argument relies on the Islamic distinction between the divine essence (dhāt) and attributes (ṣifāt) while rejecting any notion of multiple eternal hypostases.
This is a classic Māturīdī approach: using rational consistency to defend tawḥīd.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the quiz question about the main logical flaw Nasafi identifies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنيان وراء العلم والقدرة والإرادة معنيان وراء الحياة، وقولهم: القدرة من جملة الحياة فاسد لوجود الحياة بلا قدرة، ولتزايد القدرة وامتناع ذلك على الحياة. وكذا قولهم: إن السمع والبصر من جملة العلم خطأ، لأنهما معنيان وراء العلم على ما نقرر ذلك في مسألة الرؤية – إن شاء الله تعالى. فعلى هذا ينبغي أن يقولوا إنه ثمانية أقانيم ولم يقتصروا على الثلاثة. ثم نقول لهم: أتزعمون أن العلم والحياة معنيان وراء الذات أم تزعمون أنهما يرجعان إلى الذات؟ فإن قالوا: هما راجعان إلى الذات فهو إذا جوهر واحد أقنوم واحد. وإن قالوا هما معنيان وراء الذات، قيل لهم : قولوا إنه جوهر واحد وله أقنومان ولا تقولوا ثلاثة أقانيم. ثم قيل لهم : إذا كان الجوهر الذي هو الذات من جملة الأقانيم الصفات فمن الموصوف به ؟ فإن قالوا شيئا وراء الذات، فقد جعلوا الذات صفة لموصوف فإذا الله تعالى هو الموصوف لا هذا الجوهر الذي هو صفة لغيره، وإن قالوا: هو الذات بعينه، فقد جعلوا الذات صفة لنفسه موصوفا له، فإذا هو صفة صفته وموصوف موصوفه. وإن قالوا: هو صفة ولا موصوف له، فقد أثبتوا صفة لا لموصوف، وهذا باطل خارج عن قضية العقول، وقد أطنب أئمة أهل الإسلام في إيضاح مقالاتهم، وإبطال ذلك كله على وجه يتيقن المنصف بالوقوف عليها على التحاق القوم بأهل التجاهل ولا وجه إلى ذكر ذلك في كتابنا المبني على إيجاز، فاكتفينا بهذا القدر، والله الموفق.
Two meanings beyond knowledge and power and will, two meanings beyond life. And their saying: 'Power is part of life' is corrupt because life exists without power, and because power increases and that is impossible for life. Likewise their saying: 'Hearing and sight are part of knowledge' is wrong, because they are two meanings beyond knowledge, as we will establish that in the question of vision—if Allah wills. So on this basis they should say that there are eight hypostases and not limit themselves to three. Then we say to them: Do you claim that knowledge and life are two meanings beyond the essence, or do you claim that they refer back to the essence? If they say: They refer back to the essence, then it is one substance, one hypostasis. And if they say: They are two meanings beyond the essence, then it is said to them: Say that it is one substance and it has two hypostases, and do not say three hypostases. Then it is said to them: If the substance which is the essence is among the hypostases of attributes, then who is described by it? If they say: Something beyond the essence, then they have made the essence a description of something described, so Allah Almighty is the described, not this substance which is a description of another. And if they say: It is the very essence, then they have made the essence a description of itself and described for it, so it would be the description of its description and the described of its described. And if they say: It is a description without any described, then they have affirmed a description with no described, and this is false, outside the judgment of intellects. The imams of Ahl al-Islam have elaborated in clarifying their statements and refuting all that in a way that the fair-minded person, upon coming across them, becomes certain that the people belong to the ignorant. There is no way to mention that in our book built on conciseness, so we suffice with this amount. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Christian Trinitarian claim that the divine essence has three hypostases (aqānīm). Nasafi begins by pointing out an internal inconsistency: if knowledge, power, and will are distinct meanings beyond the essence, and likewise life, hearing, and sight are distinct meanings, then logically there would be eight hypostases, not three. He then systematically examines possible positions the opponent might take regarding whether attributes are distinct from the essence or identical to it. If attributes refer back to the essence, then there is only one hypostasis. If they are distinct, then there would be multiple hypostases, and the question arises: who is the subject described by these attributes? If the subject is something beyond the essence, then the essence becomes an attribute of something else, which is absurd because Allah is the subject of all attributes. If the subject is the essence itself, then the essence becomes both attribute and subject, leading to self-referential confusion. If they claim there is no subject, that would be an attribute without a described subject, which is rationally impossible. Nasafi concludes that the detailed refutations have been given by earlier imams, and he suffices with this concise argument.

ذات
صفة
جوهر
علم
قدرة
حياة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Christian Trinity is logically consistent with Islamic monotheism.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the Trinity implies either an infinite regress of hypostases or absurdities in the attribution of attributes, proving it inconsistent with rational monotheism.

Checkpoint

B) If each attribute is a distinct meaning, then there should be eight hypostases, not three.
C) The Bible mentions seven hypostases.
D) They cannot prove the existence of the Holy Spirit.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that the Trinitarian hypostases are logically unsustainable because a consistent application of their own reasoning leads to an absurd multiplication of hypostases and self-contradictory descriptions of the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, Nasafi uses logic to expose internal contradictions in the Trinity. But are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that you have never subjected to such logical scrutiny? Will you commit today to examining one of your own assumptions about God's attributes using the Quran and the reasoning of the scholars?
?
The passage shows that attributing multiple distinct hypostases to God leads to absurdity. But in your heart, do you sometimes think of Allah's attributes as if they were separate 'parts'? How can you train your mind to worship Allah as One with perfect unity, avoiding any mental division of His essence?
?
Imagine you are asked by a Christian friend, 'How is your concept of God's attributes different from the Trinity?' Based on this lesson, would you be able to explain the logical incoherence of the Trinity? If not, what steps will you take to deepen your understanding so you can defend tawhid with clarity and confidence?

Literalists' Evidence for Anthropomorphism
Nasafi lists verses and rational arguments used by literalists to support anthropomorphic attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural and rational proofs that literalists (mushabbiha) use to argue for anthropomorphic attributes of Allah.

Core Idea
The literalists use apparent meanings (ẓawāhir) of texts and a rational analogy to argue that God is a body.

In refuting anthropomorphism, the author first presents the evidence of those who hold such views. This passage enumerates the Quranic verses, prophetic hadiths, and rational argument that the literalists rely on. By seeing their strongest proofs, we can appreciate why the refutation is necessary and how it addresses their core claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the scriptural citations
Read the Arabic text carefully and list the Quranic verses and hadiths that the literalists cite. Observe how they use apparent meanings (ẓawāhir) to support their view.
The verses include 'I created with My two hands' (Saad 75) and others.
2
Identify the rational argument
Find the rational analogy presented: since God is Living, Hearing, Seeing, and Acting, and all such beings are bodies, therefore God must be a body. Note that the passage is cut off mid-sentence.
This analogy is a form of qiyās that the author will refute.
3
Consider the need for ta'wil
Ask yourself why Maturidi scholars do not accept these proofs at face value. Reflect on the principle that Allah transcends human attributes.
The refutation (not present here) would involve metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil).

أما المخالفون لنا في الاسم والمعنى فهم يتعلقون بظواهر الآيات والأحاديث في نحو قوله عز وجل: {خَلَقْتُ بيدَى}، {وَلِتُصْنَعَ عَلَى عَيْنِي}، {وَالسَّمَوَاتُ مَطويت بِيَمِينِهِ}، {يَحْسَرَى عَلَى مَا فَرَّطْتُ فِي جَنبِ اللهِ}، {اللهُ نُورُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ}، والمعروف أنه خلق آدم على صورته، وأن الجبار يضع قدمه في النار، وأنه يضحك إلى أوليائه حتى تبدوا نواجذه، وأن الصدقة تقع في كف الرحمن فيربيها أحدكم فلو. في أمثال لهذا كثيرة، ويتعلقون أيضا بما ترأى لهم أنه دلیل معقول من ذلك أنه حي سميع بصير فاعل، وكل حي سميع بصير فاعل في

Commentary
This passage presents the evidence used by literalists (mushabbiha) who affirm anthropomorphic attributes in a literal sense. They cite Quranic verses that mention God's hands, eye, right hand, side, and light, as well as hadiths about God's image, foot, laughter, and hand receiving charity. They also use a rational analogy: since God is living, hearing, seeing, and acting, and all such beings are bodies, then God must be a body. The author is listing their proofs before refuting them (the refutation presumably follows in subsequent pages).

Answering the Objection: Why God's Attributes Do Not Need a Specifier
Imam Nasafi answers the objection that divine attributes need a specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's attributes of perfection are necessary of existence and do not require an external specifier, unlike contingent shapes.

Core Idea
God's attributes are necessary, not contingent—they need no specifier.

Imam Nasafi addresses a sharp objection: If the world's contingent shapes require a specifier, why don't God's attributes? In this passage, he clarifies that divine attributes are necessary of existence, while their opposites are impossible. This distinction nullifies the objection and reaffirms divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the full argument. Notice how the objector tries to apply the same logic to God.
The objection is implicitly stated at the beginning: 'It should not be said...'
2
Identify the key distinction
Identify how Imam Nasafi distinguishes between necessary (wājib) and possible (jā'iz) existence. This is the core of the answer.
He also mentions impossible (mumtani') for the opposites of attributes.
3
Understand the implications
Reflect on how this distinction defends God's attributes from requiring an external cause. The witnessed world of originated things illustrates the point.
Connect to the broader theme of tawhid and divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال إن عندكم اختص بكونه حيا عالما قادرا سميعا بصيرا، ولا يقال إن اختصاصه كان بتخصيص مخصص وكذا هذا، لأنا نقول : اختصاصه بأحد الجائزين لن يكون إلا بتخصيص مخصص، وهذه الصفات واجبة الوجود لا جائزة الوجود، وأضدادها ممتنعة الوجود لما أنها نقائص، ويستحيل ثبوت النقص على القديم، ونحن ندعي استحالة اختصاص أحد الجائزين، أو أحد الوجود الجائزة المحتملة مع مساواة غيره إياه في الجواز، وانعدام دليل المزية، وما ألزموه ليس من هذا القبيل، فأما ما أثبتوه فهو من هذا القبيل، لأنه ليس في شكل من هذه الأشكال كما لا يساويه غيره فيه، ولا نقيضه ولا يساويه فيها غيره من الأشكال، فثبت الاستواء في ذلك، وفيما ألزموا الأمر بخلافه حتى إن من المشاهد لما كانت النقائص جائزة على كل محدث لم تثبت صفة من هذه الصفات التي صفات الكمال إلا بتخصيص مخصص فكذا هذا، والذي يحقق استواء هذه الأشكال في الجواز أن من عين واحدا منها لم ينفصل ممن عين آخر، ولا دليل على صحة ما ادعاه وإبطال ما ادعى خصمه، يحققه أنه ليس في بعضها من المدح أو من النقيصة ما ليس فيها وراءه، وفي صفات الكمال الأمر بخلافه وبالله التوفيق.
And it should not be said that according to you He is specified as living, knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing, and it should not be said that His specification was by the specification of a specifier, and likewise this. For we say: His specification to one of the two possible things is not except by the specification of a specifier. But these attributes are necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd), not possible of existence (jā'iz al-wujūd), and their opposites are impossible of existence (mumtani' al-wujūd) because they are deficiencies. And it is impossible for deficiency to be established for the Eternal. And we claim the impossibility of the specification of one of the two possible things, or one of the possible contingent existences, with the equality of another to it in possibility and the absence of evidence of excellence. And what they have objected is not of this kind. As for what they have affirmed, it is of this kind. Because there is not in one shape of these shapes what is not in another that equals it, nor its opposite, nor what equals it in it from other shapes. So equality in that is established. And in what they objected, the matter is contrary to that. Even in the witnessed, since deficiencies are possible for every originated thing, no attribute from among these attributes which are attributes of perfection is established except by the specification of a specifier; so likewise this. And what establishes the equality of these shapes in possibility is that whoever specifies one of them is not distinguished from whoever specifies another, and there is no evidence for the correctness of what he claimed and the invalidity of what his opponent claimed. This is verified by the fact that there is not in some of them of praise or deficiency what is not in others beyond it. And in the attributes of perfection, the matter is contrary to that. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. The objector says: According to your own reasoning, God is specified as being living, knowing, powerful, etc., and those specifications must also require a specifier, so your argument against the world being a body would apply to God's attributes too. The author responds by distinguishing between necessary (wājib) and contingent (jā'iz) existence. God's attributes of perfection (life, knowledge, power) are necessary; their opposites are impossible because they are deficiencies. Since God is eternal, deficiency cannot attach to Him. So these attributes are not contingent possibilities that require an external specifier; they are necessary concomitants of the divine essence. In contrast, shapes of bodies are purely contingent—there is no inherent excellence of one over another, so their specification requires an external cause. In the witnessed world, originated things that lack perfection require a specifier to receive their attributes; but for God, perfection is essential, not contingent. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
جائز الوجود
ممتنع الوجود
نقائص
صفات الكمال
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's attributes of perfection are contingent and require an external specifier, just like the shapes of bodies.
Correction: God's attributes are necessary of existence, not contingent; their opposites are impossible because they are deficiencies. Thus they do not require an external specifier.

Checkpoint
Why do God's attributes (life, knowledge, power) not require an external specifier according to Imam Nasafi?
A) Because they are impossible of existence
B) Because they are necessary of existence and their opposites are impossible
C) Because they are contingent but chosen by God Himself
D) Because they are acquired from another source

Key Takeaway
God's attributes of perfection are necessary and thus do not require an external specifier, defending God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'Living, Knowing, Powerful', do you truly believe these are essential to Him and not accidental? How does that transform your trust in Him today?
?
Consider a moment you doubted God's wisdom or power. Did you accidentally treat His attributes as if they could be otherwise? How can this lesson anchor you when doubts arise?
?
If God's perfection is necessary and cannot be less, then every deficiency in you is a reminder of your contingency. Will you let that drive you to complete dependence on Him, or will you resist?

Refutation of the Claim that Non-Bodies Cannot Have Attributes
Nasafi refutes the claim that only bodies can have attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the anthropomorphist claim that only bodies can possess attributes like life and knowledge.

Core Idea
Attributes like life can inhere in non-bodies, as seen in indivisible parts.

The anthropomorphists argued that since Allah is living, knowing, and powerful, He must be a body because only bodies can have these attributes. Nasafi dismantles this by pointing to the witnessed world: a living body is composed of indivisible parts, and life resides in each part individually. Those parts are not bodies, yet they have life. Thus, the claim is refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read Nasafi's refutation in Arabic and English, noting the structure: the doubt, the response, and the handling of an objection.
The Arabic text ends abruptly; the argument continues on the next page.
2
Analyze the core argument from the witnessed world
Nasafi argues that in the witnessed world, a living body is composed of indivisible parts (juz' la yatajuzza') called substances, and life resides in each part. Since these parts are not bodies, life can exist in non-bodies.
This directly refutes the claim that only bodies can have attributes.
3
Examine the response to Hisham ibn al-Hakam's objection
Nasafi addresses the objection that indivisible parts do not exist, giving two responses: (1) the argument still holds for anthropomorphists who accept indivisible parts, and (2) he has proven the falsehood of infinite divisibility.
Notice how Nasafi covers multiple positions to ensure a thorough refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على شبه الخصوم أنه تعالى حي قادر عالم ويستحيل اتصاف ما ليس بجسم بهذه الصفات، فهي مجرد دعوى، وتعلقه بالشاهد فاسد فإن في الشاهد نجد ما هو موصوف بهذه الصفات وليس بجسم، فإن الجسم إذا كان حيا وهو متركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة وهي المسماة جواهر ما قام بكل جزء منه حياة على حدة، فيكون حيا وإن لم يكن جسما، فإن منعوا وجود ما لا يتجزأ من الأجزاء على أصل هشام ابن الحكم، إذ الجزء عنده يتجزأ لا إلى نهاية له وهو أحد المخالفين لنا في هذه المسألة، فنقول: هذا الفصل لازم على غيره من المجسمة. ثم إنا أقمنا الدلالة على بطلان القول بتجزؤ الجزء لا إلى نهاية، فيصير أيضا محجوجا بعدم قيام الدليل على بطلان تلك المسألة. ثم نقول لهم: ماذا
Refutation of the doubts of the opponents. [They say:] He, exalted, is living, powerful, knowing, and it is impossible for what is not a body to be described with these attributes. This is a mere claim, and its attachment to the witnessed world is corrupt, because in the witnessed world we find what is described with these attributes and is not a body. For if a body is living and it is composed of indivisible parts, which are called substances (jawahir), and life resides in each part individually, then it is living even if it is not a body. And if they deny the existence of indivisible parts according to the principle of Hisham ibn al-Hakam – for the part according to him is divisible without end and he is one of those opposing us on this issue – then we say: this ruling is necessary for other anthropomorphists. Then we have established the proof of the falsehood of the statement of the infinite divisibility of the part, so he also becomes refuted by the absence of a proof for the falsehood of that issue. Then we say to them: what...
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a doubt raised by the anthropomorphists. They argue that it is impossible for a non-body to be described with attributes like life, knowledge, and power. Nasafi calls this a mere claim (da'wa). He then argues from the witnessed world (shahid): we find things that are described with these attributes yet are not bodies. He explains that a living body is composed of indivisible parts (juz' la yatajuzza') called substances (jawahir), and each part individually has life. Thus, the composite whole is living, but the life is actually in the parts, which are not themselves bodies. Therefore, life can inhere in non-bodies. He then anticipates an objection from Hisham ibn al-Hakam, who denied indivisible parts, positing instead infinite divisibility. Nasafi notes that Hisham is one of the opponents on this issue. He responds that even if one accepts Hisham's position, the ruling (fasl) is still necessary for other anthropomorphists who do accept indivisible parts. Moreover, Nasafi states that he has established proof against infinite divisibility, so Hisham is also refuted. The text ends abruptly with 'then we say to them: what...' indicating that the argument continues on the next page.

جزء لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument relies entirely on the existence of indivisible parts; if one denies them, the refutation fails.
Correction: Nasafi shows that even if one denies indivisible parts (as Hisham does), the refutation still holds for those who accept them, and he also provides a separate proof against infinite divisibility.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi refute the claim that non-bodies cannot have attributes?
A) By proving that God is a body.
B) By showing that living bodies are composed of non-bodily parts that have life.
C) By denying the existence of life.
D) By accepting infinite divisibility.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the anthropomorphist doubt by showing that life can exist in non-bodily indivisible parts, and even if one denies such parts, the argument still holds for other positions.

Refuting the Claim that Only Bodies Have Attributes
Imam Nasafi refutes the first rational doubt of the opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the claim that only bodies can be described as living, knowing, and powerful.

Core Idea
Life is found in non-bodies, so God's life does not require a body.

Imam Nasafi tackles a crucial objection from anthropomorphists who argue that attributes like life and knowledge can only belong to bodies. He shows that their claim is baseless and that the observable world actually contains living entities that are not bodies, such as atoms. This lesson unpacks his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponents' claim
Read the opponent's doubt: that it is impossible for a non-body to have attributes like life and knowledge. Note that Nasafi calls this a mere claim without proof.
This is the first rational doubt he refutes.
2
Examine Nasafi's counterexample
Notice how Nasafi uses atoms (jawahir) as an example of living non-bodies. Each atom has life even though it is not a body. This undermines the opponent's reliance on the observable world.
Atoms are parts of bodies but not bodies themselves.
3
Address the infinite division objection
See how Nasafi handles the objection from Hisham ibn al-Hakam about atoms not existing. He says the argument still binds other anthropomorphists, and he notes that infinite division is invalid.
Thus the refutation stands even against those who deny atoms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على شبه الخصوم أنه تعالى حي قادر جئنا إلى دفع ما تمسكوا به من الشبهات . فأما العقلية منها ففاسدة لحصولها على دعوى متعرية عن البرهان أما الشبهة الأولى وهي عالم ويستحيل اتصاف ما ليس بجسم بهذه الصفات، فهي مجرد دعوى، وتعلقه بالشاهد فاسد فإن في الشاهد نجد ما هو موصوف بهذه الصفات وليس بجسم، فإن الجسم إذا كان حيا وهو متركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة وهي المسماة جواهر ما قام بكل جزء منه حياة على حدة، فيكون حيا وإن لم يكن جسما، فإن منعوا وجود ما لا يتجزأ من الأجزاء على أصل هشام ابن الحكم، إذ الجزء عنده يتجزأ لا إلى نهاية له وهو أحد المخالفين لنا في هذه المسألة، فنقول: هذا الفصل لازم على غيره من المجسمة. ثم إنا أقمنا الدلالة على بطلان القول بتجزؤ الجزء لا إلى نهاية، فيصير أيضا محجوجا بعدم قيام الدليل على بطلان تلك المسألة.
Refutation of the doubts of the opponents: He is Living, Powerful. We come to repelling the doubts they cling to. As for the rational ones among them, they are invalid because they are based on a claim devoid of proof. As for the first doubt—namely, that it is impossible for what is not a body to be described with these attributes—it is a mere claim, and its reliance on the witnessed world is invalid. For in the witnessed world we find that which is described with these attributes while not being a body. For the body, when it is living, is composed of indivisible parts called atoms (jawahir), and in each part a separate life subsists, so it is living even if it is not a body. If they deny the existence of indivisible parts according to the doctrine of Hisham ibn al-Hakam—since with him the part divides infinitely and he is one of those who oppose us on this issue—we say: this argument is binding on other anthropomorphists. Then we have established the proof of the invalidity of the doctrine of infinite division of the part, so he is also defeated by the lack of proof of the invalidity of that issue.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (radd) of a specific doubt. Imam Nasafi first states that the opponents' rational doubts are baseless because they are mere claims without proof. The first doubt is the claim that what is not a body cannot be described as living, knowing, or powerful. He argues this is a mere assertion, and its appeal to the observable world (shahid) is flawed because in the observable world we find things that have these attributes but are not bodies. For example, a living body is composed of indivisible atoms, each of which has life. Thus, the atom itself is living but is not a body (since it is part of a body). If the opponent (like Hisham ibn al-Hakam who denies indivisible atoms) argues that parts are infinitely divisible, Nasafi responds that this argument still binds other anthropomorphists who accept atoms. Furthermore, he states that the doctrine of infinite division is itself invalid, so that opponent is also refuted. Thus, the claim that only bodies can have these attributes is not proven.

شبهة
الشاهد
الجسم
الجواهر
هشام ابن الحكم
التجزؤ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only bodies can be described as living, knowing, or powerful.
Correction: Even in the observable world, atoms (indivisible parts) are living but are not bodies, so life can exist in non-bodies, and God's attributes are not dependent on being a body.

Checkpoint

Bodiliness: Not a Cause Nor a Condition for Divine Attributes
Nasafi refutes the claim that bodiliness is a cause or condition for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi argues that being a body cannot be a cause or condition for possessing attributes of perfection, and why this refutes the anthropomorphists.

Core Idea
Bodiliness is neither cause nor condition for divine attributes; the arguments collapse under scrutiny.

Imam Nasafi engages with the second doubt of the anthropomorphists, who claim that God must be a body to possess attributes. He forces them to clarify whether they consider bodiliness a cause or a condition. Then he systematically dismantles both positions, showing that neither holds up to rational analysis. This passage exemplifies the Maturidi method of using rational argumentation to defend divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get an overview of the argument's flow.
Notice the repeated structure of 'If they say... we say'.
2
Identify the two options: cause vs. condition
Nasafi first asks the opponents to clarify whether bodiliness is a cause (illa) or a condition (shart) for attributes. Understand the distinction.
A cause necessitates its effect; a condition enables but does not necessitate.
3
Examine the refutation of the cause option
If bodiliness were a cause, then every body would necessarily have attributes like life, knowledge, and power. But inanimate bodies lack these, so the argument fails.
Nasafi gives examples like motion and blackness to illustrate causal necessity.
4
Examine the refutation of the condition option
If bodiliness is a condition, it is a claim without proof. The analogy from the witnessed world leads to either stipulating worldly limitations (which is disbelief) or invalidating the evidence. Also, why bodiliness rather than self-subsistence?
This section shows the weakness of the conditional argument.
5
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Since God is proven eternal and possessing attributes through other proofs, the opponents' demand for bodiliness is baseless. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument's thrust is clear.
Note that the passage continues, but the core refutation is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم: ماذا تدعون أن كون الحي القادر العالم جسما جار مجرى العلل لهذه الأوصاف أم جار مجري الشروط؟ فإن قالوا هو جار مجرى العلل وهو قول عامتهم نقول: إنه باطل، لأن كونه جسما لو كان تعلقه بكونه حيا عالما قادرا تعلق العلل بالأحكام، لما احتمل الانفكاك بينهما، إذ الانفكاك بين العلل العقلية وأحكامها محال كما في الحركة مع كون محلها متحركا، والسواد مع كون محله أسود وغير ذلك، وحيث رأينا أجساما ليست بحية ولا قادرة ولا عالمة ولا فاعلة وهي الجمادات علم أن كونه جسما لم يتعلق بكونه حيا قادرا عالما تعلق العلل بالأحكام، فإن قالوا: إن كونه جسما جار مجرى الشروط لكون الذات حيا عالما قادرا فاعلا لا مجرى العلل حتى إنما هو جسم يصح لثبوت هذه الأوصاف له، وما ليس بجسم فليس بصالح. قلنا : هذه أيضا دعوى. لم قلتم إن صلاحية ما يوجد في الشاهد حيا عالما قادرا كانت لكونه جسما، وعدم الصلاحية للأعراض لانعدام كونها أجساما؟ فإن اعتمدتم على مجرد الوجود فلم قلتم : إن مجرد الوجود يدل على كون ذلك شرطا؟ أليس أنكم لم تجدوا في الشاهد حيا قادرا سميعا بصيرا إلا ما هو لحم ودم متناه من الجهات الست (محل للآفات أفتشترطون هذا في الغائب؟ فإن قالوا: نعم)، فقد انسلخوا من الدين، وإن قالوا: لا. أبطلوا دليلهم. ثم نقول لهم وأي أثر لكونه جسما في كونه شرطا لقبول هذه الصفات؟ ولن يجدوا إلى ذلك سبيلا أبدا. ثم يقال لهم : ما من جنس إلا وهو قائم بالذات فلم قلتم : إن شرط كون الذات عالما قادرًا حيا هو كونه جسما لا كونه قائما بالذات؟ فكان تعليقكم ذلك بكونه جسما لا بكونه قائما بالذات دعوى لا برهان عليها . ثم نقول لهم: لما ثبت بالدليل أنه تعالى قديم وأنه حي عالم قادر، وأقمنا الدلالة أن الجسمية من
Then we say to them: What do you claim—that the living, powerful, knowing being's being a body runs the course of a cause (al-'illal) for these attributes, or the course of a condition (al-shurut)? If they say it runs the course of a cause—and that is the saying of their majority—we say: it is false, because if its being a body were related to its being living, knowing, powerful in the relation of a cause to its effect, separation between them would be impossible, since separation between rational causes and their effects is impossible, as in motion with its locus being moving, and blackness with its locus being black, and so on. And since we see bodies that are not living, not powerful, not knowing, not acting—namely inanimate objects—we know that its being a body is not related to its being living, powerful, knowing in the relation of a cause to its effect. If they say: Its being a body runs the course of a condition for the essence to be living, knowing, powerful, acting, not the course of a cause, such that it is a body that is valid for the establishment of these attributes in it, and what is not a body is not valid. We say: This too is a claim. Why do you say that the validity of what is found in the witnessed world as living, knowing, powerful is due to its being a body, and the lack of validity for accidents (a'rad) is due to their not being bodies? If you rely on mere existence, why do you say that mere existence indicates that that is a condition? Is it not that you have not found in the witnessed world a living, powerful, hearing, seeing being except what is flesh and blood, limited in the six directions, subject to defects? Do you then stipulate this in the unseen? If they say yes, they have stripped themselves of religion; if they say no, they have invalidated their evidence. Then we say to them: And what effect does its being a body have in its being a condition for accepting these attributes? And they will never find a way to that. Then it is said to them: Every genus subsists by itself, so why do you say that the condition for the essence to be knowing, powerful, living is its being a body rather than its being self-subsistent? Thus your attachment of that to its being a body rather than its being self-subsistent is a claim without proof. Then we say to them: Since it is established by proof that He is eternal and that He is living, knowing, powerful, and we have established the proof that bodiliness (al-jismiyya) is...
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the second doubt. Imam Nasafi challenges the opponents to specify whether being a body is a cause (illa) for the attributes or a condition (shart). If they say cause, he argues that a cause necessitates its effect inseparably, so every body would have to be living, knowing, and powerful. But we see inanimate bodies that lack these attributes, so bodiliness cannot be a cause. If they say condition, he argues that this is a mere claim. Why should being a body be a condition rather than being self-subsistent? Moreover, the observable world shows that living beings are bodies with specific limitations (flesh, blood, directions, defects). If the opponents stipulate these limitations for God, they become disbelievers; if not, their evidence collapses. He further asks what effect bodiliness actually has as a condition—none can be shown. The ultimate argument is that since God is established as eternal and possessing attributes through proofs, and bodiliness is not proven to be a condition, the opponents' position is baseless. The passage ends mid-sentence as it continues onto the next page.

Key Terms
العلة
الشرط
الجسمية
الشاهد
الغائب
الأعراض
القائم بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being a body is a necessary condition for possessing attributes like life, knowledge, and power.
Correction: Imam Nasafi shows that no evidence supports this, and the argument leads to contradictions or anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Nasafi, what is the consequence if the opponents say that being a body is a cause for the attributes?
A) They would have to accept that God is composite.
B) All bodies would have to be living, knowing, and powerful, but inanimate bodies disprove this.
C) They would be polytheists.
D) It would not affect the argument.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's refutation shows that bodiliness cannot be rationally established as a cause or condition for divine attributes, preserving God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself imagining God in human terms—thinking of Him as having a form, limits, or similar needs? How does Nasafi’s argument against making bodiliness a condition for attributes expose the hidden anthropomorphism in your own heart? Will you actively replace those mental images with the truth that He is unlike anything you have ever known?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes like His power and knowledge, do you rely on analogies from created beings? Nasafi shows that such analogies collapse when pressed. Are you willing to trust the revealed descriptions of God, even when they surpass your experience, and repent of any tendency to confine Him to your own limited categories?
?
The opponents demanded that God be a body based on what they saw in the world. Have you ever demanded that God conform to your expectations—how He should act, what He should provide, or how He should answer your prayers? What would it mean to let go of those demands and submit to His transcendence, even when it challenges your comfort?

Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
Refutation of the Claim that Being a Body is a Cause for Attributes
Refutation of the claim that being a body is a cause for God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why being a body cannot be a cause (illa) for God's attributes, using the rational principle that cause and effect are inseparable.

Core Idea
If being a body caused divinity, all bodies would be divine—but they are not.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi refutes a key objection from those who argue that God’s attributes must be caused by His being a body. He first frames the opponent’s choice: is being a body a cause or a condition? Then he shows that treating it as a cause leads to an absurdity, because we observe bodies that lack life, power, and knowledge. This argument relies on a fundamental rule of causality: a true cause cannot exist without its effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: dilemma, refutation, examples, conclusion.
The passage uses a conditional 'if...then' argument.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that being a body runs the course of causes for attributes. Find this phrase and understand the two options given.
3
Understand the principle of cause-effect inseparability
The author gives examples: movement cannot be separated from a moving locus, nor blackness from a black locus. Why is separation impossible?
This is a rational axiom in kalam.
4
Apply the counterexample
The author points to inanimate bodies (جمادات) that lack the attributes. How does this disprove the cause claim?
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Explain in your own words why being a body cannot be a cause for God's attributes.
This ties back to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تدعون أن كون الحي القادر العالم جسما جار مجرى العلل لهذه الأوصاف أم جار مجري الشروط؟ فإن قالوا هو جار مجرى العلل وهو قول عامتهم نقول: إنه باطل، لأن كونه جسما لو كان تعلقه بكونه حيا عالما قادرا تعلق العلل بالأحكام، لما احتمل الانفكاك بينهما، إذ الانفكاك بين العلل العقلية وأحكامها محال كما في الحركة مع كون محلها متحركا، والسواد مع كون محله أسود وغير ذلك، وحيث رأينا أجساما ليست بحية ولا قادرة ولا عالمة ولا فاعلة وهي الجمادات علم أن كونه جسما لم يتعلق بكونه حيا قادرا عالما تعلق العلل بالأحكام.
You claim that the existence of the living, powerful, knowing being as a body runs the course of causes for these attributes, or runs the course of conditions? If they say it runs the course of causes—and this is the statement of the majority of them—we say: It is false, because if being a body were attached to its being living, knowing, powerful with the attachment of causes to effects, then separation between them would not be possible, for separation between intellectual causes and their effects is impossible, as in movement with its locus being moving, and blackness with its locus being black, and other than that. And since we have seen bodies that are not living, nor powerful, nor knowing, nor acting—and they are inanimate objects—we know that being a body is not attached to its being living, powerful, knowing with the attachment of causes to effects.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that being a body is a cause (illa) for God's attributes of life, knowledge, and power. The author begins by presenting the opponent's dilemma: is being a body a cause or a condition? Then he addresses the cause claim, stating it is false. The reasoning is strict: if body were a cause, the attributes would be inseparable from it, as cause and effect are inseparable in rational terms. Examples are given: movement cannot be separated from its moving locus, nor blackness from its black locus. However, we observe many bodies (inanimate objects) that lack these attributes, proving that body is not a cause. This refutation relies on the logical principle that a true cause cannot exist without its effect.

Key Terms
جسم (jism)
علّة (illa)
حكم (hukm)
انفكاك (infikak)
جمادات (jamaadat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that any common trait between the observed and the unseen must be a cause.
Correction: The passage shows that being a body is a common trait but not a cause, because inanimate bodies do not have the attributes.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that being a body is a cause (illa) for God's attributes?
A) Because cause and effect cannot be separated, but we see bodies without attributes.
B) Because God is not a body.
C) Because the opponent has no proof.
D) Because attributes are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Being a body cannot be a cause for God's attributes because cause and effect are inseparable, and we observe bodies without divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the attributes of God—His life, power, and knowledge—do you ever find yourself unconsciously reducing Him to the level of created beings by assuming His attributes must have a cause like ours do? What specific analogy from this lesson will you use to guard your mind against that error?
?
The lesson shows that cause and effect are inseparable in rational thought. Have you ever acted as if the presence of a certain trait in you (like your body) must produce certain divine qualities? How can you cultivate a deeper reverence for God's transcendence by remembering that His attributes are not caused by anything?
?
If you truly internalize that God's essence and attributes are uncaused and unlike any created thing, what comfortable assumption about how God 'works' will you have to let go of? Write down one area of your life where you've been treating God's actions as mechanically caused by your circumstances, and commit to replacing that with tawakkul.

Refutation as Mere Assertion and Reductio Ad Absurdum
Exposes the claim as unproven and reduces it to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that being a body is a condition for divine attributes by exposing it as a mere assertion and using a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Core Idea
Observed patterns in the world do not force necessary conditions on the Unseen.

The opponent argues that being a body is a condition for attributes like life and knowledge, based on what we see in the world. The author immediately identifies this as a mere claim without proof. He then challenges the reasoning: if you rely on observed existence, why not also require God to be flesh and blood? This leads to a dilemma that demolishes the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
The opponent states that being a body is like a condition (shart) for the essence to have attributes, not a cause, and that non-bodies cannot have these attributes.
2
Identify the author's initial response
The author dismisses it as a mere claim ('hadhihi da'wa'). Notice that he immediately challenges the opponent to provide proof.
This sets the tone for the entire refutation.
3
Understand the two rhetorical questions
First question: Why do you attribute validity to body rather than to something else? Second: If you rely on mere existence, why does that indicate a condition?
4
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
The author points out that in the witnessed realm, every living, hearing, seeing thing is flesh, blood, and finite. If you require that for God, you leave religion; if not, your proof fails.
This dilemma is the core of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إن كونه جسما جار مجرى الشروط لكون الذات حيا عالما قادرا فاعلا لا مجرى العلل حتى إنما هو جسم يصح لثبوت هذه الأوصاف له، وما ليس بجسم فليس بصالح. قلنا : هذه أيضا دعوى. لم قلتم إن صلاحية ما يوجد في الشاهد حيا عالما قادرا كانت لكونه جسما، وعدم الصلاحية للأعراض لانعدام كونها أجساما؟ فإن اعتمدتم على مجرد الوجود فلم قلتم : إن مجرد الوجود يدل على كون ذلك شرطا؟ أليس أنكم لم تجدوا في الشاهد حيا قادرا سميعا بصيرا إلا ما هو لحم ودم متناه من الجهات الست محل للآفات أفتشترطون هذا في الغائب؟ فإن قالوا: نعم فقد انسلخوا من الدين، وإن قالوا: لا. أبطلوا دليلهم. ثم نقول لهم وأي أثر لكونه جسما في كونه شرطا لقبول هذه الصفات؟ ولن يجدوا إلى ذلك سبيلا أبدا. ثم يقال لهم : ما من جنس إلا وهو قائم بالذات فلم قلتم : إن شرط كون الذات عالما قادرًا حيا هو كونه جسما لا كونه قائما بالذات؟ فكان تعليقكم ذلك بكونه جسما لا بكونه قائما بالذات دعوى لا برهان عليها . ثم نقول لهم: لما ثبت بالدليل أنه تعالى قديم وأنه حي عالم قادر ، وأقمنا الدلالة أن الجسمية من أمارات الحدوث وثبوت أمارات الحدوث في القديم محال كانت نتيجة هذه المقدمات الصادقة الثابتة بالبرهان اليقيني خروج الجسمية عن كونها شرطا، لكون الذات عالما قادرا حيا، والله الموفق.
If they say: 'Being a body runs the course of conditions for the essence to be living, knowing, powerful, acting, not the course of causes, such that it is only that a body is valid for the establishment of these attributes for it, and what is not a body is not valid.' We say: This too is a claim. Why do you say that the validity of what is found in the witnessed realm as living, knowing, powerful was due to its being a body, and the invalidity of accidents is due to their not being bodies? If you rely on mere existence, why do you say that mere existence indicates that this is a condition? Is it not that you have not found in the witnessed realm a living, powerful, hearing, seeing thing except that which is flesh and blood, finite from the six directions, a place for afflictions? Do you then condition this in the unseen? If they say: 'Yes,' they have stripped themselves from religion, and if they say: 'No,' they have invalidated their proof. Then we say to them: And what effect does being a body have in its being a condition for accepting these attributes? They will never find a way to that. Then it is said to them: There is no genus except that it is self-subsistent, so why do you say that the condition for the essence to be knowing, powerful, living is its being a body, not its being self-subsistent? So your attaching that to its being a body, not to its being self-subsistent, is a claim without proof. Then we say to them: Since it has been established by proof that He, exalted, is eternal and that He is living, knowing, powerful, and we have established the indication that corporeality is one of the signs of temporality, and the establishment of signs of temporality in the Eternal is impossible, the result of these true premises established by certain proof is the removal of corporeality from being a condition for the essence to be knowing, powerful, living. And God is the Guide.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that being a body is a condition (shart) for God's attributes. The author systematically dismantles this position. First, he labels it a mere assertion without proof. He then challenges the reasoning: why attribute validity to body rather than to self-subsistence? He presses further: if the argument relies on observed existence, then one would also have to condition that God be flesh, blood, and finite—which leads to absurdity. If they accept, they leave religion; if not, their proof collapses. Next, he questions what effect 'being a body' actually has as a condition, asserting that they can never demonstrate it. He then points out that all genera are self-subsistent, so why privilege body over self-subsistence? Finally, he provides a positive proof: we have established that God is eternal and that corporeality is a sign of temporality. Since a sign of temporality cannot apply to the Eternal, corporeality is removed as a condition. This argument uses certain premises to demonstrate that body cannot be a condition for the Eternal's attributes.

Key Terms
شرط (shart)
جسم (jism)
دعوى (da'wa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The observed pattern that living beings are bodies is a necessary condition for life and attributes.
Correction: The author shows that this pattern is not a necessary condition, as it would lead to absurd conclusions about God if applied to the Unseen.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the opponent accepts that God must be flesh and blood?
A) They have a strong proof.
B) They have invalidated their proof.
C) They have stripped themselves from religion.
D) They have demonstrated a condition.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's claim that body is a condition for attributes is a mere assertion that collapses when its logical consequences are examined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that you have simply assumed from your experience of the created world? What specific pattern in your life have you mistakenly treated as a condition for Allah's attributes? How will you uproot that assumption and replace it with revelation-based knowledge?
?
The opponent was willing to strip themselves from religion rather than admit their proof was flawed. Have you ever clung to a flawed argument about Allah out of pride? What would it cost you to let it go? Will you surrender that pride today?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes, do you unconsciously imagine Him as having human limitations? This passage shows that such a comparison leads to absurdity. In what specific area of your worship do you still treat Allah as if He were like His creation? What step will you take tonight to correct that in your heart?

Positive Proof: Self-Subsistence as Condition and Corporeality as Sign of Temporality
Presents a positive proof that body cannot be a condition for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's positive argument that self-subsistence (qa'im bi-dhatihi) is the real condition for attributes, and that corporeality is a sign of temporality, thus cannot be a condition for the Eternal.

Core Idea
Corporeality is a sign of temporality, so it cannot be a condition for the Eternal's attributes.

After dismantling the opponent's claim as a mere assertion, the author provides a constructive argument. He questions why the condition is body rather than self-subsistence, since all genera are self-subsistent. Then he presents a proof: corporeality is a sign of temporality (amarat al-huduth), and the Eternal cannot have signs of temporality. Therefore, body cannot be a condition for His attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's third question
The author asks: what effect does being a body have as a condition? He asserts they can never find a real effect.
This challenges any supposed causal connection.
2
Understand the shift to self-subsistence
The author points out that all genera are self-subsistent (qa'im bi-dhatihi). So why privilege body over self-subsistence?
This suggests the real condition is self-subsistence.
3
Analyze the positive proof
The author presents a syllogism: (1) God is eternal, (2) corporeality is a sign of temporality, (3) signs of temporality cannot be in the Eternal, therefore corporeality is not a condition.
This is a certain proof based on established premises.

Divine Attributes

Self-Subsistence: The True Condition for Attributes
The author explains that self-subsistence is the condition for attributes, not composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that self-subsistence (qiyam bi-dhat) is the necessary condition for an essence to be described with attributes, and that being a body or composite is not required.

Core Idea
Attributes require a self-subsistent subject, not a bodily one.

In this passage, the author clears up a common confusion: many assume that only bodies can have attributes like life and knowledge. But the real condition, he explains, is self-subsistence. By following his logical reasoning, we will see why accidents cannot possess attributes and why the divine essence does not need to be composite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice the key terms like قيام بالذات (qiyam bi-dhat) and تركيب (tarkib).
2
Identify the central claim
The author states that an essence is living only if life subsists in it. Look for how he applies this to other attributes (knowledge, power). This establishes the need for a subject.
He repeats 'قيام صفة بذات' (subsistence of attribute in essence) several times.
3
Follow the logical necessity
The author argues that an attribute cannot subsist in something that is not self-subsistent. Trace the reasoning: if an attribute subsisted in another attribute, it would be impossible. Therefore, the subject must be self-subsistent.
He says 'قيام الصفة بالصفة محال' (subsistence of attribute in attribute is impossible).
4
Understand the role of composition
The author then clarifies that self-subsistence does not require composition (تركيب). Removing composition does not make attribute subsistence impossible. So the condition is self-subsistence, not bodyhood.
He contrasts his view with those who think a body is necessary.
5
Apply the conclusion
Finally, note why accidents cannot have attributes: they lack self-subsistence, not because they are not bodies. This refutes the doubt mentioned at the end.
The author says 'لا لكونها غير الجسم' (not due to their not being bodies).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نكشف عن حقيقة المعنى فنقول : إن الذات لن تكون حيا إلا وأن تكون الحياة قائمة به، وكذا العلم والعالم والقدرة والقادر، وقيام هذه الصفات بما لا قيام له بذاته محال لما مر غير مرة. إن قيام الصفة بالصفة محال، فكان من ضرورة قيام الصفة بذات أن يكون ذلك الذات قائما بنفسه، ولا ضرورة إلى أن يكون متركبا مؤتلفا، إذ ليس في زوال التركيب مع وجود القيام بالذات ما يوجب استحالة قيام صفة به، فإذا كان من شرط اتصاف الذات بهذه الصفات كونه قائما بنفسه لا كونه جسما، واستحالة اتصاف الأعراض بكونها حية قادرة عالمة سميعة فاعلة مريدة، لكونها غير قائمة بالذات لا لكونها غير الجسم. وبالوقوف على هذه الجملة ظهرت صحة ما ادعيت أن هذه الشبهة مبنية على دعوى خالية عن البرهان فلا يمكن التعويل عليها، والله الموفق.
Then we uncover the true meaning and say: Indeed, the essence will not be living unless life is subsisting in it, and likewise knowledge and the knowing, power and the powerful. And the subsistence of these attributes in that which has no self-subsistence is impossible, as has been mentioned more than once. Indeed, the subsistence of an attribute in an attribute is impossible. So it is necessary from the subsistence of an attribute in an essence that that essence be self-subsistent, and there is no necessity for it to be composite and compounded. For there is nothing in the removal of composition along with the existence of self-subsistence that necessitates the impossibility of an attribute subsisting in it. So if the condition for the essence to be described with these attributes is its being self-subsistent, not its being a body, and the impossibility of accidents being described as living, powerful, knowing, hearing, acting, willing, is due to their not being self-subsistent, not due to their not being bodies. And by pausing on this summary, the correctness of what I claimed becomes apparent: that this doubt is based on a claim devoid of proof, so it cannot be relied upon. And God is the Guide.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a positive exposition of the true condition for attributes. The author reveals the 'true meaning': for an essence to be living, knowledge and life must subsist (qā’im) in it. But attributes cannot subsist in something that is not self-subsistent, as repeated earlier. Since an attribute cannot inhere in another attribute, the subject must be self-subsistent (qā’im bi-nafsihi). Importantly, there is no need for the subject to be composite (murakkab) or compounded (mu’talif). The removal of composition does not hinder attribute subsistence. Thus, the condition is self-subsistence, not corporeality. Accidents are incapable of possessing attributes precisely because they lack self-subsistence, not because they are not bodies. The author concludes that the entire doubt is built on an unproven claim.

Key Terms
قيام بالذات (qiyam bi-dhat)
ذات (dhat)
صفة (sifa)
تركيب (tarkib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The condition for having attributes is being a body.
Correction: The correct condition is self-subsistence; being a body is not necessary. Accidents are not bodies yet lack attributes due to lack of self-subsistence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can accidents not possess attributes like life and knowledge?
A) Because they are not bodies.
B) Because they are not self-subsistent.
C) Because they are temporally originated.
D) Because they are not visible.

Key Takeaway
Self-subsistence, not bodyhood, is the essential condition for an essence to be described with attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live, know, and act every day. But are you truly self-subsistent? When you feel powerful, do you remember that your power is borrowed from the One who is Self-Subsistent? How would your prayer change if you really believed He owes nothing to anyone?
?
The passage shows that accidents cannot bear attributes because they are not self-subsistent. In what ways do you live like an accident—depending on situations or people for your sense of worth? What would it mean to ground your identity in the Self-Subsistent rather than in passing things?
?
If God's attributes require no composition, then He is perfect without any parts. Do you ever act as if God is 'missing' something unless you serve Him, as if He needs you? How does the truth that He is perfectly self-subsistent reshape your worship from obligation to need?

Self-Subsistence as the Condition for Divine Attributes
Nasafi explains that self-subsistence is the condition for attributes, not corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why self-subsistence, not corporeality, is the necessary condition for an essence to bear attributes, and how this refutes the sophism that attributes imply a body.

Core Idea
Divine attributes require self-subsistence, not corporeality.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi clarifies a crucial point about divine attributes. Many have mistakenly thought that since accidents cannot have attributes, only bodies can be described with life and knowledge. Nasafi corrects this: what is needed is that the essence be self-subsisting, not that it be a body. Let's read his explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice the structure: a claim, then proof, then refutation.
2
Identify the key claim
Nasafi states the condition for being described with attributes: self-subsistence (qiyām bi-dhāt), not being a body. Find this statement.
3
Follow the argument
Trace the reasoning: attributes cannot subsist in non-self-subsisting nor in another attribute; therefore the essence must be self-subsisting. Note that no composition is required.
4
See the refutation
Nasafi concludes that the objection (shubha) is based on an unproven claim. Understand why this refutes the opponent.
5
Summarise the lesson
Write a brief summary in your own words of the condition for attributes and why the sophism is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نكشف عن حقيقة المعنى فنقول: إن الذات لن تكون حيا إلا وأن تكون الحياة قائمة به، وكذا العلم والعالم والقدرة والقادر، وقيام هذه الصفات بما لا قيام له بذاته محال لما مر غير مرة. إن قيام الصفة بالصفة محال، فكان من ضرورة قيام الصفة بذات أن يكون ذلك الذات قائما بنفسه، ولا ضرورة إلى أن يكون متركبا مؤتلفا، إذ ليس في زوال التركيب مع وجود القيام بالذات ما يوجب استحالة قيام صفة به، فإذا كان من شرط اتصاف الذات بهذه الصفات كونه قائما بنفسه لا كونه جسما، واستحالة اتصاف الأعراض بكونها حية قادرة عالمة سميعة فاعلة مريدة، لكونها غير قائمة بالذات لا لكونها غير الجسم. وبالوقوف على هذه الجملة ظهرت صحة ما ادعيت أن هذه الشبهة مبنية على دعوى خالية عن البرهان فلا يمكن التعويل عليها، والله الموفق.
Then we uncover the reality of the meaning and say: The essence will not be living unless life subsists in it, and similarly knowledge and the knower, power and the powerful. The subsistence of these attributes in that which has no self-subsistence is impossible, as has been stated many times. The subsistence of an attribute in an attribute is impossible. Therefore, of necessity, for an attribute to subsist in an essence, that essence must be self-subsisting, and there is no necessity for it to be composite or compounded. For there is nothing in the removal of composition with the existence of self-subsistence that necessitates the impossibility of an attribute subsisting in it. So if it is a condition of the essence being qualified by these attributes that it be self-subsisting, not that it be a body, and the impossibility of accidents being described as living, powerful, knowing, hearing, acting, willing is because they are not self-subsisting, not because they are not bodies. By understanding this summary, the correctness of what I claimed becomes clear: that this sophism is based on a claim devoid of proof, so it cannot be relied upon. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of the true condition for divine attributes. The author first states that the Essence cannot be living unless life subsists in it, and similarly for knowledge and power. He then argues that an attribute cannot subsist in something that is not self-subsisting, as established earlier. Furthermore, an attribute cannot subsist in another attribute. Therefore, for an attribute to subsist in an essence, that essence must be self-subsisting (qāʼim bi-nafsihi). However, there is no requirement that the essence be composed or compounded (murakkab muʼtalif). The removal of composition does not prevent attributes from subsisting in a self-subsisting essence. Thus, the condition for being qualified by these attributes is self-subsistence, not corporeality. The impossibility of accidents being described as living, etc., is because they are not self-subsisting, not because they are not bodies. Consequently, the objection (shubha) that the author is refuting is based on an unproven claim and is invalid.

ذات
حياة
علم
قدرة
قيام بالذات
جسم
شبهة
