Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God must be a body for attributes to subsist in Him.
Correction: Nasafi explains that self-subsistence, not corporeality, is the necessary condition for an essence to bear attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the necessary condition for an essence to be described with attributes like life and knowledge?
A) That it be a body
B) That it be self-subsisting
C) That it be composed of parts
D) That it be an accident

Key Takeaway
Self-subsistence, not corporeality, is the essential prerequisite for an essence to be described with attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Your own existence, knowledge, and power are borrowed—you are not self-subsisting. How does recognizing that God alone is qāʼim bi-nafsihi transform your reliance on Him in a specific difficulty you are facing right now?
?
Nasafi dismisses the opposing view as 'a claim devoid of proof.' Are there other beliefs you hold about Deen that you've accepted without evidence? What would it cost you to honestly examine their basis today?

Theological Views on the Attribute of Hand (Yad)
Presents and critiques various views on the attribute 'hand'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main interpretations of the attribute 'hand' (yad) and the author's method of refuting views that are inconsistent with their own theological principles.

Core Idea
Divine attributes cannot be reduced to created things without contradicting scripture and reason.

This passage surveys several theological positions on the meaning of Allah's attribute 'hand.' After listing the views of Ahl al-Hadith, Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and al-Jubba'i, the author refutes the Mu'tazili interpretations by showing they contradict their own principles. The passage is a good example of the Maturidi method of critiquing other schools from within their own frameworks.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify each view
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. List the different groups mentioned and what each says about the attribute 'hand' (yad).
There are four distinct views: Ahl al-Hadith, Ash'aris (two sub-views), Mu'tazila, and al-Jubba'i.
2
Analyze the refutations
Notice how the author refutes the Mu'tazili and al-Jubba'i interpretations. He does not simply reject them but points out inconsistencies with their own principles.
For the Mu'tazili 'power' interpretation, he notes they deny power as an attribute. For al-Jubba'i's 'blessing' interpretation, he uses the creation of Adam verse.
3
Reflect on the author's own position
The author says the Mu'tazili interpretation is 'sound upon our principle' but not on theirs. Ask: What does this imply about the Maturidi position on the attribute of power?
This shows that the author (Maturidi) affirms the attribute of power, unlike the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجماعة من أهل الحديث يزعمون أن اليد صفة الله تعالى يخص بها أولياءه بما شاء. واليدان عند بعض الأشعرية صفتان، وعند القلانسي هما صفة واحدة، وعند جماعة من المعتزلة اليد عبارة عن القدرة، وهذا على أصلهم غير مستقيم، لأنهم لا يثبتون الله تعالى قدرة فكيف تكون اليد عبارة عن القدرة؟ فأما على أصلنا فهو مستقيم، وزعم الجبائي أن اليد عبارة عن النعمة، وهذا على أصله لا يستقيم، لأنه تعالى أخبر أنه خلق آدم عليه السلام بيديه والنعمة عند الجبائي مخلوق لا يخلق به مخلوق، فكذا عندهم تخصيص أحد بنعمة غير جائز، وقد خص بها آدم عليه السلام.
And a group of the people of hadith (Ahl al-Hadith) claim that the hand (al-yad) is an attribute of Allah Almighty with which He distinguishes His friends as He wills. And the two hands (al-yadan) according to some of the Ash'aris are two attributes, and according to al-Qalanisi they are one attribute. And according to a group of the Mu'tazila, 'hand' is an expression for power (al-qudra), and this is not sound upon their own principle, because they do not affirm for Allah Almighty power; so how can 'hand' be an expression for power? As for upon our principle, it is sound. And al-Jubba'i claimed that 'hand' is an expression for blessing (al-ni'ma), and this upon his principle is not sound, because He Almighty informed that He created Adam, peace be upon him, with His two hands, and blessing according to al-Jubba'i is a created thing; a created thing cannot create another created thing. Likewise, according to them, distinguishing someone with a blessing is not permissible, and He did distinguish Adam, peace be upon him, with it.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques different theological views on the attribute of 'hand' (yad) attributed to Allah. The author begins with the view of a group of Ahl al-Hadith who take 'hand' as a real attribute but unspecified in modality. Then he notes that among the Ash'aris, some say there are two attributes (corresponding to the dual 'two hands'), while Qalanisi says it is one attribute. Next, a group of Mu'tazila interpret 'hand' as power (qudra). The author refutes this by pointing out that the Mu'tazila do not affirm the attribute of power for Allah, so their interpretation is inconsistent with their own principle. However, he adds that upon the author's own (Maturidi) principle, it would be sound because they do affirm power. Then he refutes al-Jubba'i's interpretation of 'hand' as blessing (ni'ma). The refutation is based on the verse of creation: Allah created Adam with His two hands. If 'hand' means blessing, then it would imply that a created thing (blessing) created another created thing, which is impossible. Also, blessing is something that can be given to one but not another, but it is not permissible according to them to distinguish someone with a blessing? Actually the argument: they hold that distinguishing with a blessing is not permissible, yet Adam was distinguished? The text says 'they hold that distinguishing someone with a blessing is not permissible, and He did distinguish Adam with it.' This may be a logical refutation. The author points out the inconsistency of Jabba'i's own principle with the verse.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author accepts the Mu'tazili interpretation of 'hand' as power.
Correction: The author only says it is 'sound upon our principle' (i.e., consistent with Maturidi ontology) but does not endorse it; his own view is that of Ahl al-Hadith or the Ash'ari position.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the Mu'tazili interpretation of 'hand' as power inconsistent?
A) Because they deny the attribute of power.
B) Because the Qur'an explicitly says 'hand' is a body part.
C) Because they interpret 'hand' as blessing.
D) Because they reject all attributes.

Key Takeaway
Theological interpretations of divine attributes must be consistent with both scripture and the interpreter's own theological principles.

Interpreting Ambiguous Terms: Yamin, 'Ayn, Janb, and Nur
Lists multiple meanings for yamin, 'ayn, janb, nur and selects those befitting Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author applies the method of ta'wil to specific ambiguous terms by listing their possible meanings and selecting the one that does not contradict tawhid.

Core Idea
Context resolves ambiguity in divine attributes.

In this passage, the author continues the method of interpreting ambiguous terms by providing concrete examples. For each term—yamin, 'ayn, janb, and nur—he lists multiple possible meanings, including literal bodily references, and then shows why only certain meanings are appropriate for Allah. This lesson walks through each example to see the ta'wil process in action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the range of meanings for each term.
Notice the poetic citation for yamin.
2
Identify the terms and their meanings
For each term, list the possible meanings given by the author. Pay attention to which meanings are rejected as incompatible with tawhid.
3
Examine the criterion used
The author consistently chooses the meaning that 'does not contradict tawhid'. This is the key principle of ta'wil.
Tawhid here means the absolute oneness and transcendence of Allah.
4
Analyze the argument for janb
The author uses the Quranic verse (39:56) to show that 'janb' cannot mean a limb because negligence (tafrit) applies to obedience, not a limb.
This is a specific example of contextual interpretation.
5
Reflect on the method
Consider how this approach prevents anthropomorphism (tashbih) while affirming the revealed text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واليمين يذكر ويراد بها الجارحة، ويذكر ويراد بها القوة قال القائل: إذا ما رأيته رفعت لمجد تلقاه عرابة اليمين أي بالقوة، والعين تذكر ويراد بها الحفظ، وتذكر ويراد بها الرؤية، وتذكر ويراد بها الجارحة فتحمل على ما لا يناقض التوحيد، والجنب يذكر ويراد به الجارحة، ويراد به الحذاء، ويراد به الجوار والصاحب بالجنب، ويراد به السقوط، ويراد به الأجل، ويراد به الأمر، يقال: هل تعلم ما فعلت في جنبي أي في أمري، ويراد به الطاعة، فيحمل المذكور في الآية على ما يليق بصفات الله تعالى ولا ينقض التوحيد. يحققه أنه قال: عَلَى مَا فَرَّطْتُ فِي جَنْبِ اللهِ (سورة الزمر 39 - الآية 56) و التفريط لا يكون في الجارحة، إنما يكون في الطاعة والمضي في موجب أمره. والنور يذكر ويراد به المنور.
And 'the right hand' (al-yamin) is mentioned and by it is meant the limb, and it is mentioned and by it is meant strength. The poet said: 'When I see him raised for glory, I meet him [؟] the right hand' meaning strength. And 'the eye' (al-'ayn) is mentioned and by it is meant protection, and it is mentioned and by it is meant sight, and it is mentioned and by it is meant the limb; so it is applied to what does not contradict tawhid. And 'the side' (al-janb) is mentioned and by it is meant the limb, and by it is meant the side (al-hidha'?), and by it is meant the neighbor (al-jawar) and the companion by one's side, and by it is meant falling (al-suqut), and by it is meant the term (al-ajal), and by it is meant the command (al-amr). It is said: 'Do you know what was done in my side (janbi)?' i.e., in my affair. And by it is meant obedience. So what is mentioned in the verse is applied to what befits the attributes of Allah Almighty and does not violate tawhid. This is confirmed by the fact that He said: 'Over what I neglected in the matter of Allah' (al-Zumar 39:56), and negligence (al-tafrit) does not occur in the limb, rather it occurs in obedience and proceeding in the implication of His command. And 'the light' (al-nur) is mentioned and by it is meant the illuminator (al-munawwir).
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage continues the method of ta'wil by listing more ambiguous terms and their possible meanings. For each term, the author shows that it has both literal (limb) and figurative meanings, and the appropriate meaning that does not contradict tawhid is chosen. The term 'yamin' can mean limb or strength; a poetic line is cited to show usage for strength. The term 'ayn' can mean protection, sight, or limb. The term 'janb' has many meanings: limb, side, neighbor, falling, term, command, obedience. The author gives an example from the Qur'an: 'On what I neglected in the side (janb) of Allah' (39:56). Negligence (tafrit) applies to obedience, not a limb, so here 'janb' must mean something like 'command' or 'obedience'. Finally, 'nur' (light) is mentioned and by it is meant 'the illuminator' (al-munawwir), i.e., Allah who gives light, not a physical light. Thus, all these terms are interpreted in ways consistent with tawhid.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin)
العين (al-'ayn)
الجنب (al-janb)
النور (al-nur)
التفريط (al-tafrit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ambiguous terms like yamin, 'ayn, janb, nur each have only one fixed meaning.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that each term has multiple possible meanings, and the correct interpretation is the one that suits Allah's majesty and does not violate tawhid.

Checkpoint
How does the author argue that 'janb' in the verse 'عَلَى مَا فَرَّطْتُ فِي جَنْبِ اللهِ' does not mean a limb?
A) Because a limb cannot be neglected.
B) Because the word 'janb' never means limb in the Qur'an.
C) Because the verse is metaphorical.
D) Because the author does not give a reason.

Key Takeaway
Ambiguous terms in the Qur'an have multiple possible meanings; the theologian's task is to select the one that befits Allah's transcendence.

Repent from that and embrace the transcendence that truly befits Him, even if it feels more distant at first.

God's Laughter is His Pleasure
Nasafi explains God's laughter as metaphorical for His pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine attributes like 'laughter' are interpreted metaphorically in Sunni theology, referring to God's pleasure and not to a physical action.

Core Idea
God's laughter is a metaphor for His pleasure, not a literal action.

When the Prophet ﷺ narrated that God 'laughs' at His friends, some might imagine a human-like laughter. Imam Nasafi, following the Maturidi tradition, clarifies that this is a metaphorical expression. He cites Arabic linguistic usage and poetry to show that 'laughter' can mean 'showing pleasure' or 'manifestation.' This lesson unpacks his evidence and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once slowly, then read the literal translation. Notice the key term 'يضحك' and the explanation 'أي ظهر الرضا عنهم'.
The passage includes poetic lines; don't worry about the poetry details yet.
2
Understand the core interpretation
Nasafi directly states that 'laughs' means 'shows pleasure' (zahara al-rida). This is the central claim. The rest is supporting evidence.
3
Examine the Arabic linguistic examples
Nasafi gives two examples: the palm blossom 'laughs' when it opens, and gardens 'laugh' with flowers. Both illustrate metaphorical use of 'laughter' for manifestation or beauty.
These are common Arabic idioms.
4
Analyze the poetic evidence
The poet uses 'showing molars' to exaggerate the appearance of evil. Nasafi explains this is a metaphor, not literal. This reinforces that divine attributes can be metaphorical.
5
Reflect on the theological principle
The key takeaway: when scripture attributes human-like actions to God, Sunni theologians interpret them in a way befitting His majesty, often as metaphors for mercy, pleasure, or power.
This protects tawhid and avoids anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما روي أنه تعالى يضحك إلى أوليائه أي ظهر الرضا عنهم يقال: ضحكت الطلعة إذا أظهرت ما في جوفها. ويقال: ضحكت الرياض بأنوارها إذا ظهرت، ويقولون إذا أرادوا المبالغة في وصف ظهور الشيء: أبدى ناجذيه. قال القائل: قوم إذا الشر أبدى ناجذيه لهم طاروا إليه ذرافات ووحدانا وليس للشر ناجذان في الحقيقة، وإنما المراد المبالغة في ظهوره تشبيها بمن.
And what is narrated that He Almighty laughs at His friends, meaning that He shows pleasure with them. It is said: 'The palm blossom laughed' when it shows what is inside it. And it is said: 'The gardens laughed with their flowers' when they appear. And they say, when they intend exaggeration in describing the appearance of something: 'He showed his molars.' The poet said: 'A people who, when evil shows its molars to them, they fly to it in groups and singly.' And evil does not actually have molars, but the intention is exaggeration in its appearance by comparing it to one who.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage interprets the hadith that attributes laughter to God. The author explains that 'laughs' here means 'shows pleasure' (azhara al-rida). He then provides linguistic and poetic evidence for metaphorical usage: the palm blossom is said to 'laugh' when it opens, gardens 'laugh' with flowers when they appear, and poets use 'showing molars' to exaggerate the appearance of something. The example of evil showing its molars is given, noting that evil does not literally have molars; it is exaggeration. Thus, God's laughter is a metaphor for His pleasure.

Key Terms
يضحك
الرضا
المبالغة
تشبيه
الناجذين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God laughs literally, with a mouth and teeth like humans.
Correction: Nasafi shows that 'laughter' in the hadith is a metaphor for God's pleasure, supported by Arabic linguistic usage where 'laughter' can mean appearance or manifestation.

Checkpoint

B) Showing pleasure and approval
C) Creation of joy
D) Anger

Key Takeaway
When God is described as 'laughing' in hadith, it metaphorically signifies His pleasure and approval, not a literal action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Allah's Attributes of Majesty are Not Physical
Sanusi clarifies that Allah's attributes of greatness are spiritual, not physical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's attributes of majesty (al-azama) and pride (al-kibriya) refer to His spiritual splendor, not physical size, and how this refutes anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Allah's greatness is about sublime majesty, not physical bulk.

Have you ever thought that describing Allah as 'great' might imply physical size? The misunderstanding arises from applying human categories to the Divine. In this passage, Imam Sanusi clearly distinguishes between spiritual majesty (jalal, rif'a) and physical bulk, showing that praise of Allah is based on His transcendent splendor, not on physical parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the core passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the two sets of terms: spiritual vs physical.
Key terms: العظمة, الكبرياء, الجلال, الرفعة, الغلظ, الضخامة.
2
Identify the distinction
The author states that attributes like majesty are 'in terms of splendor and elevation, not thickness and bulk.' Understand that this is a rejection of physical interpretation.
3
Understand the evidence
The evidence given is that these are attributes of praise, and praise is based on spiritual qualities, not multiplicity of parts (which would imply physical composition). Grasp why this is a strong refutation.
4
Connect to conclusion
See how this invalidates the anthropomorphists (mujassima). Reflect on why this distinction is crucial for pure tawhid.
Anthropomorphists were groups who attributed physical form to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يوصف الله تعالى به من العظمة والكبرياء فذاك من حيث الجلال والرفعة لا من حيث الغلظ والضخامة بدليل أنهما من صفات المدح، والمدح يكون بما ذكرنا لا بكثرة الأجزاء فيبطل تعلق المجسمة به والله الموفق.
And what Allah the Exalted is described with of majesty and pride, that is in terms of splendor and elevation, not in terms of thickness and bulk. As evidence that they are attributes of praise, and praise is with what we mentioned, not with multiplicity of parts. Thus the association of the anthropomorphists (mujassima) with it is invalidated. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage clarifies that Allah's attributes of majesty (al-azama) and pride (al-kibriya) are not physical qualities like thickness or bulk. Rather, they refer to His splendor and highness. The author argues that these are attributes of praise, and praise is based on such meanings, not on having many parts (which would imply physicality). This refutes the anthropomorphists (mujassima) who attribute literal physicality to Allah.

Correction: They refer to spiritual splendor and elevation, not physical dimensions, as these are attributes of praise and praise is based on spiritual qualities, not multiplicity of parts.

Why Divine Attributes Do Not Admit Superlatives
Nasafi argues that terms like wujud do not admit superlatives, while jism does.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributes like existence and self-subsistence cannot take comparative or superlative forms, whereas composite concepts like jism can.

Core Idea
Attributes without increase cannot be compared.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi argues that certain attributes of Allah, such as existence (wujud) and self-subsistence (qiyam bil-dhat), do not admit of increase or decrease. Therefore, they cannot be expressed in comparative or superlative forms. This distinction helps clarify why terms like 'ajsam' (more bodily) are applicable only to created bodies, not to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first a list of attributes without increase, then the example of jism, then the analogy with 'ilm.
Pay attention to the terms تزايد, مبالغة, تفضيل.
2
Identify the two categories
Nasafi distinguishes between attributes that have no increase (e.g., الوجود, القيام بالذات) and those that do (e.g., كثرة الأجزاء for jism). Note why superlatives are impossible for the first category.
The key is that تزايد (increase) is absent in divine attributes.
3
Understand the analogy
Nasafi compares jism with 'ilm: just as 'ālim and a'lam revolve with 'ilm, so jasīm and ajsam revolve with kathrat al-ajzā'. This shows that jism is derived from composition, not from essence.
This analogy reinforces that attributes without increase do not derive terms of comparison.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبصر، والقيام بالذات، والوجود، والقيام بالنفس لما كان لا يجري فيها التزايد يجري فيهما المبالغة فلا يقال فيه فعيل ولا فعال ولا التفضيل، فلا يقال أفعل، وأنه لا يقال: هذا أوجد من ذاك ولا أقوم بالذات منه، وحيث رأينا أنهم يقولون عن كثرة الأجزاء: هذا جسيم على طريق المبالغة، وعند التفضيل يقولون: هذا أجسم من ذلك. دل أنه ليس باسم لما لا تزايد له في نفسه وهو الوجود، والقيام بالذات بل هو اسم لما له تزايد، وذلك هو كثرة الأجزاء لا معنى آخر متزايد فإن لفظة المبالغة والتفضيل من الجسم يدوران وجودا وعدما مع كثرة الأجزاء لا مع غيرها من المعاني فعلم أنه اسم له كالمبالغة بلفظ العلم والتفضيل بلفظة أعلم لما كانا يدوران مع العلم وجودا وعدما لا مع غيره من المعاني علم أن العالم مأخوذ من العلم فكذا هذا، وكذا القدير وأقدر من القدرة، وكذا في سائر الصفات
And sight, self-subsistence, existence, and self-standing, since they do not admit of increase, exaggeration and comparison do not apply to them, so neither the pattern faʿīl nor faʿʿāl nor the comparative is said, nor is afʿal said; and it is not said: this is more existent than that, nor more self-subsistent than he. And when we see that they say, concerning multitude of parts: this is jism (body) in the way of exaggeration, and in comparison they say: this is ajsam (more bodily) than that, it indicates that it is not a name for what has no increase in itself, namely existence and self-subsistence, but is a name for what has increase, and that is multitude of parts, not another increasing meaning. For the word of exaggeration and comparison from jism revolve, in existence and non-existence, with multitude of parts, not with other meanings. So it is known that it is a name for it, just like the exaggeration with the word ʿilm (knowledge) and the comparative with the word aʿlam (more knowledgeable), since they revolve with knowledge in existence and non-existence, not with other meanings. So it is known that ʿālim (knower) is derived from ʿilm. Likewise this, and likewise al-qadīr (powerful) and aqdar (more powerful) from qudra (power), and likewise in the rest of the attributes.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a proof by Imam Nasafi that attributes like existence and self-subsistence do not admit of increase, and therefore cannot take superlative forms (e.g., awjad, aqwam). He observes that the word jism is used in exaggerated and comparative forms (jasīm, ajsam) precisely because it indicates multitude of parts, which does admit increase. This shows that jism is not a name for what has no increase (existence or self-subsistence) but for what has increase, namely composition. Nasafi then draws an analogy with ʿālim and aʿlam, which are derived from ʿilm and revolve with it, showing that the derivation of jism follows the same pattern—it indicates its meaning. He concludes that the same principle applies to all attributes: those without increase do not take superlatives, whereas composite notions do.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because we say 'al-ʿaẓīm' (the Great) of God, we can also say 'ajsam' (more bodily) of Him.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that glory attributes are not based on composition; jism implies increase (multitude of parts), which is inapplicable to Allah.

Checkpoint

B) Because jism is a derogatory term.
C) Because jism is not found in the Quran.
D) Because jism means spiritual body.

Key Takeaway
Attributes that do not admit increase cannot be expressed in superlative forms.

Why Aqdam Is Used Despite Qadim's Absoluteness
Nasafi responds to the objection that qadim has a comparative form despite having no meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's response to the objection that the comparative 'aqdam' implies increase in an attribute that should be absolute.

Core Idea
Comparative forms like aqdam refer to priority in existence, not to increase in the attribute itself.

The objector points out that people say 'aqdam' (more ancient) even though qadim (ancient) is supposed to be an absolute attribute with no degrees. Nasafi responds by clarifying the meaning of such comparatives. They either indicate that qidam is a meaning that admits comparison, or they refer to priority in time, not to an increase in the essence of the attribute. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the objector's claim: the word 'aqdam' is used, so increase must be possible in qidam. But Nasafi rejects this logic.
2
Understand Nasafi's rejection
He calls the objection bankrupt and impossible, because comparison cannot exist where increase is impossible.
3
Examine the two explanations
First, qidam is a meaning that admits comparison. Second, even if not a meaning, it denotes priority in existence with exaggeration.
These are two valid interpretations from earlier Maturidi scholars.
4
Apply the distinction to wujud and qiyam
Nasafi notes that existence and self-subsistence never take comparatives, confirming no increase in those absolutes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: ليس في ورود لفظة التفضيل والمبالغة من معنى ما يدل على ثبوت التزايد في ذلك المعنى فإنهم يقولون: هذا قديم وهذا أقدم منه، والقدم ليس بمعنى، وما ليس بمعنى لا يجري فيه التزايد قيل: هذا كلام لا يحمل عليه إلا الإفلاس والوقاحة، فإن إثبات التفضيل فيما لا يتصور فيه التزايد والتفضيل محال، ومثل هذا المحال لا يصح بما نظن أنه نظيره ثم نقول: القدم عند قدماء أصحابنا معنى فيجري فيه التفاضل، بعضهم وإن لم يكن هو معنى ولكنه اسم للمتقدم في الوجود بشرط المبالغة لا لمطلق الوجود، فيكون قولهم: أقدم أي أسبق وجودا، ولا يطلق إلا عند هذا، فأما فيما نحن فيه فلا تزايد في الوجود ولا في القيام بالذات، ولا يطلق لفظة التفضيل عنه عند ترادف الوجود أو السبق في الوجود أو التأخر، إنما يطلق عند كثرة الأجزاء فدل أن ما ذهبوا إليه غير لازم.
For if it is said: The occurrence of the word of comparison and exaggeration does not indicate the establishment of increase in that meaning, for they say: this is qadīm (ancient) and this is aqdam (more ancient) than it, and qidam (ancientness) is not a meaning, and what is not a meaning does not admit of increase. It is said: This speech cannot be carried except upon bankruptcy and impudence, for establishing comparison in what does not admit of increase and comparison is impossible, and such an impossibility cannot be validated by what we think is its analogue. Then we say: Qidam according to the earlier of our companions is a meaning, so comparison occurs in it; some of them, although it is not a meaning, it is a name for what precedes in existence with the condition of exaggeration, not for absolute existence. So their saying 'aqdam' means prior in existence, and it is not used except at this. As for what we are discussing, there is no increase in existence or in self-subsistence, and the word of comparison is not used regarding them at equality of existence or priority in existence or posteriority; it is only used at multitude of parts. So it indicates that what they went to is not necessary.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that the comparative form aqdam is used for qadim even though qidam is not a meaning that admits increase, so the earlier argument is flawed. Nasafi refutes this by calling the objection bankrupt and impudent. He argues that comparison in what does not admit increase is impossible, and the example of aqdam does not disprove this. He explains that according to earlier Maturidi scholars, qidam is a meaning that does admit comparison. Alternatively, even if qidam is not a meaning, it is a name for prior existence with exaggeration, so aqdam means 'more prior in existence'—a valid comparison in a different category. In contrast, terms like wujud and qiyam bidh-dhat have no such variation, so their comparatives are never used. Thus the objector's point is irrelevant.

مبالغة

معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since 'aqdam' is used, any attribute can have a comparative form.
Correction: Nasafi distinguishes between attributes that refer to priority in time (which can have comparatives) and those that refer to essence (which cannot).

Checkpoint

A) It is used only for created things, not God.
B) Qidam is a meaning that admits increase, or it denotes priority in existence.
C) It is just a linguistic convention with no logical basis.
D) Aqdam actually means 'more powerful'.

Key Takeaway
The comparative 'aqdam' does not imply increase in the attribute of ancientness; it either refers to a meaning that admits degrees or to priority in existence with exaggeration.

Refutation of Changing Word Meanings Between Seen and Unseen
Nasafi refutes the objection that word meanings differ between seen and unseen, citing two disastrous consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that the meanings of words like jism do not change when applied to Allah, and the disastrous consequences if they did.

Core Idea
Changing word meanings between seen and unseen destroys language and permits blasphemy.

In this passage, Nasafi faces an objection: words like ʿaẓīm seem to have different meanings when applied to God. He responds forcefully, showing that if we allow such flexibility, we would undermine all language and open the door to applying any repulsive term to Allah. This lesson unpacks his argument and its two critical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the structure: objection followed by answer.
The objection uses an analogy with ʿaẓīm.
2
Identify the objector's argument
The objector claims that just as ʿaẓīm in the seen means composition but in the unseen means something else, so too jism might not imply composition in the unseen. Grasp this analogy.
3
Understand Nasafi's response
Nasafi calls it pure ignorance. He asserts that the requirement of language does not differ between seen and unseen. If it did, anything could be said of Allah.
He lists examples like tall, eating, etc.
4
Examine the two consequences
Nasafi enumerates two results: nullification of languages and permission to apply repulsive words to Allah. See how these follow from the objector's premise.
This shows the seriousness of the objection.
5
Apply the lesson
Reflect on why this principle is important: if we allow metaphorical reinterpretation to change meanings arbitrarily, we lose the ability to speak about God truthfully.
This connects to the broader topic of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إطلاق هذا في المخلوقات وإن كان يدل على التركب وإثبات المبالغة يدل على كثرة الأجزاء فلم قلتم: إنه في الغائب يدل على هذا؟ أليس أن لفظة العظيم في الشاهد تدل على الجثة والتركيب، وأعظم يدل على كثرة الأجزاء، ومع ذلك لا يفهم هذا منه في الغائب؟ قيل: هذا جهل محض؛ فإن مقتضى اللغة مما لا يختلف في الشاهد والغائب، ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يسمى الغائب طويلا عريضا، ساكنا، متحركا، آكلا، شاربا، ولا يراد بذلك ما يفهم في الشاهد، وفي هذا أمران: أحدهما إبطال اللغات، والثاني إجازة إطلاق كل لفظة مستشنعة على الباري جل وعلا، وهذا باطل.
For if they say: The application of this (word jism) to created things, though it indicates composition and the establishing of exaggeration indicates multitude of parts, then why did you say that in the unseen it indicates this? Is it not that the word al-ʿaẓīm (the great) in the witnessed indicates body and composition, and aʿẓam (greater) indicates multitude of parts, yet still this is not understood of it in the unseen? It is said: This is pure ignorance; for the requirement of language does not differ in the witnessed and the unseen. If this were permissible, it would be permissible to call the unseen tall, wide, stationary, moving, eating, drinking, without intending by that what is understood in the witnessed. And in this are two matters: one of them is the nullification of languages, and the second is the permission to apply every repulsive word to the Exalted and Glorious Creator, and this is false.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objectors argue that the word jism in the seen world indicates composition, but in the unseen it need not—just as ʿaẓīm in the seen indicates body and composition, but when applied to God it means glory, not body. Nasafi refutes this as pure ignorance, asserting that language does not change meaning between seen and unseen. If such a shift were allowed, one could apply any physical term to God, like 'tall' or 'eating', without intending the literal meaning. This would nullify language and permit blasphemous descriptions. Hence, the objector's analogy fails because ʿaẓīm is actually ambiguous (see next lesson), but jism is not; the attempt to treat them similarly is false.

Key Terms
الشاهد
الغائب
اللغة
العظيم
التركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Metaphorical reinterpretation (ta'wil) can arbitrarily change the meaning of any word when applied to Allah.
Correction: Nasafi shows that if such arbitrary change were allowed, language would be nullified and any repulsive term could be used for Allah. Rather, only words that are inherently ambiguous (like ʿaẓīm) can be interpreted differently, but not words with fixed meanings like jism.

Checkpoint
What two consequences does Nasafi list if we allow word meanings to change between seen and unseen?
A) Loss of faith and eternal punishment.
B) Nullification of languages and permission to apply repulsive words to God.
C) Agreement with philosophers and rejection of prophets.
D) Denial of divine attributes and affirmation of corporealism.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi asserts that words have consistent meanings across seen and unseen, and any attempt to arbitrarily change them would destroy language and permit blasphemy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Ambiguous vs Univocal Terms: 'Aẓīm and Jism
Nasafi distinguishes mushtarak and non-mushtarak terms with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that jism cannot be applied to Allah even metaphorically, while 'aẓīm can be used with the appropriate meaning.

Core Idea
'Aẓīm is ambiguous, jism is univocal—only the former can be used of God.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla continues the discussion of divine names and attributes. Nasafi distinguishes between terms that have multiple meanings (mushtarak) and those with a single fixed meaning. By examining the words 'aẓīm and jism, he shows that applying jism to God is impossible because its sole lexical meaning entails composition, while 'aẓīm can be used in the sense of majesty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow: Nasafi first explains 'aẓm, then jism, then addresses an objection.
The passage is cut off at the end; the final objection is incomplete.
2
Identify the two key terms
Find 'aẓm (greatness) and jism (body) in the text. Note how Nasafi describes each: 'aẓm has two meanings, jism has one.
Pay attention to the Arabic words مشتركة and ليست بمشتركة.
3
Understand the objection and response
The objector claims that jism can be used metaphorically like 'aẓm. Nasafi refutes this by showing jism's settled usage in the witnessed world.
The objection uses 'amr jasm' (a weighty matter) to argue for a majestic meaning.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
Consider why lexical usage in the witnessed world determines applicability to God. This is a key principle in kalam about divine names.
Univocal terms with imperfect meanings cannot be repurposed metaphorically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم: لفظة العظم في الشاهد مشتركة قد تطلق على كثرة الأجزاء، وقد تطلق على رفعة القدر والجلال، ويجوز استعماله في الشاهد على كل واحد من الأمرين، فإذا أطلق على الغائب يراد به ما يصح عليه من المعنيين وهو الجلال والرفعة دون ما لا يصح عليه، وهو التركب وكثرة الأجزاء، ولفظة الجسم ليست بمشتركة بل هي موضوعة لمعنى واحد وهو التألف والتركب، فلا يفهم منه في الغائب إلا هذا وهو محال عليه، فإن قالوا: نحن أيضا لا نقول له جسم من حيث التركب والتألف بل من حيث الجلال والرفعة، قلنا: لفظة الجسم ليست بمستعملة في الشاهد للجلال والرفعة وعظم الرتبة بل هي مستعملة للتأليف والتركب، وثبوت الجثة كلفظة الشخص والطلل والشح، وإطلاق هذه الأسماء على القديم ممتنع لامتناع ما وضعت هذه الأسماء له من المعاني على القديم فكذلك هذا فإن قالوا: لا بل في الجسم معنى العظمة ألا تراهم يقولون: هذا أمر جسيم يريدون به العظم لا التركيب إذ
Then we say to them: The word al-ʿaẓm (greatness) in the witnessed is ambiguous (mushtarak); it may be applied to multitude of parts, and may be applied to highness of rank and majesty, and its use in the witnessed for each of the two matters is permissible. So when it is applied to the unseen, what is intended is what is valid for it from the two meanings, namely majesty and highness, not what is not valid for it, namely composition and multitude of parts. But the word al-jism (body) is not ambiguous; rather it is placed for one meaning, namely combination and composition. So nothing is understood from it in the unseen except this, and it is impossible for Him. If they say: We also do not call Him jism in terms of combination and composition, but in terms of majesty and highness. We say: The word jism is not used in the witnessed for majesty and highness and greatness of rank; rather it is used for combination and composition and possession of a body, like the words shakhṣ (person), ṭalal (ruin), and shiḥḥ (stinginess). The application of these names to the Pre-eternal is impossible because of the impossibility upon the Pre-eternal of the meanings for which these names are placed. So likewise this. For if they say: No, in jism there is the meaning of greatness; do you not see that they say: 'This is a jasm (weighty) matter', meaning by it greatness, not composition?
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of the previous point. Nasafi distinguishes between mushtarak (ambiguous) and non-mushtarak (univocal) words. 'Aẓm is ambiguous in the seen—it can mean multitude of parts or majesty. When applied to God, only the majestic meaning is intended. However, jism is not ambiguous; it has one meaning: combination and composition. Therefore, when applied to God, it can only mean composition, which is impossible. The objector then claims they do not intend composition but majesty. Nasafi denies this, stating that jism is never used in the seen for majesty; it is always for composition and body, like shakhṣ or ṭalal. So applying it to God is invalid. The text cuts off during a final objection where the objector cites 'amr jasm' (a weighty matter) to prove jasm means greatness, but the passage ends mid-sentence.

Key Terms
مشتركة
لفظة العظم
لفظة الجسم
التألف
التركب
الجثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that jism can be used metaphorically for majesty in Arabic, similar to 'aẓīm.
Correction: Nasafi argues that jism has no metaphorical usage for majesty in the witnessed language; it is exclusively used for physical composition, so it cannot be applied to God.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi say that jism cannot be applied to God even if one intends majesty?
A) Because majesty is not a quality of God.
B) Because jism is never used in the Arabic language to mean majesty.
C) Because God has no body.
D) Because jism is synonymous with 'person'.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that terms used for God must be examined for their settled lexical meanings: ambiguous words can be used in their good sense, but univocal words implying imperfection are impossible to apply.

Refuting the Objection on 'Azim: Language Consistency
Author refutes an objection about applying 'azim to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the literal meaning of 'azim must be consistent across visible and invisible realms, and the consequences of denying this consistency.

Core Idea
Language cannot mean one thing for creation and another for God.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi confronts an objection: if 'azim implies composition in created beings, why should it imply the same for God? He refutes this by asserting the consistency of language and warns of two dangerous consequences if we allow terms to shift meaning arbitrarily. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify the objectors' argument: they claim that the term 'azim in the visible implies composition, but in the Invisible it might not.
2
Understand the refutation
Notice how the author rejects this as 'sheer ignorance' and states that language requirements do not change.
The author uses 'قيل' to introduce his own response.
3
Examine the two consequences
The author lists two disastrous outcomes if we accept shifting meanings: invalidation of languages and allowing repugnant terms for God.
These are the two matters (أمران) mentioned at the end.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider why consistency of language is a foundational principle in kalam for discussing divine attributes.
This connects to the broader method of analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the invisible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إطلاق هذا في المخلوقات وإن كان يدل على التركب وإثبات المبالغة يدل على كثرة الأجزاء فلم قلتم: إنه في الغائب يدل على هذا؟ أليس أن لفظة العظيم في الشاهد تدل على الجثة والتركيب، وأعظم يدل على كثرة الأجزاء، ومع ذلك لا يفهم هذا منه في الغائب؟ قيل: هذا جهل محض؛ فإن مقتضى اللغة مما لا يختلف في الشاهد والغائب، ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يسمى الغائب طويلا عريضا، ساكنا، متحركا، آكلا، شاربا، ولا يراد بذلك ما يفهم في الشاهد، وفي هذا أمران: أحدهما إبطال اللغات، والثاني إجازة إطلاق كل لفظة مستشنعة على الباري جل وعلا، وهذا باطل.
If they say: Using this (term) in created beings, although it indicates composition, and the comparative form indicates multiplicity of parts, so why do you say that it indicates this in the Invisible? Is it not that the word 'azim (عظيم) in the witnessed indicates body and composition, and a'zam (أعظم) indicates multiplicity of parts, yet this is not understood from it in the Invisible? It is said: This is sheer ignorance; for the requirement of language does not differ between the witnessed and the Invisible. If this were permissible, then it would be permissible to call the Invisible tall, wide, stationary, moving, eating, drinking, without intending by that what is understood in the witnessed. In this are two matters: one is the invalidation of languages, and the second is permitting every repugnant word to be applied to God, may He be exalted, and this is false.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objectors argue that even if 'azim implies composition in the visible world, it might not do so when applied to God. The author responds that language is consistent; to allow a different meaning would destroy language and allow any term to be applied to God. He highlights two disastrous consequences: invalidation of languages and permitting repugnant words for God.

Correction: The author argues that linguistic requirements are invariant; if 'azim implies composition in the visible, it must do so in the invisible. Otherwise, language loses consistency and any term could be used for God.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Ambiguous and Unequivocal Terms for God
Author distinguishes between ambiguous and unequivocal terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Kalam scholars distinguish between ambiguous and unequivocal terms, and why only the appropriate meaning of an ambiguous term can be applied to God.

Core Idea
One word can carry two meanings; the other carries only one—and that decides if it can describe God.

When describing God, theologians must choose words carefully. Some words have multiple meanings, while others have only one. This passage from Imam Nasafi explains how to handle each type, using the examples 'azim (great) and jism (body). Understanding this distinction prevents attributing impossible qualities to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's argument flow.
Pay attention to the terms مشتركة and غير مشتركة.
2
Identify the two terms and their meanings
Identify 'azim (عظم) and jism (جسم) in the passage. List the possible meanings for each as given by the author.
'Azim has two meanings; jism has only one.
3
Understand why jism cannot be used for God
Reflect on why the author says jism is not ambiguous. What would it imply about God if jism were used? Connect to the concept of divine simplicity.
The passage says jism means composition (تألف وتركب), which is impossible for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم: لفظة العظم في الشاهد مشتركة قد تطلق على كثرة الأجزاء، وقد تطلق على رفعة القدر والجلال، ويجوز استعماله في الشاهد على كل واحد من الأمرين، فإذا أطلق على الغائب يراد به ما يصح عليه من المعنيين وهو الجلال والرفعة دون ما لا يصح عليه، وهو التركب وكثرة الأجزاء، ولفظة الجسم ليست بمشتركة بل هي موضوعة لمعنى واحد وهو التألف والتركب، فلا يفهم منه في الغائب إلا هذا وهو محال عليه.
Then we say to them: The word 'azim (عظم) in the witnessed is ambiguous; it may be used for multiplicity of parts, and it may be used for sublimity of rank and majesty. It is permissible to use it in the witnessed for each of the two meanings. So when it is used for the Invisible, what is intended is the meaning that is valid for Him, which is majesty and sublimity, not what is not valid for Him, which is composition and multiplicity of parts. But the word jism (جسم) is not ambiguous; rather it is coined for one meaning, which is composition and joining, so nothing is understood from it in the Invisible except this, and that is impossible for Him.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the difference between ambiguous and unambiguous terms. The author shows that 'azim is ambiguous, carrying two possible meanings: multiplicity of parts or sublimity. When applied to God, the appropriate meaning (sublimity) is taken. In contrast, jism has only one meaning—composition—so it cannot be applied to God in any sense without implying something impossible.

Correction: The author clarifies that jism is not ambiguous; it has only one literal meaning (composition), so it cannot be applied to God at all, even metaphorically.

Key Takeaway
The word 'azim can be used for God because it has a noble meaning, but jism cannot because it only means composition—impossible for God.

Refuting the Argument from 'Amr Jasim'
Author refutes the claim that jism implies greatness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that jism implies greatness is fallacious and the distinction between jism and jasim.

Core Idea
The word jasim is metaphorical; jism itself never signifies greatness.

In this lesson, we examine an argument that seeks to prove that the word jism (body) implies greatness, based on the expression 'amr jasim' (a great matter). The objection is that if jism implies greatness, it could be used for Allah. The author refutes this by showing that the term used for greatness is jasim, not jism, and that it is a metaphorical intensive form. This highlights careful semantic distinctions in theological language.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the objection and the response.
2
Identify the objection
The objectors claim that 'jism' contains the meaning of greatness, citing the phrase 'amr jasim' and poetic evidence.
Consider why they think this supports their view.
3
Analyze the response
The author argues that 'jasim' is a metaphorical intensive form, not the same as 'jism'. It likens great matters to bodies with many parts.
Notice the distinction between 'jism' and 'jasim'.
4
Understand the conclusion
The objectors inadvertently concede that 'jism' is not used for glorification, so their inference fails.
This shows the importance of precise terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لا بل في الجسم معنى العظمة ألا تراهم يقولون: هذا أمر جسيم يريدون به العظم لا التركيب إذ لا تركيب للأمر لما أنه عرض قال جرير يخاطب عمر بن عبد العزيز: حملت أمرا عظيما فاصطبرت له وسرت فيه بأمر الله يا ويقال: جسيمات الأمور مشوبة بالمكاره، قلنا: هذا مستعمل على طريق المجاز تشبيها لما عظم من الأمور بما كثر أجزاؤه من الأجسام، إذ هذه لفظة مبالغة تنبئ عن كثرة في التركيب ومثل هذا لا يجوز إطلاقه على الله تعالى على أن المستعمل في هذا لفظة الجسيم لا لفظة الجسم، فإنهم ما استعملوا هذه اللفظة قط في معنى التعظيم فأنتم ساعدتمونا على امتناع لفظة الجسم في أسمائه، فما استعمل في معنى التعظيم، لا تجوزون إطلاقه، وما تجوزون إطلاقه لا يستعمل للتعظيم البتة؛ فبطل الاستدلال والله الموفق.
If they say: No, but in jism (جسم) there is a meaning of greatness. Do you not see them saying: 'This is a jasim (جسيم) matter,' meaning greatness, not composition, since a matter has no composition because it is an accident. Jarir said, addressing Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz: 'You bore a great matter and endured it and proceeded in it by the command of God...' And it is said: 'The jasim matters are mixed with hardships.' We say: This is used metaphorically, likening matters that are great to bodies that have many parts, for this is an intensive form indicating multiplicity in composition. Such usage is not permissible for God. Moreover, what is used in this sense is the word jasim (جسيم), not the word jism (جسم). They never used this word (jism) for the meaning of glorification. So you have agreed with us on the impossibility of the word jism among His names. So what is used for glorification, you do not permit its application, and what you permit to apply is never used for glorification. Thus the inference is invalid, and God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that jism implies greatness. The objectors cite 'amr jasim' as evidence. The author responds that 'amr jasim' is a metaphorical usage, likening a great matter to a body with many parts. The intensive form 'jasim' is used, not 'jism' itself. Furthermore, 'jism' has never been used for glorification, so the objectors have inadvertently conceded that 'jism' is not a name of glorification. The argument is self-defeating.

جسيم
مجاز
مبالغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The expression 'amr jasim' proves that the word 'jism' itself can mean greatness.
Correction: The word used in the expression is 'jasim' (a metaphorical intensive form), not 'jism'. 'Jism' is never used for glorification.

Checkpoint
What word is used metaphorically for greatness in the phrase 'amr jasim'?
A) Jism
B) Jasim
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The inference from 'amr jasim' fails because jasim is metaphorical and not synonymous with jism, so jism does not imply greatness.

Why God Is Called a Thing but Not a Body
Answers why God is called 'thing' but not 'body' by examining meaning, revelation, and consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theologians affirm that God is a 'thing' but deny that He is a 'body', based on meaning, scripture, and consensus.

Core Idea
Calling God a 'thing' is affirmed by meaning, revelation, and consensus; calling Him a 'body' is refuted by all three.

This passage presents a sharp objection: if you can say 'a thing unlike things', why not 'a body unlike bodies'? The author shows this is a misunderstanding. By examining the meaning of each term, what the Qur'an says, and what the early community agreed upon, he demonstrates that the two cases are not parallel. The lesson illustrates how kalam reasoning uses multiple criteria to evaluate theological language.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial reply
Notice how the author first dismisses the objection as arbitrary (taḥakkum) before giving a substantive answer.
2
Examine the three criteria for 'thing'
The author analyzes the meaning (maʿnā), revelation (sharʿ), and consensus (ijmāʿ) to show that calling God a 'thing' is valid. Understand each criterion.
Pay attention to the analogy about the horse.
3
Apply the same criteria to 'body'
Now see how each criterion leads to the opposite conclusion for 'body': impossible meaning, no scriptural basis, no consensus. This asymmetry is the key point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إذا قلتم: إنه شيء لا كالأشياء فهلا تقولون إنه جسم لا كالأجسام؟ قلنا: هذا تحكم لا معارضة، وأول ما يجب عنه أن يقال: إنما أقول ذلك لأنه شيء لا كالأشياء بجسم لا كالأجسام، وهذا لأنا إن نظرنا إلى معنى الشيء وهو الموجود فإنه لا يقتضي إلا مطلق الوجود فالله تعالى موجود، وإن نظرنا إلى ورود الشرع فقد ورد بقوله تعالى: ﴿ قُلْ أَيُّ شَيْءٍ أَكْبَرُ شَهَدَةٌ قُلِ اللهُ ﴾ ولو لم يكن شيئا لم يكن لقوله معنى وفائدة ولكان لغوا من الكلام كما لو قيل: أي السباع أسرع مشيا؟ فقال قائل: الفرس. كان مخطئا في كلامه لما أن الفرس ليس من جملة السباع، ولما صح ههنا قوله تعالى: ﴿ قُلِ الله ﴾ علم أنه شيء وإنا نظرنا إلى إجماع المسلمين فوجدنا الإجماع قبل إبداع جهم قوله: إنه تعالى لا يقال له شيء منعقدا وخلافه العاري عن الدليل لا يقدح في الإجماع السابق المنعقد عن الدليل، فأما في الجسم فإن نظرنا إلى المعنى وهو التركب وجدناه محالا على القديم، وإن نظرنا إلى الشرع والتوقيف فهو منعدم، وإن نظرنا إلى الإجماع فهو غير منعقد.
If it is said: When you say that He is a thing unlike things, why do you not say that He is a body unlike bodies? We say: This is an arbitrary imposition, not a counter-argument. The first thing that must be said in response is that I only say that because He is a thing unlike things, not a body unlike bodies. This is because if we look at the meaning of 'thing' – which is the existent – it does not necessitate except absolute existence, so God Almighty is existent. And if we look at the coming of the Law, it has come in His statement: 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: God' (Qur'an 6:19). If He were not a thing, His statement would have no meaning or benefit and would be idle speech, like if one said: 'Which beast of prey is fastest in walking?' and someone answered: 'The horse.' He would be mistaken in his speech because the horse is not among the beasts of prey. And since here His statement 'Say: God' is valid, it is known that He is a thing. And if we look at the consensus of the Muslims, we find that consensus was established before Jahm invented his saying that the Exalted is not called a thing, and opposition devoid of evidence does not invalidate the previous consensus established on evidence. As for the body, if we look at its meaning – which is composition – we find it impossible for the Eternal. If we look at the Law and revelation, it is absent. And if we look at consensus, it is not established.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent asks: if you allow calling God 'a thing unlike things', why not call Him 'a body unlike bodies'? The author first dismisses this as arbitrary (taḥakkum), not a genuine counter-argument. He then gives a structured answer examining three criteria: meaning (maʿnā), revealed scripture (sharʿ/tawqīf), and consensus (ijmāʿ). For 'thing' (shayʾ), its meaning is 'existent' (mawjūd), which applies to God. Scripture confirms it in the Qur'anic verse where God is called a 'thing'. Consensus before Jahm ibn Ṣafwān affirmed that God is called a 'thing'; later opposition is baseless. For 'body' (jism), its meaning is 'composition' (tarkīb), which is impossible for the Eternal. No revealed text uses it for God, and there is no consensus. Thus the two terms are not parallel. The author uses an analogy: like calling a horse a 'beast of prey' would be a category error, so calling God a 'body' would be an error while calling Him a 'thing' is correct.

Key Terms
شيء (shayʾ)
موجود (mawjūd)
تركب (tarkub)
توقيف (tawqīf)
إجماع (ijmāʿ)
جهم (Jahm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God can be called a 'thing unlike things', He can also be called a 'body unlike bodies' because both are analogical affirmations.
Correction: The author shows that the two terms are not analogous: 'thing' means existent, which is true of God, while 'body' means composed, which is impossible for Him. Each term must be evaluated on its own meaning, scriptural evidence, and consensus.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is calling God 'a thing unlike things' permissible but calling Him 'a body unlike bodies' is not?
A) Because 'thing' means existent, which is true of God, while 'body' means composed, which is impossible for God.
B) Because the Qur'an calls God a body.
C) Because there is consensus that God is a body.
D) Because 'thing' is a negative attribute while 'body' is positive.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of calling God a 'thing' does not entail permission to call Him a 'body' because the two terms differ fundamentally in meaning, scriptural support, and consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah, do you ever use words or descriptions that you have not verified by meaning, scripture, or consensus? What will you do today to examine one such term you habitually use?

The author argues that an innovation (like denying that Allah is a 'thing') does not break a prior consensus established on evidence. How will you check it against the three criteria?

Will you commit to learning the proper terms from revelation before speaking about Allah today?

The Validity of Calling God 'Thing'
Defense of the term 'thing' for God using meaning, Quran, and consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'thing' (shay’) is legitimately applied to Allah based on its meaning, scriptural evidence, and consensus.

Core Idea
'Thing' means existent, not body: a crucial distinction in Islamic theology.

The objection that calling God a 'thing' should imply calling Him a 'body' is based on a false analogy. In this passage, the author demonstrates that the term 'thing' has a solid foundation in language, revelation, and the consensus of early Muslims. We will explore why 'thing' is a safe and necessary attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the objection and the author's first response ('This is arbitrary, not a rebuttal'). Notice how the author immediately rejects the analogy.
2
Examine the argument from the meaning of 'thing'
The author explains that 'thing' simply means 'existent', which applies to Allah. This is the linguistic basis.
3
See the Quranic proof and consensus evidence
The author cites Quran 6:19 and the early consensus of Muslims to further support calling God a 'thing'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إذا قلتم: إنه شيء لا كالأشياء فهلا تقولون إنه جسم لا كالأجسام؟ قلنا: هذا تحكم لا معارضة، وأول ما يجب عنه أن يقال: إنما أقول ذلك لأنه شيء لا كالأشياء بجسم لا كالأجسام، وهذا لأنا إن نظرنا إلى معنى الشيء وهو الموجود فإنه لا يقتضي إلا مطلق الوجود فالله تعالى موجود، وإن نظرنا إلى ورود الشرع فقد ورد بقوله تعالى: {قُلْ أَيُّ شَيْءٍ أَكْبَرُ شَهَادَةً قُلِ اللَّهُ} ولو لم يكن شيئا لم يكن لقوله معنى وفائدة ولكان لغوا من الكلام كما لو قيل: أي السباع أسرع مشيا؟ فقال قائل: الفرس. كان مخطئا في كلامه لما أن الفرس ليس من جملة السباع، ولما صح ههنا قوله تعالى: {قُلِ اللَّهُ} علم أنه شيء، وإنا نظرنا إلى إجماع المسلمين فوجدنا الإجماع قبل إبداع جهم قوله: إنه تعالى لا يقال له شيء - منعقدا وخلافه العاري عن الدليل لا يقدح في الإجماع السابق المنعقد عن الدليل، فأما في الجسم فإن نظرنا إلى المعنى وهو التركب وجدناه محالا على القديم، وإن نظرنا إلى الشرع والتوقيف فهو منعدم، وإن نظرنا إلى الإجماع فهو غير منعقد، وإثبات ما هذا سبيله بإثبات ما ذاك سبيله محال ممتنع وجهل بالمقايسة.
If it is said: When you say that He is a thing not like things, why do you not say that He is a body not like bodies? We say: This is arbitrary, not a rebuttal. And the first thing that must be said in response is: I only say that because He is a thing not like things, a body not like bodies. This is because if we consider the meaning of 'thing', which is the existent, it necessitates only absolute existence, so Allah Most High is existent. And if we consider the coming of revelation, it has come in His statement: 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: Allah' (Quran 6:19). And if He were not a thing, this statement would have no meaning or benefit, and it would be vain speech, like if it were said: 'Which beast of prey is fastest in running?' and someone said: 'The horse,' he would be mistaken in his speech because the horse is not among the beasts of prey. And since here the statement of Allah 'Say: Allah' is correct, it is known that He is a thing. And if we consider the consensus of the Muslims, we find that consensus before the innovation of Jahm's statement that He cannot be called a thing was established, and his contrary opinion devoid of proof does not harm the previous consensus established by proof. As for the body: if we consider the meaning, which is composition, we find it impossible for the Eternal; if we consider revelation and specification, it is absent; and if we consider consensus, it is not established. And to affirm that which is in this state by affirming that which is in that state is impossible, prohibited, and ignorance of analogy.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent argues: since you say God is a 'thing not like things', why not also say He is a 'body not like bodies'? The author responds that this is a forced analogy ('تحكم') and then gives a detailed refutation. First, he explains that the term 'thing' simply means 'existent' and is used in the Quran (6:19) and by early consensus, so it is valid. In contrast, 'body' implies composition, which is impossible for God; it has no scriptural basis and no consensus. Therefore, the two are not parallel. The author also provides an illustration: if someone answers 'horse' to a question about beasts of prey, it would be wrong because a horse is not a beast of prey. Similarly, applying 'body' to God would be mistaken because the meaning of 'body' (composition) does not apply to Him.

Key Terms
شَيْء
إِجْمَاع
تَوْقِيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'thing' implies 'body' and therefore should be used similarly.
Correction: The term 'thing' simply means existent and does not entail composition or embodiment.

Checkpoint
What is the primary meaning of 'thing' (shay’) according to the author?
A) That which has composition
B) The existent
C) A created object
D) A physical entity

Key Takeaway
The term 'thing' is applied to God because it means existent, supported by revelation and consensus, and does not imply body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is a thing', does your heart feel any hesitation? Where does that hesitation come from—from the Quran and the consensus of the righteous, or from your own assumptions? Will you now consciously align your speech with what Allah has revealed and the early Muslims agreed upon?
?
The Jahmites broke away from consensus out of mere speculation. Have you ever rejected a true description of Allah because it felt uncomfortable or clashed with your own reasoning? What would it cost you to submit to the revealed terms, even when they challenge your intellect?
?
If calling Allah a 'thing' is correct because He is the Existent, then your own existence is a gift from Him. Does your life reflect gratitude for that gift, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? How will you today acknowledge that you are wholly dependent on the One who is the only true Existent?

Why God is Not Called 'Body'
Refutation of applying 'body' to God using meaning, revelation, and consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'body' (jism) cannot be applied to God due to its meaning, lack of scriptural evidence, and absence of consensus.

Core Idea
'Body' implies composition, which is impossible for Allah, and has no basis in revelation or consensus.

In contrast to the term 'thing', the term 'body' carries implications of composition that are incompatible with God's nature. The author systematically refutes any attempt to analogize 'body' to 'thing', showing that each criterion used for 'thing' fails for 'body'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of 'body' as composite
The author explains that 'body' implies composition (تركيب), which is impossible for the Eternal God.
2
Note the lack of scriptural support
No revelation or specific tradition (توقيف) uses 'body' for God.
3
Recognize that consensus is absent
Unlike 'thing', there is no consensus (إجماع) that God can be called a body.

Are 'Thing' and 'Body' Synonyms?
Objection and partial answer on synonymy of 'thing' and 'body'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that 'thing' and 'body' are synonyms, and why the distinction matters for divine attributes.

Core Idea
'Thing' means existence; 'body' means composition—they are not the same.

The author now addresses another objection: if you call God a 'thing', doesn't that imply He is a 'body'? The opponents assume the two terms are synonyms. In this passage, we see the beginning of a careful response that distinguishes their actual meanings. Pay attention to how the author turns the tables.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the question-and-answer format.
The passage is incomplete but the core objection is clear.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponents argue that because we use 'thing' for God, we must also call Him 'body' if the two terms are synonyms. Find this in the text.
3
Examine the author's response
The author does not immediately accept the synonymy. Instead, he asks why they assume synonymy, then points out that 'body' means 'composite' while 'thing' means 'existent'.
The argument breaks off, but the direction is clear.
4
Consider the incompleteness
Note that the text is cut off. Try to infer what the author might have said next based on the distinction he made.
This is a good moment to reflect on how kalam arguments are structured.

Al-Jubba'i's Objection: Does an Eternal Attribute Imply Similarity?
Nasafi presents al-Jubba'i's question about divine attributes and similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jubba'i used the definition of divine attributes to challenge the view that God has eternal attributes.

Core Idea
Al-Jubba'i's question seeks to show that an eternal attribute would be similar to God, undermining divine uniqueness.

In this passage, Nasafi introduces further proofs that invalidate the definition of attributes proposed by al-Jubba'i. He then explains the polemical intent behind that definition: to raise a question about the possibility of God having eternal attributes without implying similarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall flow.
Pay attention to the conjunctive 'wa' (and) that connects the two parts.
2
Identify the two parts
Notice that the passage has two main sections: first, a general statement about further proofs; second, an explanation of al-Jubba'i's intent.
3
Understand al-Jubba'i's logic
Trace the argument: God is eternal. The 'self-attribute' is the most specific attribute of the eternal. Existence and thingness are general. According to al-Jubba'i, this self-attribute is shared. Therefore, if God had an eternal attribute, that attribute would be similar to God.
This is a polemical challenge, not a concession.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why this objection is significant: it forces defenders of eternal attributes to explain how attributes are not separate entities that rival God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووراء ما بينا دلائل توجب بطلان هذا التحديد، ومراد الجبائي وابنه من هذا التحديد سؤال لهم علينا في مسألة الصفات : أن الله تعالى لو كان قديما، وهذا الوصف وصف نفسي، وهو أخص الصفات للقديم لما أن الموجود والشيء وما وراء ذلك أوصاف عامة وعلى قول الجبائي هو صفته النفسية فلو كان الله تعالى صفة قديمة لكانت مثلا الله تعالى.
And beyond what we have shown are proofs that necessitate the falsity of this definition. And the intent of al-Jubba'i and his son from this definition is a question for them against us in the issue of the attributes: that if God Almighty were eternal, and this description is a self-description, and it is the most specific attribute of the eternal, because 'existent' and 'thing' and what is beyond that are general attributes, and according to al-Jubba'i it is His self-attribute, then if God Almighty were an eternal attribute, it would be similar to God Almighty.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage introduces that there are further proofs (dala'il) invalidating the definition discussed earlier. It then explains the purpose (murad) of al-Jubba'i and his son (Abu Hashim) in proposing that definition: they intended to pose a question against the author's school on the topic of divine attributes. The question is: if God is eternal (qadim), and this description is a self-attribute (sifa nafsiyya) and the most specific attribute of the eternal, since existence and thingness are general attributes, then according to al-Jubba'i this self-attribute would be shared. Thus, if God had an eternal attribute (like His attribute of eternity), that attribute would be similar to God, implying multiplicity or similarity in the divine. This is a potential objection against the Ash'ari/Maturidi view that God has eternal attributes.

Key Terms
دلائل
القديم
الصفات النفسية
أخص الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think al-Jubba'i's question is a concession that attributes are similar to God.
Correction: In fact, it is a polemical challenge: if you affirm eternal attributes, then you must face the conclusion that an eternal attribute would be similar to God, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
What is the intent of al-Jubba'i's definition according to this passage?
A) To show that God has no attributes.
B) To argue that an eternal attribute would be similar to God.
C) To prove that attributes are eternal.
D) To define the self-attribute of God.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's definition of divine attributes was a polemical tool to argue that eternal attributes would compromise God's uniqueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate God's attributes, do you ever unconsciously imagine them as separate 'things' alongside God? How does al-Jubba'i's objection force you to examine whether your mental picture of God preserves His absolute uniqueness?
?
