Invalidation of a Definition and Contingent Similarity
Nasafi invalidates a definition and presents Al-Najjar's view on contingent similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rejection of a definition that denies divine attributes, and the view of Al-Najjar and Al-Qalanisi on the similarity of contingent things.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are not separate entities; contingent things share similarity in their contingency.

In this passage, Nasafi concludes his refutation of a definition proposed by al-Jubba'i, clarifying that divine attributes are not 'other' to God. He then introduces an alternative perspective from Al-Najjar and Al-Qalanisi, who argue that contingent things are similar in being contingent. Understanding this debate is key to grasping the Maturidi position on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the flow from refutation to a new viewpoint.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'aghyar' and 'al-muhdathayn'.
2
Identify the refutation conclusion
Find where Nasafi states that invalidating the definition makes the question invalid and that similarity applies to two groups. Understand what the 'two groups' likely refer to (divine attributes and created things).
3
Examine the nature of divine attributes
Note the claim that divine attributes are not 'aghyar' (others) to God. This means they are not separate entities but inherent to His essence.
This is a key Ash'ari/Maturidi position against those who deny attributes.
4
Understand the proof structure
Nasafi says necessary proofs show that God has eternal attributes, He is eternal, and no attribute is similar to Him. This establishes the falsity of the definition.
He promises to clarify further in the attributes section.
5
Analyze the view of al-Najjar and al-Qalanisi
Read their statement: two contingent things resemble each other in being contingent, even if they differ in other attributes. Reflect on why this is significant.
Consider why Maturidi sometimes inclined to this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا أبطلنا عليهم هذا الحد بطل السؤال على أن المماثلة تجري بين الفريقين وصفات الله تعالى ليست أغيارا له على أنا إذا بينا بالدلائل الضرورية أن الله تعالى صفات أزلية، وأنه تعالى قديم، وأنه لا تماثله الصفة ظهر بثبوت هذه المقدمات بطلان تحديدهم ونبين حقيقة هذا في مسألة الصفات. إذا انتهينا إليها إن شاء الله تعالى - وكان الحسين بن محمد النجار البصري رئيس النجارية، وأبو العباس أحمد بن إبراهيم القلانسي الرازي من متكلمي أهل الحديث يقولان: إن المحدثين يشتبهان في الحديث من حيث هما محدثان وإن اختلفا بعد ذلك في أوصاف سوى الحديث، والشيخ الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي - رحمه الله - ربما يميل إلى هذا في خلال كلامه
So if we invalidate this definition for them, the question becomes invalid, and similarity occurs between the two groups, and the attributes of God Almighty are not other (aghyar) to Him. And if we show by necessary proofs that God Almighty has eternal attributes, and that He is eternal, and that no attribute is similar to Him, then by the establishment of these premises the falsity of their definition appears. And we will explain the truth of this in the issue of attributes when we reach it, God willing. And al-Husayn ibn Muhammad al-Najjar al-Basri, the chief of the Najjariyya, and Abu al-Abbas Ahmad ibn Ibrahim al-Qalanisi al-Razi, from the theologians of the people of hadith, used to say: that two contingent things resemble each other in contingency (al-hadath) in that they are contingent, even if they differ afterward in attributes other than contingency. And the Shaykh Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi - may God have mercy on him - sometimes inclined to this in the course of his speech.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage begins by concluding that once the definition of al-Jubba'i and his son is refuted, their question (about divine attributes being similar) becomes invalid. The author asserts that similarity (mumathala) applies between two groups (perhaps created things and attributes), but divine attributes are not other (aghyar) to God; they are not separate entities. Through necessary proofs, it is established that God has eternal attributes, that He is eternal, and that no attribute is similar to Him, thus refuting their definition. The author promises to clarify this in the section on attributes. The passage then presents the views of al-Najjar and al-Qalanisi: they held that two contingent (muhdath) things are similar in their contingency (al-hadath) but may differ in other attributes. Imam al-Maturidi sometimes leaned toward this view.

Key Terms
المماثلة
الفريقين
أغيار
المحدثين
الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'attributes are not other' means attributes are identical to God (God = His attributes).
Correction: The phrase means attributes are not separate entities from God; they are not 'others' with independent existence. They are inherent in His essence without being identical or separate.

Checkpoint
According to al-Najjar and al-Qalanisi, how are two contingent things similar?
A) They are similar in all attributes.
B) They are similar in being contingent.
C) They are similar in being eternal.
D) They are similar in being substances.

Key Takeaway
Contingent things share similarity in their contingency, but divine attributes are not separate from God and are incomparable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mentally treated God's attributes as if they are separate beings or partners with Him in your understanding of tawhid? How does Nasafi's assertion that they are 'not other' challenge you to purify your belief, and what specific step will you take today to deepen your conviction in God's oneness?
?
You are a contingent being, sharing only the property of contingency with all created things. Yet you often act as if your achievements, status, or personality make you independent. In what area of your life do you most deny your total dependence on Allah, and how will you actively remind yourself of your contingency in that area this week?
?
The passage warns against any similarity between the Creator and creation. Reflect on a time you imagined God in human-like terms—perhaps with emotions or physical actions. How did that distort your worship? What concrete action will you take to replace that imagery with the pure understanding that 'nothing is like Him'?

Three Views on Similarity and Divine Attributes
Nasafi reports three positions on similarity and early philosophers' negative theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three different theological positions on similarity (tamathul) of substances and accidents, and the early philosophers' approach to divine attributes.

Core Idea
Similarity and divine naming reveal deeper debates about attributes.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla presents three distinct views on what makes things similar. Understanding these positions is crucial because they shape how later theologians talk about God's attributes. You will see al-Qalanisi's and al-Maturidi's nuanced differences on substances and accidents, and then the radical negative theology of early philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the three positions carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and translation to get the flow. Note that the author reports three separate views: al-Qalanisi, al-Maturidi, and al-awa'il (early philosophers).
2
Identify the key difference on accidents
Both agree substances are similar by a meaning, but on accidents al-Qalanisi says similarity is not by themselves nor by a meaning, while al-Maturidi says it is by a meaning that returns to their essences. What does this distinction imply?
Al-Maturidi's view allows accidents to have a real basis for similarity.
3
Understand the early philosophers' view
They restrict divine attributes to negations. Instead of 'He is existent' they say 'He is not non-existent'. Reflect on how this differs from the positive language of kalam.
This is a radical apophatic theology.
4
Connect to the larger attribute debate
The passage ends with a promise to clarify later. Consider why these views on similarity matter for understanding God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان القلانسي يقول : إن المتماثلين من الجواهر يتماثلان بمعنى يقوم بهما، والمتماثلان من الأعراض متماثلان لا لأنفسهما ولا لمعنى، والشيخ الإمام أبو منصور - رحمه الله - كان يقول : الجوهران يتماثلان بمعنى يقوم بهما، والمتماثلان من الأعراض يتماثلان بمعنى يرجع إلى ذاتهما، ونص عليه في كتابه المسمى بمآخذ الشرائع، وقال: عند الناس صفات الأعراض بأنفسها، وصفات الأعيان تكون باعتبار يحل بها، وسنكشف عن حقيقة هذا الكلام إذا انتهينا إلى مسألة الصفات إن شاء الله تعالى - وذهب كثير من الأوائل المنتسبين إلى الفلسفة أن التشابه يقع بالاشتراك في أوصاف الإثبات دون السلب، وزعموا أنه لا يطلق على الباري جل وعلا من الأسماء والأوصاف إلا ما طريقه السلب دون الإيجاب فقالوا: لا نقول إنه موجود بل نقول: إنه ليس معدوم
And al-Qalanisi used to say: that two similar substances are similar by a meaning that subsists in them, and two similar accidents are similar not by themselves nor by a meaning. And the Shaykh Imam Abu Mansur - may God have mercy on him - used to say: two substances are similar by a meaning that subsists in them, and two similar accidents are similar by a meaning that returns to their essences. And he stated this in his book called Ma'akhidh al-Shara'i', and he said: according to people, the attributes of accidents are by themselves, and the attributes of entities are by consideration of what inheres in them. And we will uncover the truth of this statement when we reach the issue of attributes, God willing. And many of the early philosophers (al-awa'il) went to the view that similarity occurs by sharing in positive attributes, not negative ones. And they claimed that nothing is attributed to the Blessed and Exalted of names and attributes except what is by way of negation, not affirmation. So they said: we do not say He is existent, rather we say: He is not non-existent.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct positions on similarity (tamathul). First, al-Qalanisi's view: two similar substances (jawahir) are similar by a meaning (ma'na) that subsists in them, while two similar accidents (a'rad) are similar neither by themselves nor by a meaning (i.e., they are similar by themselves). Second, Imam al-Maturidi's view: two substances are similar by a meaning subsisting in them, but two similar accidents are similar by a meaning that goes back to their essences (dhat). Al-Maturidi recorded this in his book Ma'akhidh al-Shara'i', adding that people consider attributes of accidents as by themselves, and attributes of entities (a'yan) as by consideration of what inheres in them. The author promises to clarify this later. Third, the view of early philosophers (al-awa'il al-muntasibin ila al-falsafa): similarity occurs by sharing positive (ithbat) attributes, not negative (salbi). They restricted divine names and attributes to negative expressions, e.g., they said 'He is not non-existent' rather than 'He is existent'.

Key Terms
الجوهر
العرض
التشابه
أوصاف الإثبات
أوصاف السلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Maturidi thought that two similar accidents are similar by themselves, like al-Qalanisi's view.
Correction: Al-Maturidi held that two similar accidents are similar by a meaning that returns to their essences, not by themselves.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maturidi, how are two similar accidents similar?
A) By themselves.
B) By a meaning subsisting in them.
C) By a meaning that returns to their essences.
D) Not by themselves nor by a meaning.

Key Takeaway
The basis of similarity (tamathul) was debated among theologians and philosophers, with implications for how we speak about God's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God in your prayers or daily speech, do you lean more toward saying what He is not instead of what He is? What does that reveal about your comfort with His transcendence versus His closeness? Commit to using one positive attribute of God consciously today.
?
The early philosophers avoided calling God 'existent' for fear of similarity with creation. Have you ever diluted your affirmation of God's attributes to avoid seeming anthropomorphic? How might that caution actually diminish your worship? This week, pray to God using tangible names like 'The Hearing' or 'The Seeing' while actively reminding yourself He hears and sees without being like creation.
?
Al-Maturidi made a distinction between how similarity works in created things and how we must affirm divine attributes without similarity. In your own thinking, do you ever treat God's attributes as if they are just bigger versions of human traits? What step can you take today to purify your understanding of one of God's names?

Invalidating a False Definition of Similarity
Al-Nasafi invalidates a definition of similarity and introduces key premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes a definition of similarity that would imply similarity between Allah and His attributes, and why this is foundational for tawhid.

Core Idea
No similarity between Creator and creation.

A false definition of similarity could lead to anthropomorphism. Al-Nasafi shows that by proving God's attributes are eternal and not distinct from His essence, any definition implying similarity fails, postponing full detail to the discussion of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the argument flow.
Notice the opening 'Since we have invalidated...' sets the stage.
2
Identify the target definition
The passage refers to a definition of similarity that has been shown invalid. Understand that the objection based on it is now moot.
The definition likely implied that similarity could exist between God and His attributes or between God and creation.
3
Examine the three premises
Al-Nasafi lists: God has eternal attributes, God is eternal, no attribute is similar to Him. These premises together make the false definition impossible.
These premises establish that divine attributes are not 'other' in a way that would allow similarity.
4
Recognize the deferral
The passage ends with a deferral to the future discussion of attributes. This indicates careful, step-by-step exposition.
This is a common pedagogical technique in kalam works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا أبطلنا عليهم هذا الحد بطل السؤال على أن المماثلة تجري بين الفريقين وصفات الله تعالى ليست أغيارا له على أنا إذا بينا بالدلائل الضرورية أن الله تعالى صفات أزلية، وأنه تعالى قديم، وأنه لا تماثله الصفة ظهر بثبوت هذه المقدمات بطلان تحديدهم ونبين حقيقة هذا في مسألة الصفات. إذا انتهينا إليها إن شاء الله تعالى - وكان الحسين بن محمد النجار البصري رئيس النجارية، وأبو العباس أحمد بن إبراهيم القلانسي الرازي من متكلمي أهل الحديث يقولان: إن المحدثين يشتبهان في الحديث من حيث هما محدثان وإن اختلفا بعد ذلك في أوصاف سوى الحديث، والشيخ الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي - رحمه الله - ربما يميل إلى هذا في خلال كلامه وكان القلانسي يقول : إن المتماثلين من الجواهر يتماثلان بمعنى يقوم بهما، والمتماثلان من الأعراض متماثلان لا لأنفسهما ولا لمعنى، والشيخ الإمام أبو منصور - رحمه الله - كان يقول : الجوهران يتماثلان بمعنى يقوم بهما، والمتماثلان من الأعراض يتماثلان بمعنى يرجع إلى ذاتهما، ونص عليه في كتابه المسمى بمآخذ الشرائع، وقال: عند الناس صفات الأعراض بأنفسها، وصفات الأعيان تكون باعتبار يحل بها، وسنكشف عن حقيقة هذا الكلام إذا انتهينا إلى مسألة الصفات إن شاء الله تعالى -
Since we have invalidated this definition for them, the question falls away: that similarity (tamathul) occurs between the two groups, and the attributes of God Most High are not other than Him; and that when we demonstrate by necessary proofs that God Most High has eternal attributes, that He is eternal, and that no attribute is similar to Him, the invalidity of their definition becomes clear with the establishment of these premises. We will explain the reality of this in the discussion of attributes when we reach it, God willing. And al-Husayn ibn Muhammad al-Najjar al-Basri, the leader of the Najjariyya, and Abu al-Abbas Ahmad ibn Ibrahim al-Qalanisi al-Razi, among the mutakallimun of the People of Hadith, say: The two originated entities resemble each other in being originated, even if they differ thereafter in other descriptions besides origination. And the Shaykh Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi—may God have mercy on him—sometimes inclines to this in his discourse. Al-Qalanisi used to say: Two similar substances are similar by a meaning subsisting in them, while two similar accidents are similar neither by themselves nor by a meaning. And the Shaykh Imam Abu Mansur—may God have mercy on him—used to say: Two substances are similar by a meaning subsisting in them, and two similar accidents are similar by a meaning referring to their essences. He stated this in his book called Ma'akhidh al-Shara'i', and said: According to people, the attributes of accidents are [predicated] by themselves, while the attributes of substances are [predicated] by a consideration of what inheres in them. We will reveal the reality of this statement when we reach the discussion of attributes, God willing.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the concept of similarity (tamathul) as it applies to divine attributes and created entities. The author first states that by invalidating a certain definition of similarity, the objection based on that definition is disproven. He then asserts that God's attributes are not distinct from His essence, and that with necessary proofs showing God's eternal attributes, His eternity, and the fact that no attribute is similar to Him, the invalidity of the opponents' definition is evident. The author postpones full explication to the discussion of attributes. Next, he presents two views on similarity among created entities: al-Najjar and al-Qalanisi hold that originated things are similar only in being originated, differing otherwise. Al-Qalanisi further distinguishes: similar substances share a meaning (e.g., a common essential property), while similar accidents are similar neither by themselves nor by a meaning. Al-Maturidi, however, holds that both substances and accidents are similar by a meaning: substances by a meaning subsisting in them, accidents by a meaning referring to their essences. He cites his book Ma'akhidh al-Shara'i' to emphasize that attributes of accidents are predicated directly, while attributes of substances are predicated by consideration of something that inheres. The discussion is deferred to the chapter on attributes.

مماثلة
صفة

D) Similarity exists between substances and accidents

Key Takeaway
Similarity between Allah and His attributes is impossible because Allah and His attributes are not distinct in a way that would allow comparison.

Views on Similarity Among Created Entities
Al-Nasafi presents two views on similarity of substance and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing views of al-Najjar, al-Qalanisi, and al-Maturidi on what makes two created entities similar, especially regarding substances and accidents.

Core Idea
Similarity in creation is not superficial; it is grounded in meanings.

After establishing that similarity cannot apply to Allah, al-Nasafi presents a debate on how similarity applies to contingent things. The key question: do substances and accidents share similarity by themselves or by a meaning?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the names and their positions.
Al-Najjar and al-Qalanisi are from different theological schools.
2
Contrast the two main views
Al-Najjar and al-Qalanisi hold that originated things are similar only in being originated. Al-Maturidi inclines differently. Identify the difference.
Al-Maturidi's view is nuanced about substances and accidents.
3
Analyze al-Qalanisi's distinction
Al-Qalanisi says substances are similar by a meaning subsisting in them, but accidents are similar neither by themselves nor by a meaning. Understand why he distinguishes.
This implies accidents have no intrinsic essence to ground similarity.
4
Analyze al-Maturidi's position
Al-Maturidi holds both substances and accidents are similar by a meaning: for substances it subsists in them, for accidents it refers to their essences.
His principle: attributes of accidents are by themselves, attributes of substances by inherence.
5
Connect to the broader theme
This debate ties into how attributes are predicated. The passage defers further explanation to the attributes discussion.
The deferral shows the interconnectedness of kalam topics.

The Necessity of Affirming Divine Attributes: Refuting Philosophers and Batiniyya
Al-Nasafi refutes those who restrict or deny divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why both positive and negative attributes must be affirmed for Allah, and why denying them leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
Denying Allah's positive attributes is not reverence but rational absurdity.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi confronts two extreme positions on divine attributes: philosophers who only use negative descriptions, and a sect that denies all descriptions altogether. As you read, notice how the author labels these views as 'delirium' and traces them back to sophistry. The lesson will unpack why the middle path of tanzih requires both affirmation of perfection and negation of imperfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the author's strong language: 'delirium' and 'sophistry'.
2
Identify the two groups
Find the philosophers' view (only negative attributes) and the Batiniyya's view (no attributes at all). Note the difference between them.
The philosophers avoid positive attributes; the Batiniyya avoid even negative ones.
3
Understand the author's condemnation
Al-Nasafi calls both views 'delirium' and links the second to sophistry. Reflect on why he considers them irrational.
He asserts that no intellect accepts a middle state between affirmation and negation.
4
Apply the principle
Conclude that affirming divine attributes is necessary for rational theology. Denying them leads to self-contradiction.
This lesson affirms the balanced approach of Ahl al-Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب كثير من الأوائل المنتسبين إلى الفلسفة أن التشابه يقع بالاشتراك في أوصاف الإثبات دون السلب، وزعموا أنه لا يطلق على الباري جل وعلا من الأسماء والأوصاف إلا ما طريقه السلب دون الإيجاب فقالوا: لا نقول إنه موجود بل نقول: إنه ليس بمعدوم ولا نقول: إنه حي عالم قادر، ولكن نقول ليس بميت ولا جاهل، ولا عاجز، وساعدهم على هذا الهذيان بعض الباطنية، وزعم بعضهم أنه تعالى لا يوصف أيضا بشيء من أوصاف النفي، وقالوا كما أن من قال: إنه موجود شبهه بالموجودات، ومن قال : إنه ليس بموجود شبهه بالمعدوم، فلا يقولون إنه شيء ولا أنه لا شيء ولا أنه جسم ولا أنه لا جسم، وهذا كله هذيان لا يقبله عقل ولا يستحله طبع، وهو خروج عن المعارف والتحاق بالمتجاهلة من السوفسطائية حيث أثبتوا وساطة بين السلب والإيجاب، والوجود والعدم.
Many of the ancients affiliated with philosophy held that similarity occurs by participation in positive descriptions, not negative ones, and they claimed that only names and descriptions that are negative are applied to the Blessed and Exalted, not positive ones. They said: We do not say He is existent, but rather we say He is not non-existent; we do not say He is living, knowing, powerful, but rather we say He is not dead, not ignorant, not powerless. Some of the Batiniyya aided them in this delirium, and some of them claimed that He, exalted, is not described by any negative descriptions either, saying: Just as he who says He is existent likens Him to existents, and he who says He is not existent likens Him to the non-existent, so they do not say He is a thing nor that He is not a thing, nor that He is a body nor that He is not a body. All of this is delirium that no intellect accepts and no nature deems lawful, and it is a departure from knowledge and an attachment to the willfully ignorant of the Sophists, who affirmed an intermediary between negation and affirmation, existence and non-existence.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two groups: philosophers who restrict divine attributes to negative ones, and a subgroup of the Batiniyya who even deny negative attributes. The author first describes the philosophers' position: they claim similarity between God and creation only occurs in positive attributes, so to avoid similarity, they only apply negative descriptions—thus they say He is not non-existent, not dead, etc., but refuse to say He is existent, living, etc. The author calls this 'delirium' (هذيان). Then he mentions that some Batiniyya went further, denying even negative attributes, arguing that any description (positive or negative) implies similarity. So they refuse to say He is a thing or not a thing, a body or not a body. The author declares this unacceptable to reason and nature, labeling it as sophistry (سوفسطائية) because it posits a middle state between affirmation and negation, which is impossible. Thus, the passage defends the necessity of affirming positive attributes for God while negating imperfections, and rejects any attempt to completely strip God of attributes.

Key Terms
صفات إثبات
صفات سلب
باطنية
سوفسطائية
هذيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is more respectful to avoid describing God with positive attributes to prevent anthropomorphism.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that this leads to denying God's perfections and is irrational. The correct approach is to affirm positive attributes while negating their imperfections (tanzih without ta'til).

Checkpoint
What do the philosophers mentioned in the passage say about saying 'God is existent'?
A) They affirm it directly
B) They say 'He is not non-existent' instead
C) They remain silent
D) They say 'He is neither existent nor non-existent'

Key Takeaway
Affirming divine attributes is necessary; denying them leads to contradictions and sophistry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that it is more respectful to avoid describing Allah out of fear of likening Him to creation? How does this passage expose that as a form of cowardice rather than reverence? What will you change in your heart as a result?
?
The Batiniyya refused to say Allah is 'existent' or 'not existent' for fear of similarity. Are there areas in your life where you remain silent about Allah's attributes because you are afraid of saying something wrong? How does this passage call you to confidently affirm what Allah has affirmed of Himself?
?
If affirming positive attributes is necessary, does your own understanding of Allah's life, knowledge, and power affect how you live? For example, do you truly live as if Allah is all-powerful and all-knowing? What specific action can you take today to demonstrate that belief?

Refuting the Batiniyya on Divine Attributes
Nasafi refutes the Batiniyya and extreme negationists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi rejects the Batiniyya's extreme position of refusing both positive and negative descriptions of Allah, and how this constitutes sophistry.

Core Idea
Denying all attributes about Allah is not tawhid but sophistry.

Imam Nasafi presents and refutes an extreme theological position. Some Batiniyya argued that even negating attributes (like 'not dead') implies similarity. Nasafi shows that this leads to absurdity and is a departure from sound knowledge, comparing it to sophism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the progression from orthodox view to Batiniyya to extreme view to refutation.
2
Identify the three positions
The passage presents three views: the orthodox view (affirming attributes and negating opposites), the Batiniyya view (only negations), and the extreme view (refusing even negations). Find each one.
The extreme view is the main target of the refutation.
3
Understand the refutation
Nasafi calls the extreme view 'hadyan' (nonsense) and says it rejects intellect and nature. He compares them to sophists who posit a middle state between affirmation and negation. Ask yourself why this is a problem.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Reflect on the key terms
Look at the key terms: هذيان, سوفسطائية, السلب والإيجاب, الوجود والعدم. How do they encapsulate Nasafi's criticism?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا نقول: إنه حي عالم قادر، ولكن نقول ليس بميت ولا جاهل ولا عاجز، وساعدهم على هذا الهذيان بعض الباطنية، وزعم بعضهم أنه تعالى لا يوصف أيضا بشيء من أوصاف النفي، وقالوا كما أن من قال: إنه موجود شبهه بالموجودات، ومن قال: إنه ليس بموجود شبهه بالمعدوم، فلا يقولون إنه شيء ولا أنه لا شيء، ولا أنه جسم ولا أنه لا جسم، وهذا كله هذيان لا يقبله عقل ولا يستحله طبع، وهو خروج عن المعارف والتحاق بالمتجاهلة من السوفسطائية حيث أثبتوا وساطة بين السلب والإيجاب، والوجود والعدم.
And we do not say: He is living, knowing, powerful; but we say: not dead, not ignorant, not powerless. And some of the Batiniyya aided them in this nonsense. And some of them claimed that He, exalted, is not described by any of the attributes of negation either. And they said: just as he who says 'He exists' resembles Him to existents, and he who says 'He is not existent' resembles Him to non-existent, so they do not say that He is a thing nor that He is not a thing, nor that He is a body nor that He is not a body. And all of this is nonsense that no intellect accepts, nor does nature deem permissible, and it is a departure from knowledge and an attachment to the ignorant sophists, as they affirmed an intermediary between negation and affirmation, and between existence and non-existence.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an extreme position held by some Batiniyya who refuse to describe God with either positive or negative attributes. Imam Nasafi begins by stating the orthodox position: we affirm positive attributes like living, knowing, and powerful, but we negate their opposites (not dead, etc.). He then describes the Batiniyya's extreme view: they argue that even saying 'existent' implies similarity to existents, and 'not existent' implies similarity to non-existents, so they end up saying nothing at all—neither that He is a thing nor not a thing, neither a body nor not a body. Nasafi dismisses this as هذيان (nonsense) that is unacceptable to intellect and nature. He compares them to sophists who posit a middle state between affirmation and negation. He concludes that this is a departure from true knowledge and a lapse into ignorant sophistry.

Misconception: Some think that denying all attributes about Allah is the safest form of tawhid and avoids anthropomorphism.
Correction: Nasafi shows that this is actually sophistry and a departure from knowledge, because it leads to logical absurdity and rejects the intellect's ability to know Allah through proper negations.

The extreme Batiniyya position of refusing both positive and negative attributes is sophistry, not true tawhid, and is rejected by reason and revelation.

Why Shared Attributes Do Not Imply Similarity
Imam Nasafi argues that shared existence and knowledge do not imply similarity due to different modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fact that Allah and creation share descriptions like 'existent' and 'knower' does not entail similarity between them, due to the differing modes of these attributes.

Core Idea
Shared words do not mean shared reality—modes matter.

Imam al-Nasafi here tackles a common objection: if God and humans both 'exist' and 'know,' aren't they similar? He shows that the words themselves do not carry the specific modalities that would ground similarity. The argument is concise but profound, relying on a clear distinction between caused and uncaused existence, and between acquired and non-acquired knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parallel structure: existence/knowledge, caused/uncaused, inferrer/necessitated.
2
Identify the key terms
Look at the Arabic terms: موجد (causer), مستدل (inferrer), مضطر (necessitated). Understand that these specify the 'mode' of existence or knowledge.
The terms are crucial for the argument.
3
Contrast the modes
Observe the contrast: created existence is caused, God's existence is uncaused; created knowledge is either inferential or necessitated, God's knowledge is neither.
This is the core distinction.
4
Understand the implication
Realize that the words 'existent' and 'knower' do not convey these modes. So similarity, which would require equality in the full description, cannot be based on the words alone.
The opponent's argument collapses.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Nasafi declares the opponents' view corrupt. Consider why this matters for Islamic theology: it protects divine transcendence without denying attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم: إن الموجود منا موجد، والعالم منا مستدل أو مضطر، والله تعالى موجود بلا موجد عالم ليس بمستدل ولا بمضطر، والمشابهة لو وقعت بالمساواة في وصف الوجود، والعلم، وقولنا: إنه موجود لا يتعرض لكونه موجدا ولا قولنا عالم لكونه مستدلا أو مضطرا، فلا تقع به المماثلة لانعدام تعرضه لما تقع به المماثلة، فدل أن ما ذهبوا إليه فاسد.
Then we say to them: The existent among us is caused, and the knower among us is either an inferrer or necessitated. Allah Most High is existent without a causer, a knower not by inference nor by necessity. And similarity, if it occurred, would be by equality in the description of existence and knowledge. But our saying 'He is existent' does not address His being caused, nor our saying 'knower' addresses His being inferrer or necessitated. So similarity does not occur by it, because it does not address that by which similarity occurs. So it indicates that what they have gone to is corrupt.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a proof that affirming the attributes of existence and knowledge for both God and created beings does not entail similarity (tamathul) between them. Al-Nasafi addresses an opponent (perhaps a personificationist or a critic) who might argue that if God and creatures share these attributes, then they are similar. The proof proceeds: the existence of created things is caused (mawjud bi-mujid), while God's existence is uncaused. Similarly, the knowledge of created beings is either acquired through inference (istidlal) or by necessity (idtirar) (as in self-evident knowledge), whereas God's knowledge is neither inferential nor necessitated. Therefore, the mere verbal agreement in the words 'existent' and 'knower' does not convey the specific modalities that would ground similarity. Since the word 'existent' does not imply 'caused' and the word 'knower' does not imply 'inferrer or necessitated', the attributes do not serve as vehicles for similarity. Thus the opponents' claim is invalid (fasid).

Key Terms
موجود (mawjud)
موجد (mujid)
عالم (alim)
مستدل (mustadill)
مضطر (mudtarr)
مماثلة (mumathala)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that sharing a linguistic term necessarily implies similarity in reality.
Correction: This passage refutes that by showing that the terms 'existent' and 'knower' are used equivocally: the mode of existence and knowledge differs fundamentally between Creator and creation.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam al-Nasafi argue that shared existence and knowledge do not entail similarity between God and creation?
A) Because existence and knowledge are not real attributes of God.
B) Because the modes of existence and knowledge differ: God is uncaused and His knowledge is not acquired.
C) Because the opponents deny that God has knowledge.
D) Because language is irrelevant to similarity.

Key Takeaway
Shared descriptions like 'existent' and 'knower' do not imply similarity because the modes of these attributes are fundamentally different between Allah and creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you say 'Allah knows everything' and then 'I know something', do you unconsciously imagine His knowledge as just a bigger version of yours? How does this lesson force you to correct that? What will you do today to train yourself to remember the difference?

The Maturidi method of negation and affirmation in divine attributes
Al-Nasafi explains the practice of adding negations to preserve divine transcendence while affirming attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi school uses the formula 'not like X' to affirm divine attributes while negating any resemblance to creatures, and why this method was pioneered by Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Affirm the attribute, negate the similarity — that is the Maturidi way.

In this passage, al-Nasafi explains a key Maturidi practice: when using a common name to describe God, they add a phrase that explicitly cancels any imagined similarity. This is not mere rhetoric but a careful theological protection for those who might misunderstand. Al-Nasafi credits Abu Hanifa as the first to formulate this approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the pattern: each attribute is immediately followed by 'not like...'.
The Arabic phrase 'لا كالموجودين' is the key.
2
Understand the rationale
Ask why the extra negation is needed. Al-Nasafi says it is to protect the imagination of those unfamiliar with theological subtleties from falling into error.
The word 'صيانة' (protection) is central.
3
Identify the origin
Note that Abu Hanifa is explicitly mentioned as the first to give this advice. This situates the method within the Hanafi-Maturidi tradition.
4
Connect to broader theology
This method balances affirmation (ithbat) and negation (nafy): it affirms the real attribute while denying any likeness to creation.
This is a hallmark of Maturidi kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مع هذا أنا نلحق بالاسم المشترك ما يوجب نفي ما يسبق إلى الوهم من معنى المماثلة، وإن لم يكن اللفظ متعرضا لذلك صيانة منا لأوهام من لا دراية له بالحقائق عن لحوق وصمة وعيب. فنقول: هو موجود لا كالموجودين، حي لا كالأحياء، عالم لا كالعلماء، وكذا في جميع الصفات ليخبر به عن ثبوت ما هو ثابت من حقيقة المعنى، ونفي ما هو منفي عن المماثلة، وأبو حنيفة – رحمه الله هو السابق إلى هذه النصيحة لعامة الخلق ثم اتبعه المتكلمون على ذلك.
Then in addition to this, we attach to the common name what necessitates the negation of the meaning of similarity that precedes to the imagination, even if the word itself does not address that, as a protection on our part for the fancies of those who have no knowledge of realities from being affected by a stain and defect. So we say: He is existent not like existents, living not like the living, knowing not like the knowers, and likewise in all the attributes, so that it conveys about the affirmation of what is established of the reality of the meaning, and the negation of what is negated concerning similarity. And Abu Hanifa – may Allah have mercy on him – was the foremost in this advice to the common people, then the mutakallimun followed him in that.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage explains the Maturidi method of combining affirmation with negation when speaking about God's attributes. Al-Nasafi states that even though a common name (e.g., 'existent') is used for both God and creation, they 'attach' to it a phrase that explicitly cancels any imagined similarity (like 'not like existents'). This is done as a precautionary measure for those unfamiliar with theological subtleties, to prevent them from falling into error. The formula 'He is existent not like existents, living not like the living, knowing not like the knowers' affirms the reality of the attribute (e.g., existence, life, knowledge) while denying any likeness to creatures. This practice was pioneered by Abu Hanifa and later adopted by the mutakallimun. The point is that the attribute is real and positive in God, but it is not comparable to the attribute in creatures.

Key Terms
اسم مشترك (ism mushtarak)
وهم (wahm)
صيانة (siyana)
مماثلة (mumathala)
أبو حنيفة (Abu Hanifa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'not like existents' is a mere verbal trick.
Correction: It is a precise theological safeguard that affirms the reality of the attribute while protecting against anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint

A) To deny that God has any attributes.
B) To protect the imagination of uninformed people from thinking there is similarity.
C) To show that God's attributes are metaphorical.
D) To make the language more poetic.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi method of adding 'not like' after each attribute is a precise theological safeguard that affirms God's real attributes while preserving His absolute uniqueness.

Shared Names Do Not Imply Similarity
Nasafi lists linguistic counterexamples to show shared names do not imply similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how linguistic evidence from Arabic demonstrates that shared names do not imply similarity between things.

Core Idea
Shared names do not create similarity; language itself proves otherwise.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi begins his refutation of al-Nashi by appealing to the actual usage of Arabic speakers. He lists numerous examples where the same word is applied to opposite things in a literal sense, showing that sharing a name does not entail any similarity between the named objects. This lesson examines those examples and the key principle they establish.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation once to get the flow of the argument. Notice the list of examples.
The examples are dense; take your time.
2
Identify the examples of shared names
Find each example: 'nāhil', 'ḥillal', 'qur'', 'kawn', 'mawjūd', 'color', 'taste', 'smell', and 'ayn'. Note that each word applies to two different things literally.
The point is that these words are used literally for opposites, not metaphorically.
3
Understand the conclusion
Nasafi states that these examples show that shared names do not necessitate similarity. The final sentence asserts that the name itself has no effect in proving similarity.
This refutes al-Nashi's claim that similarity follows from shared naming.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفساد هذا القول مما يقف عليه من لا معرفة له بهذه الصفات فإن أهل اللغة أطلقوا أسماء كثيرة على المتضادين على كل واحد منهما حقيقة كاسم الناهل للعطشان ،والريان والحلل للصغير والكبير، والقرء للحيض والطهر ، وكذا اسم الكون ينطلق على المتضادين، وكذا الموجود وكذا اللون والطعم والرائحة في أمثلة لذلك كثيرة، وكذا يطلقون الأسماء المشتركة على مواضع مختلفة كاسم العين وغيره من غير أن يوجب ذلك مماثلة بين المسميات بها، ولأنا قد بينا أن لا أثر للاسم في إثبات المشابهة. ثم يقال له: إنك أثبت إطلاق هذه الأسماء على الله تعالى وعلى المخلوقات بطريق الحقيقة لئلا يلزم الاشتراك والمماثلة، وجعلت ذلك في أحد المحلين حقيقة وفي المحل الآخر مجازا فرارا عن إثبات المماثلة، وقد بينا أن الاسم بطريق الحقيقة كثيرا ما ينطلق على المحلين من غير أن يكون بينهما مشابهة وقط لا ينقل الاسم من محل الحقيقة إلى غيره بطريق المجاز إلا بمناسبة قوية ومشابهة متأكدة فيما بينهما حتى قال أهل اللغة: إن المجاز تشبيه بدون كاف التشبيه وذلك لدلالة تأكد المشابهة بينهما ، إذ لو ذكر حرف التشبيه، فقيل : هذا كالأسد ربما يسبق إلى وهم السامع أنه يثبت المشابهة بينهما لاشتراكهما في أصل الشجاعة دون استوائهما في قدرها، وإذا قيل: هو أسد فقد جعل الأسد ذاته، لأن خبر المبتدأ إذا كان فردا كان عين المبتدأ، وإذا كان ذاته أسدا كانت نهاية شجاعة الأسد فيه، وإذا كانت المشابهة لازمة بين محل المجاز ومحل الحقيقة، ولا لزوم لها عند جعل الاسم في المحلين جميعا ثابتا بطريق الحقيقة
And the corruption of this statement is something that one who has no knowledge of these attributes can understand. For the experts in language (Ahl al-Lugha) have applied many names to opposites, each one literally, like the name 'nāhil' for the thirsty and the satiated, and 'ḥillal' for the small and the big, and 'qur'' for menstruation and purity. Likewise the name 'kawn' (being) applies to opposites, and likewise 'mawjūd' (existing), and likewise color, taste, and smell in many examples. And likewise they apply shared names to different loci, like the name 'ayn' (eye/spring) and others, without that necessitating similarity between the named things. And because we have established that the name has no effect in proving similarity. Then it is said to him: You have established the application of these names to God and to creatures by way of literalness to avoid sharing and similarity, and you made that in one locus literal and in the other metaphorical, fleeing from establishing similarity. And we have established that the name by way of literalness frequently applies to two loci without there being similarity between them; and absolutely the name is not transferred from a literal locus to another by way of metaphor except with a strong relationship and an emphatic similarity between them, to the point that the experts in language said: Metaphor is a comparison without the letter of comparison (kāf), and that indicates the emphasis of similarity between them. For if the letter of comparison were mentioned, and it is said 'this is like a lion', perhaps it might come to the mind of the hearer that it establishes similarity between them due to their sharing in the root of courage without their equality in its measure. And when it is said 'he is a lion', one has made the lion his very essence, because the predicate of a subject when it is a single noun is identical to the subject. And if his essence is a lion, then the utmost courage of the lion is in him. And if similarity is necessary between the locus of metaphor and the locus of literalness, there is no necessity for it when the name is established in both loci by way of literalness.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is the refutation of al-Nashi's position. The author states that anyone with even a basic knowledge of God's attributes (ṣifāt) can see the falsity of al-Nashi's claim. He then provides linguistic counterexamples: Arabic speakers apply many names to opposite things in a literal sense. For instance, 'nāhil' can mean both thirsty and satisfied; 'ḥillal' can mean both small and large; 'qur'' can mean both menstruation and purity. Similarly, 'kawn' (being), 'mawjūd' (existing), and terms like color, taste, and smell are applied to opposites without implying similarity. Also, names like 'ayn' (eye/spring) are used for different things without implying similarity. The author emphasizes that he has already shown that a name has no bearing on proving similarity. Then he addresses al-Nashi directly: 'You claimed that these names (e.g., existent, living) must be literal in one locus and metaphorical in the other to avoid similarity, but we have shown that a name can be literal for both without implying similarity. Moreover, metaphor (majāz) actually requires a strong connection and emphatic similarity between the two things, as the linguists say: metaphor is a comparison without the letter of comparison (kāf). The omission of the kāf indicates an even stronger similarity. The author offers the example of calling a brave man a 'lion'. If one says 'this man is like a lion', one might think the similarity is only in the root of courage, not in its full measure. But if one says 'he is a lion', it equates the subject with the essence of a lion, implying maximum courage. Thus, metaphor implies a stronger similarity than literal usage. Therefore, if al-Nashi claims the names are metaphorical either on God or creatures, he would be asserting a strong similarity between them, which is the opposite of his intention. In contrast, holding the names literal for both without implying similarity is the correct position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share a name, they must be similar in some essential way.
Correction: Arabic usage shows many words applied to opposites literally, without any similarity implied.

Checkpoint

A) 'nāhil' for thirsty and satiated
B) 'asad' for lion and brave man
C) 'mā'' for water and essence
D) 'shajar' for tree and lineage

Key Takeaway
Shared names in Arabic do not necessitate similarity between the named objects.

?

Metaphor Implies Stronger Similarity than Literal Usage
Nasafi analyzes metaphor to show it implies strong similarity, refuting al-Nashi's position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic nature of metaphor and why it implies a stronger similarity than literal usage, as argued by Nasafi.

Core Idea
Metaphor is comparison without 'like' – it asserts identity.

In this part of the refutation, Nasafi turns al-Nashi's own argument against him. Al-Nashi claimed that names like 'existent' and 'living' must be applied literally to either God or creatures and metaphorically to the other to avoid similarity. Nasafi shows that metaphor actually requires a strong similarity, whereas literal usage does not. This lesson unpacks the linguists' definition of metaphor and the famous example of calling a brave man a 'lion'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the quote from linguists and the lion example.
Pay attention to the distinction between 'like a lion' and 'is a lion'.
2
Understand the linguists' definition of metaphor
The linguists say: 'Metaphor is a comparison without the letter of comparison (kāf).' This omission emphasizes similarity.
The absence of 'like' makes the comparison more direct.
3
Analyze the lion example
Compare 'he is like a lion' (suggests partial similarity) with 'he is a lion' (equates the subject with the essence of a lion, implying maximum courage).
This shows that metaphor claims identity, not just similarity.
4
Connect to al-Nashi's position
If al-Nashi says the names are metaphorical either for God or creatures, he would be asserting a strong similarity, which is the opposite of his intention. Literal usage for both avoids that implication.
Nasafi's argument is clever: al-Nashi's attempt to avoid similarity through metaphor actually backfires.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفساد هذا القول مما يقف عليه من لا معرفة له بهذه الصفات فإن أهل اللغة أطلقوا أسماء كثيرة على المتضادين على كل واحد منهما حقيقة كاسم الناهل للعطشان ،والريان والحلل للصغير والكبير، والقرء للحيض والطهر ، وكذا اسم الكون ينطلق على المتضادين، وكذا الموجود وكذا اللون والطعم والرائحة في أمثلة لذلك كثيرة، وكذا يطلقون الأسماء المشتركة على مواضع مختلفة كاسم العين وغيره من غير أن يوجب ذلك مماثلة بين المسميات بها، ولأنا قد بينا أن لا أثر للاسم في إثبات المشابهة. ثم يقال له: إنك أثبت إطلاق هذه الأسماء على الله تعالى وعلى المخلوقات بطريق الحقيقة لئلا يلزم الاشتراك والمماثلة، وجعلت ذلك في أحد المحلين حقيقة وفي المحل الآخر مجازا فرارا عن إثبات المماثلة، وقد بينا أن الاسم بطريق الحقيقة كثيرا ما ينطلق على المحلين من غير أن يكون بينهما مشابهة وقط لا ينقل الاسم من محل الحقيقة إلى غيره بطريق المجاز إلا بمناسبة قوية ومشابهة متأكدة فيما بينهما حتى قال أهل اللغة: إن المجاز تشبيه بدون كاف التشبيه وذلك لدلالة تأكد المشابهة بينهما ، إذ لو ذكر حرف التشبيه، فقيل : هذا كالأسد ربما يسبق إلى وهم السامع أنه يثبت المشابهة بينهما لاشتراكهما في أصل الشجاعة دون استوائهما في قدرها، وإذا قيل: هو أسد فقد جعل الأسد ذاته، لأن خبر المبتدأ إذا كان فردا كان عين المبتدأ، وإذا كان ذاته أسدا كانت نهاية شجاعة الأسد فيه، وإذا كانت المشابهة لازمة بين محل المجاز ومحل الحقيقة، ولا لزوم لها عند جعل الاسم في المحلين جميعا ثابتا بطريق الحقيقة
And the corruption of this statement is something that one who has no knowledge of these attributes can understand. For the experts in language (Ahl al-Lugha) have applied many names to opposites, each one literally, like the name 'nāhil' for the thirsty and the satiated, and 'ḥillal' for the small and the big, and 'qur'' for menstruation and purity. Likewise the name 'kawn' (being) applies to opposites, and likewise 'mawjūd' (existing), and likewise color, taste, and smell in many examples. And likewise they apply shared names to different loci, like the name 'ayn' (eye/spring) and others, without that necessitating similarity between the named things. And because we have established that the name has no effect in proving similarity. Then it is said to him: You have established the application of these names to God and to creatures by way of literalness to avoid sharing and similarity, and you made that in one locus literal and in the other metaphorical, fleeing from establishing similarity. And we have established that the name by way of literalness frequently applies to two loci without there being similarity between them; and absolutely the name is not transferred from a literal locus to another by way of metaphor except with a strong relationship and an emphatic similarity between them, to the point that the experts in language said: Metaphor is a comparison without the letter of comparison (kāf), and that indicates the emphasis of similarity between them. For if the letter of comparison were mentioned, and it is said 'this is like a lion', perhaps it might come to the mind of the hearer that it establishes similarity between them due to their sharing in the root of courage without their equality in its measure. And when it is said 'he is a lion', one has made the lion his very essence, because the predicate of a subject when it is a single noun is identical to the subject. And if his essence is a lion, then the utmost courage of the lion is in him. And if similarity is necessary between the locus of metaphor and the locus of literalness, there is no necessity for it when the name is established in both loci by way of literalness.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is the refutation of al-Nashi's position. The author states that anyone with even a basic knowledge of God's attributes (ṣifāt) can see the falsity of al-Nashi's claim. He then provides linguistic counterexamples: Arabic speakers apply many names to opposite things in a literal sense. For instance, 'nāhil' can mean both thirsty and satisfied; 'ḥillal' can mean both small and large; 'qur'' can mean both menstruation and purity. Similarly, 'kawn' (being), 'mawjūd' (existing), and terms like color, taste, and smell are applied to opposites without implying similarity. Also, names like 'ayn' (eye/spring) are used for different things without implying similarity. The author emphasizes that he has already shown that a name has no bearing on proving similarity. Then he addresses al-Nashi directly: 'You claimed that these names (e.g., existent, living) must be literal in one locus and metaphorical in the other to avoid similarity, but we have shown that a name can be literal for both without implying similarity. Moreover, metaphor (majāz) actually requires a strong connection and emphatic similarity between the two things, as the linguists say: metaphor is a comparison without the letter of comparison (kāf). The omission of the kāf indicates an even stronger similarity. The author offers the example of calling a brave man a 'lion'. If one says 'this man is like a lion', one might think the similarity is only in the root of courage, not in its full measure. But if one says 'he is a lion', it equates the subject with the essence of a lion, implying maximum courage. Thus, metaphor implies a stronger similarity than literal usage. Therefore, if al-Nashi claims the names are metaphorical either on God or creatures, he would be asserting a strong similarity between them, which is the opposite of his intention. In contrast, holding the names literal for both without implying similarity is the correct position.

Names as Haqiqa for Opposites Without Similarity
The author refutes the opponent's use of majāz to avoid similarity between God and creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that names can be used literally (ḥaqīqa) for different or even opposite entities without necessitating any similarity between them, and how the opponent's use of majāz to avoid similarity actually backfires.

Core Idea
Literal names can unite opposites without implying resemblance.

This lesson tackles a critical point in the debate about divine attributes: when the same name applies to God and creation, does it force us to say they are similar? The author argues no—using a name literally for two different things does not imply any commonality of essence. He proves this with linguistic examples and then turns the tables on those who try to escape similarity by labeling one usage as metaphor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the opening examples: names applied to opposites as literal terms. Notice the author's assertion that this does not require similarity.
The examples include 'thirsty', 'garment', 'menstrual period', 'existence', 'color', and 'eye'.
2
Understand the opponent's position
The opponent tries to avoid similarity by making the name literal for one (e.g., God) and metaphorical for the other (creation). The author refutes this by showing that metaphor actually requires a strong similarity between the two loci.
This is the key logical move the author makes.
3
Explore the nature of metaphor
The author quotes linguists: metaphor is a simile without the particle 'like'. This means it implies an even stronger resemblance than a simile. The example 'he is a lion' is given to illustrate that the predicate becomes identical to the subject, showing utmost similarity.
Contrast this with a simile like 'he is like a lion', which is weaker.
4
Grasp the author's conclusion
The author concludes that the opponent's position is absurd: by using majāz for one locus, he actually commits himself to affirming a necessary similarity between God and creatures, contradicting his own goal.
Thus, the safer position is to treat both loci as literal, which does not imply similarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أهل اللغة أطلقوا أسماء (١) كثيرة على المتضادين على كل واحد منهما حقيقة كاسم الناهل للعطشان والريان والحلل للصغير والكبير والقرء للحيض والطهر وكذا اسم الكون ينطلق على المتضادين وكذا الموجود وكذا اللون والطعم والرائحة في أمثلة لذلك كثيرة وكذا يطلقون الأسماء المشتركة على مواضع مختلفة كاسم العين وغيره من غير أن يوجب ذلك (٢) مماثلة بين المسميات (٣) بها ولأنا قد بينا أن لا أثر للاسم في إثبات المشابهة ثم يقال له إنك أثبت إطلاق هذه الأسماء على الله تعالى وعلى المخلوقات بطريق الحقيقة لئلا يلزم الاشتراك والمماثلة وجعلت ذلك في أحد المحلين حقيقة وفي المحل الآخر مجازا فرارا عن إثبات المماثلة وقد بينا أن الاسم بطريق الحقيقة كثيرا ما ينطلق على المحلين من غير أن يكون بينهما مشابهة وقط لا ينقل الاسم من محل الحقيقة إلى غيره بطريق المجاز إلا بمناسبة قوية ومشابهة متأكدة فيما بينهما (٤) حتى قال أهل اللغة إن المجاز تشبيه بدون كاف التشبيه وذلك لدلالة تأكد المشابهة بينهما إذ لو ذكر حرف التشبيه فقيل هذا كالأسد ربما يسبق إلى وهم السامع أنه يثبت المشابهة (٥) بينهما لاشتراكهما في أصل الشجاعة دون استوائهما في قدرها وإذا قيل هو أسد فقد جعل الأسد ذاته لأن خبر المبتدأ إذا كان فردا كان (٦) عين المبتدأ وإذا كان ذاته أسدا كانت نهاية شجاعة الأسد فيه وإذا كانت المشابهة لازمة بين محل المجاز ومحل الحقيقة ولا لزوم لها عند جعل الاسم في المحلين جميعا ثابتا (٧) بطريق الحقيقة على ما قررت فمن فرض جعل الاسم في المحلين حقيقة لئلا يلزمه إثبات أن ذلك غير موجب للمماثلة وجعل الاسم في أحد المحلين مجازا وفي مع الآخر حقيقة أن ثبوت المشابهة فيه لازمة فلا غاية لحمقه ولا نهاية لجهله بواقع الأسامي
For the people of the language have applied many names to opposites, each one of them as a literal (haqīqa) term, like the name 'thirsty' (al-nāhil) for the thirsty and the satiated (al-rayyān), and 'garment' (al-ḥulal) for the small and the large, and 'menstrual period' (al-qar') for menstruation and purity, and likewise the name 'existence' (al-kawn) applies to opposites, and likewise 'existent' (al-mawjūd), and likewise color, taste, and smell, with many examples of that. And likewise they apply shared names to different places, like the name 'eye' (al-ʿayn) and others, without that necessitating similarity between the named things. And because we have shown that the name has no effect in establishing similarity. Then it is said to him: You have affirmed applying these names to God Almighty and to creatures as literal (ḥaqīqa) in order to avoid commonality and similarity, and you made that in one of the two loci as literal and in the other locus as metaphorical (majāz), fleeing from establishing similarity. And we have shown that the name as literal often applies to the two loci without there being any similarity between them, and absolutely the name is not transferred from the literal locus to another by way of metaphor except with a strong correspondence and an assured similarity between them, until the people of language said: Metaphor is a simile without the particle of simile (kāf al-tashbīh), and that is because of the indication of the assured similarity between them. For if the particle of simile were mentioned, saying 'this is like a lion', it might come to the mind of the listener that it establishes similarity between them because they share in the essence of courage without being equal in its measure. But when it is said 'he is a lion', then he has made the lion itself his essence, because the predicate of a subject, if it is a single word, is the same as the subject. And if his essence is a lion, then the utmost courage of the lion is in him. And if similarity is necessary between the locus of metaphor and the locus of literal, and there is no necessity for it when the name is made in both loci as literal according to what you have established, then whoever supposes making the name in both loci as literal so that he avoids affirming that that does not necessitate similarity, and makes the name in one locus as metaphor and in the other locus as literal, then the establishment of similarity in it is necessary. So there is no limit to his foolishness and no end to his ignorance of the reality of names.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent who, to avoid implication of similarity (mumāthala) between God and creatures, proposes that names applied to both are literal (ḥaqīqa) in one locus and metaphorical (majāz) in the other. The author argues that names are often used literally for opposites without implying similarity, as shown by linguistic examples: words like 'thirsty' apply to both the thirsty and the satiated, 'garment' to both small and large, 'menstrual period' to menstruation and purity. Even apparently synonymous or shared names like 'existence' and 'color' apply to different things without necessitating resemblance. Moreover, metaphor actually requires a strong similarity between the two loci—so strong that linguists say majāz is a simile without the particle 'like' (ka). If one says 'Zayd is a lion', it implies the utmost lion-like courage in him, because the predicate is identical to the subject. Therefore, the opponent's move to use majāz for one locus actually commits him to affirming a necessary similarity between God and creatures, contradicting his aim to avoid similarity. Making both loci literal does not imply similarity, as shown by linguistic usage. So the opponent's position is absurd and shows ignorance of how names really work.

Correction: The author shows through linguistic examples that names are used literally for opposites (like thirsty/satiated) without implying any resemblance; literal usage does not entail similarity.

Key Takeaway

The Criterion for Literal and Metaphorical Usage
Definition of criterion with illustration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the practical criterion for distinguishing literal (ḥaqīqa) from metaphorical (majāz) usage based on the possibility of truthful negation.

Core Idea
If you can truthfully deny a term, it's metaphorical; if you can't, it's literal.

Before we can discuss whether divine attributes are literal or metaphorical, we need a clear criterion to tell the difference. The author provides a simple test: if a negation of the term is true, then the usage is metaphorical; if false, literal. This lesson unpacks that criterion and its application to the example of 'lion'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author presents the criterion before the example.
The passage uses a conditional structure: 'what is possible to negate... then it is metaphorical.'
2
Understand the criterion
Identify the two parts of the criterion: (1) if negation is possible and the negator is not a liar → majāz; (2) if negation is impossible and the negator would be a liar → ḥaqīqa.
The key is that the negation itself must be truthful or false.
3
Apply it to the lion example
See how the criterion works with 'lion': saying of a real lion 'not a lion' is false (literal), but saying of a brave human 'not a lion' is true (metaphorical).
This concrete example makes the abstract criterion easy to grasp.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له إن العلم الفاصل بين الحقيقة والمجاز أن ما يصح نفيه ولم يكن نافيه في (١) نفيه كاذبا كان مجازا وما (٢) لا يصح نفيه وكان نافيه في نفيه كاذبا فهو حقيقة ألا ترى أن من قال الحيوان الذي له زئير إنه ليس بأسد كان كاذبا ولو قال للآدمي الشجاع إنه ليس بأسد بل هو آدمي كان صادقا ثم إذا قلت في (٣) أحد الروايتين أن هذه الأسامي لله تعالى مجازا وللخلق حقيقة كان مقتضي قولك إن من قال إن الله تعالى ليس بموجود كان صادقا وكذا إذا قال إنه ليس بحي ولا قادر ولا عالم ولا سميع ولا بصير ولا خالق وهذا هو التعطيل الصريح والدهرية المحضة وإذا قلت على الرواية الأخرى إن هذه الأسامي لله تعالى حقيقة وللخلق مجاز كان مقتضى قولك أن من قال الناشى لا يعلم الله تعالى ولا (٤) رسوله ولا صحة دين الإسلام ولا بطلان ما وراء الإسلام من الأديان ولا يعلم نفسه أنه موجود ولا أنه مؤمن ولا أنه في الدنيا ولا يعلم السماء ولا الأرض ولا شيئا من الحقائق كان صادقا في مقالته وهذا هو السوفسطائية الكبرى ثم من العجب أن من زعم أن العبد له علم والله تعالى لا علم له على زعمه لنفيهم الصفات ثم
Then it is said to him: The distinguishing knowledge between literal (ḥaqīqa) and metaphorical (majāz) is that what is possible to negate, and its negator in his negation is not a liar, then it is metaphorical. And what is not possible to negate, and its negator in his negation is a liar, then it is literal. Do you not see that the one who says of an animal that has a roar 'it is not a lion' is a liar, but if he says of the brave human 'he is not a lion, but a human', he is truthful. Then if you say in one of the two reports that these names are metaphorical for God Almighty and literal for creatures, then the implication of your statement is that the one who says 'God Almighty is not existent' is truthful, and likewise if he says 'He is not living, nor powerful, nor knowing, nor hearing, nor seeing, nor creator'. This is pure taʿṭīl (denial of attributes) and absolute materialism (dahriyya). And if you say in the other report that these names are literal for God Almighty and metaphorical for creatures, then the implication of your statement is that the one who says 'the growing person does not know God Almighty, nor His messenger, nor the correctness of the religion of Islam, nor the falsity of other religions besides Islam, nor does he know himself that he exists, nor that he is a believer, nor that he is in the world, nor does he know the sky nor the earth nor any realities' is truthful in his saying. This is the great sophism (al-sūfisṭāʾiyya al-kubrā). Then it is amazing that the one who claims that the servant has knowledge while God Almighty has no knowledge — according to his claim due to their denial of attributes — then...
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage provides a clear criterion to distinguish literal (ḥaqīqa) from metaphorical (majāz) usage. The criterion is practical: if a negation of the term for something is possible and the negator is not a liar, then the term is used metaphorically for that thing; if the negation is impossible and the negator would be a liar, then it is literal. For example, negating 'lion' of a specific roaring animal is false, so 'lion' is literal for that animal; but negating it of a brave human is true, so it is metaphorical. The author then applies this criterion to the opponent's two possible positions. The first position: names like 'existent', 'living', etc., are metaphorical for God and literal for creatures. Then saying 'God is not existent' would be truthful, because you can negate a metaphorical term without lying. This leads to outright denial of God's existence and attributes (taʿṭīl) and pure materialism (dahriyya). The second position: names are literal for God and metaphorical for creatures. Then saying 'a person does not know God, His messenger, the truth of Islam, or any reality' would be truthful, because the metaphorical application of knowledge to creatures can be negated without lying. This leads to total skepticism (sūfisṭāʾiyya). Both positions are absurd and self-defeating. The author ends by noting the irony of those who affirm knowledge for the servant but deny it for God (the Muʿtazila), though the sentence is cut off at the page break.

Key Terms
حقيقة (ḥaqīqa)
مجاز (majāz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The distinction between literal and metaphorical is arbitrary or purely conventional.
Correction: The author provides an objective criterion: the truthfulness of negation. It is not arbitrary but based on whether the term can be truthfully denied of the subject.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a term considered metaphorical (majāz)?
A) When negating it makes the speaker a liar.
B) When negating it is possible and the speaker is truthful in that negation.
C) When it is used in a poetic context.
D) When it refers to God.

Key Takeaway
The criterion for ḥaqīqa vs. majāz is the truthfulness of negation: if negating the term is true, it's metaphorical; if false, it's literal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Powerful', do you apply the criterion from this lesson? If someone denied that Allah is All-Powerful, would they be a liar? Is 'All-Powerful' literal or metaphorical for Allah? How does your answer affect your awe of Him? Commit this week to test one attribute term with this criterion and reflect on what it reveals about your understanding of Allah.
?
Think of a quality you admire in a fellow human—like 'generous'. If you called them 'ocean of generosity', is that literal or metaphorical? Now, when you read verses like 'Allah is the Forgiving', do you assume it is literal? Could it be metaphorical? How does the possibility of metaphor change your reliance on His forgiveness? Will you investigate one attribute this week with the same logical test?
