Refutation of Opponent's Positions on Divine Attributes
Refutation of two opponent positions using criterion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how both possible positions on the literal/metaphorical status of divine names lead to absurd consequences: either outright denial of God (taʿṭīl) or complete skepticism (sūfisṭāʾiyya).

Core Idea
If divine names are metaphorical for God, you can deny them truthfully; if they are literal for God, you can deny them for creatures truthfully — both lead to absurdity.

Having established the criterion for literal and metaphorical, the author now applies it to the debate about divine attributes. He shows that the opponent's two possible positions both collapse into unacceptable outcomes. This lesson explores these consequences and the logical force of the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The author presents two alternative positions: (1) divine names are metaphorical for God and literal for creatures, or (2) they are literal for God and metaphorical for creatures. Notice how each is introduced.
These are the only two logical possibilities given the context.
2
Trace the implications of the first position
If names are metaphorical for God, then negating them of God would be truthful (by the earlier criterion). This means saying 'God is not existent' would be true, leading to ta'til and dahriyya.
Ta'til means denial of God's attributes, and dahriyya is pure materialism.
3
Trace the implications of the second position
If names are literal for God and metaphorical for creatures, then negating them of creatures would be truthful. This would mean a person does not truly know God, religion, or any reality — leading to total skepticism (sufista'iyya).
The author calls this 'the great sophism'.
4
Reflect on the irony
The author ends by noting the absurdity of those (like the Mu'tazila) who affirm knowledge for the servant but deny it for God, showing inconsistency.
The passage is cut off, but the point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له إن العلم الفاصل بين الحقيقة والمجاز أن ما يصح نفيه ولم يكن نافيه في (١) نفيه كاذبا كان مجازا وما (٢) لا يصح نفيه وكان نافيه في نفيه كاذبا فهو حقيقة ألا ترى أن من قال الحيوان الذي له زئير إنه ليس بأسد كان كاذبا ولو قال للآدمي الشجاع إنه ليس بأسد بل هو آدمي كان صادقا ثم إذا قلت في (٣) أحد الروايتين أن هذه الأسامي لله تعالى مجازا وللخلق حقيقة كان مقتضي قولك إن من قال إن الله تعالى ليس بموجود كان صادقا وكذا إذا قال إنه ليس بحي ولا قادر ولا عالم ولا سميع ولا بصير ولا خالق وهذا هو التعطيل الصريح والدهرية المحضة وإذا قلت على الرواية الأخرى إن هذه الأسامي لله تعالى حقيقة وللخلق مجاز كان مقتضى قولك أن من قال الناشى لا يعلم الله تعالى ولا (٤) رسوله ولا صحة دين الإسلام ولا بطلان ما وراء الإسلام من الأديان ولا يعلم نفسه أنه موجود ولا أنه مؤمن ولا أنه في الدنيا ولا يعلم السماء ولا الأرض ولا شيئا من الحقائق كان صادقا في مقالته وهذا هو السوفسطائية الكبرى ثم من العجب أن من زعم أن العبد له علم والله تعالى لا علم له على زعمه لنفيهم الصفات ثم
Then it is said to him: The distinguishing knowledge between literal (ḥaqīqa) and metaphorical (majāz) is that what is possible to negate, and its negator in his negation is not a liar, then it is metaphorical. And what is not possible to negate, and its negator in his negation is a liar, then it is literal. Do you not see that the one who says of an animal that has a roar 'it is not a lion' is a liar, but if he says of the brave human 'he is not a lion, but a human', he is truthful. Then if you say in one of the two reports that these names are metaphorical for God Almighty and literal for creatures, then the implication of your statement is that the one who says 'God Almighty is not existent' is truthful, and likewise if he says 'He is not living, nor powerful, nor knowing, nor hearing, nor seeing, nor creator'. This is pure taʿṭīl (denial of attributes) and absolute materialism (dahriyya). And if you say in the other report that these names are literal for God Almighty and metaphorical for creatures, then the implication of your statement is that the one who says 'the growing person does not know God Almighty, nor His messenger, nor the correctness of the religion of Islam, nor the falsity of other religions besides Islam, nor does he know himself that he exists, nor that he is a believer, nor that he is in the world, nor does he know the sky nor the earth nor any realities' is truthful in his saying. This is the great sophism (al-sūfisṭāʾiyya al-kubrā). Then it is amazing that the one who claims that the servant has knowledge while God Almighty has no knowledge — according to his claim due to their denial of attributes — then...
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage provides a clear criterion to distinguish literal (ḥaqīqa) from metaphorical (majāz) usage. The criterion is practical: if a negation of the term for something is possible and the negator is not a liar, then the term is used metaphorically for that thing; if the negation is impossible and the negator would be a liar, then it is literal. For example, negating 'lion' of a specific roaring animal is false, so 'lion' is literal for that animal; but negating it of a brave human is true, so it is metaphorical. The author then applies this criterion to the opponent's two possible positions. The first position: names like 'existent', 'living', etc., are metaphorical for God and literal for creatures. Then saying 'God is not existent' would be truthful, because you can negate a metaphorical term without lying. This leads to outright denial of God's existence and attributes (taʿṭīl) and pure materialism (dahriyya). The second position: names are literal for God and metaphorical for creatures. Then saying 'a person does not know God, His messenger, the truth of Islam, or any reality' would be truthful, because the metaphorical application of knowledge to creatures can be negated without lying. This leads to total skepticism (sūfisṭāʾiyya). Both positions are absurd and self-defeating. The author ends by noting the irony of those who affirm knowledge for the servant but deny it for God (the Muʿtazila), though the sentence is cut off at the page break.

Key Terms
تعطيل (taʿṭīl)
دهرية (dahriyya)
سوفسطائية (sūfisṭāʾiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that saying divine names are metaphorical for God is harmless; the author shows it leads to outright denial of existence.
Correction: According to the criterion, if 'existent' is metaphorical for God, then saying 'God is not existent' is truthful — which implies God does not exist, a clear absurdity.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of the second position (names literal for God, metaphorical for creatures)?
A) It leads to ta'til.
B) It leads to dahriyya.
C) It leads to sufista'iyya (skepticism).
D) It leads to affirming God's attributes.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's positions on divine attributes are self-defeating: one leads to atheism (taʿṭīl), the other to total skepticism (sūfisṭāʾiyya).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is knowing' but quickly add 'but not like our knowing', does your heart truly affirm God's perfect knowledge, or do you subtly fall into the trap of denying it? What would it cost you to lose that certainty?
?
If the skeptic's position were true, you could not trust that you know God or His messenger. Have you ever considered that your faith stands on the very literal meaning of these names? How does that realization press you to cling more firmly to the truth?

Why Allah's Knowledge is Unlike Created Knowledge
Al-Nasafi proves Allah's knowledge is unlike created knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why describing both Allah and creatures as 'knowing' does not imply any resemblance between them.

Core Idea
Shared names do not mean shared reality when attributes differ fundamentally.

Earlier lessons established the principle that similitude requires equality in the attribute itself. Now Al-Nasafi applies this principle to the attribute of knowledge. Even though Allah and His creatures are both described as 'knowing', the nature of their knowledge is radically different. This passage demonstrates two decisive differences that prevent any similarity.

Guided Path
1
Recall the condition for similitude
The passage begins by referencing what has come before: similitude only arises when two things are equal in an attribute and share in its reality. Keep this condition in mind.
This is the logical foundation of the argument.
2
Identify the two differences given
Al-Nasafi lists two pairs of opposing characteristics: eternal vs. originated, and necessary vs. contingent. Notice that each pair describes the same distinction from a different angle.
These are essential, not accidental, differences.
3
Draw the conclusion
Because of these differences, the knowledge of Allah and created knowledge cannot be equal. Therefore, no similitude exists between them.
The conclusion flows directly from the premise.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Ask yourself: how does this argument protect divine transcendence? It shows that shared names do not compromise God's uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۱۰ - عدم المماثلة بين علم الله وعلم المخلوق: وبالوقوف على ما تقدم أيضا عرف أن الله تعالى وإن كان عالما وغيره كان عالما لم تثبت مماثلة بين الله تعالى وبين غيره لأن المماثلة إنما تثبت بين عالم وعالم إذا تساويا في العلم واشتركا في حقيقته، وعلم الله تعالى مخالف لعلم غيره، فإن علم الله قديم وعلم غيره محدث، وعلم الله واجب الوجود وعلم غيره جائز الوجود، فعلم الله ليس مثلا لعلم غيره، ولا يساويه في شيء، فلا مماثلة بينهما.
10 - Non-similarity between the knowledge of Allah and the knowledge of the created: And by understanding what has preceded, it is also known that Allah, exalted, even if He is knowing and other than Him is knowing, no similitude is established between Allah, exalted, and other than Him, because similitude is established only between a knower and a knower when they are equal in knowledge and share in its reality. And the knowledge of Allah, exalted, is different from the knowledge of other than Him, for the knowledge of Allah is eternal (qadīm) and the knowledge of other than Him is originated (muḥdath), and the knowledge of Allah is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) and the knowledge of other than Him is contingent of existence (jā'iz al-wujūd). So the knowledge of Allah is not similar to the knowledge of other than Him, nor is it equal to it in anything, so there is no similitude between them.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a proof that Allah's knowledge is not similar to created knowledge. Building on the earlier discussion of similitude (mumāthala), the author argues that even though both Allah and creatures are described as 'knowing' (ālim), this does not imply similitude. Similitude requires equality in the attribute and sharing in its reality. However, Allah's knowledge is eternal (qadīm) and necessary (wājib al-wujūd), whereas created knowledge is originated (muḥdath) and contingent (jā'iz al-wujūd). Since these characteristics are fundamentally different, there is no equality or similitude between them. Therefore, the shared description 'knowing' does not compromise divine transcendence.

محدث (muḥdath)

جائز الوجود (jā'iz al-wujūd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If both Allah and creatures are described as 'knowing', they must be similar in some way.
Correction: Al-Nasafi explains that similitude requires equality in the attribute and sharing in its reality. Since Allah's knowledge is eternal and necessary while created knowledge is originated and contingent, they are not equal, so no similitude follows from sharing the name 'knowing'.

Key Takeaway

Why Shared Names Do Not Imply Similarity
Nasafi proves the non-similarity of God’s knowledge to created knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming the same attribute name (e.g., 'knowing') for God and creatures does not imply similarity, based on the fundamental differences in their realities.

Core Idea
Shared names do not mean shared natures.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a key concern in Islamic theology: how can we describe God with terms like 'knowing' and 'powerful' without falling into anthropomorphism? He demonstrates that the realities behind these attributes are incomparable. This lesson walks through his argument and its wider implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the full passage in Arabic and then the English translation. Notice the logical structure of the argument.
2
Identify the key differences between God’s knowledge and created knowledge
Nasafi lists several differences: eternality, encompassing all things, and not being necessary or acquired. List them and reflect on why each difference disproves similarity.
3
Understand the extension to other attributes
Nasafi says the same applies to power, life, hearing, and sight. Consider how the same reasoning holds.
4
Grasp the refutation of the Bāṭiniyya
The passage ends with a critique of those who avoid affirming attributes altogether out of fear of tashbīh. Understand why this fear is misplaced according to Nasafi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالوقوف على ما تقدم أيضا عرف أن الله تعالى وإن كان عالما وغيره كان عالما لم تثبت مماثلة بين الله تعالى وبين غيره لأن المماثلة إنما تثبت بين عالم وعالم لثبوت المماثلة بين علميهما، ولا مماثلة بين علم الله تعالى وعلم غيره، لأن علم الله تعالى دائم وهو لا يفنى، وهو يشتمل على المعلومات أجمع، وهو ليس بضروري ولا مكتسب، وعلم غيره عرض مستحيل البقاء لا يشتمل علم واحد على معلومات كثيرة بل يعلم كل معلوم بعلم على حدة، فهو إما ضروري وإما مكتسب فلم يكن بينهما مماثلة، فلم يكن بين العالم والعالم مماثلة، وكذا هذا في القدرة والحياة والسمع والبصر، وظهر بهذا جهل بعض المنتسبين إلى الفلسفة من الأوائل، وجهالة الباطنية من امتناعهم عن وصف الله تعالى بأنه شيء، حي، عالم، قادر، سمیع، بصير، تحرزا عن وقوع التشبيه، لما بينا أنه لا تشابه يحصل بهذا على ما قررنا.
And by pausing upon what has preceded, it is also known that God Almighty, even if He is knowing and another is knowing, no similarity is established between God Almighty and another, because similarity is only established between a knower and a knower through the establishment of similarity between their two knowledges. And there is no similarity between the knowledge of God Almighty and the knowledge of another, because the knowledge of God Almighty is everlasting, it does not perish, and it encompasses all known things, and it is neither necessary nor acquired. And the knowledge of another is an accident, impossible of endurance; a single knowledge does not encompass many known things, but rather he knows each known thing by a separate knowledge. So it is either necessary or acquired. So there was no similarity between them, and consequently no similarity between the knower and the knower. And likewise this applies to power, life, hearing, and sight. And by this the ignorance of some who claim philosophy among the ancients became apparent, and the ignorance of the Bāṭiniyya who refrain from describing God Almighty as a thing, living, knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing, out of caution against the occurrence of anthropomorphism, because we have made clear that no similitude results from this, as we have established.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a proof against anthropomorphism (tashbīh) regarding God's attributes, specifically knowledge. Nasafi argues that although both God and creatures are described as 'knowing' ('ālim), this shared term does not imply similarity (mumāthala) between them. Similarity in knowers would require similarity in their knowledge, but God's knowledge and created knowledge are fundamentally different in three respects: 1) God's knowledge is eternal (dā'im) and imperishable, while created knowledge is an accident ('araḍ) that cannot endure. 2) God's single knowledge encompasses all known things (ma'lūmāt) at once, whereas a creature's knowledge is particular—each known thing requires a separate act of knowledge, and one act of knowledge cannot cover many things. 3) God's knowledge is neither necessary (ḍarūrī) nor acquired (muktasab)—it is essential and self-subsistent—while created knowledge is either necessary (e.g., self-evident truths) or acquired through reasoning. These radical differences show that the attribute of knowledge in God is not comparable to that in creatures. Nasafi then extends this argument to other attributes like power, life, hearing, and sight. He concludes that this reasoning exposes the error of the ancient philosophers and the Bāṭiniyya, who avoided affirming such attributes for God out of fear of anthropomorphism, not realizing that the mere affirmation of terms does not entail similitude when the realities differ so greatly.

Key Terms
علم ('ilm)
مماثلة (mumāthala)
تشبيه (tashbīh)
عرض ('araḍ)
ضروري (ḍarūrī)
مكتسب (muktasab)
باطنية (Bāṭiniyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming the same attribute name for God and creatures implies they are alike in that attribute.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the realities are fundamentally different, so the shared term does not entail similarity.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is God's knowledge not similar to created knowledge?
A) Because God's knowledge is acquired, not necessary
B) Because God's knowledge is an accident that perishes
C) Because God's knowledge is eternal and encompasses all things at once
D) Because created knowledge is also eternal

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are affirmed without anthropomorphism because the realities are incomparable—shared names do not imply shared natures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Knowing,' do you truly believe that His knowledge is absolutely unlike any knowledge you possess? In what moment do you still slip into thinking of God's knowledge as just a bigger version of your own?

You claim to affirm God's attributes, but deep down, are you uncomfortable that we use the same word 'merciful' for God and for humans? What does this passage demand you change in your heart to overcome that unease?

Refuting the Attribution of Māhiyya to Allah
Nasafi refutes the attribution of māhiyya to Allah and its implication of tashbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing māhiyya (quiddity) to Allah leads to anthropomorphism, and how Nasafi refutes the claim that Abu Hanifa held this view.

Core Idea
Māhiyya implies genus, and genus implies similarity – this is why it is denied of Allah.

Imam Nasafi addresses a challenging objection: reports that Abu Hanifa himself described Allah as a 'māhiyya' known only to Him. If true, this would implicate the Hanafi school in tashbih. Nasafi first clarifies what māhiyya means – it is a question about genus, not mere existence – and then shows that claiming a quiddity for Allah entails similarity to created things. He then begins to answer the historical claim by questioning the authenticity of the reports.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the report cited by the objector and note its sources. The objector uses this to argue that tashbih is part of Abu Hanifa's doctrine.
The report is attributed to several figures, but Nasafi will later question its authenticity.
2
Analyze the meaning of māhiyya
Follow Nasafi's linguistic and logical proof that māhiyya means homogeneity and implies genus. Pay attention to the question words and the definition of genus.
This is the core argument: māhiyya leads to tashbih.
3
Examine the initial response
See how Nasafi denies the authenticity of the report from Abu Hanifa's true companions. The answer is incomplete but the direction is clear.
Nasafi does not engage with the theological content of the report; he first questions its transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : كيف أنكرتم التشبيه وقد ثبت من مذهب إمامكم أبي حنيفة أنه كان يقول: الله مائية لا يعلمها إلا هو ) (۲) روي هذه المقالة عن ضرار بن عمرو (۳) أبو القاسم الكعبي (4) في المقالات، وكذا روي عنهما هذه المقالة أبو محمد الحسيني بن موسی بن نوبخت (۹) في كتابه المسمى بالآراء والديانات، وهو آخر باب من كتابه. قال (1) أبو محمد وحكاه ابن الراوندي (۷) عن حفص (۸) الفرد قال: وحكي رجل من علماء أصحاب هشام بن الحكم (۱) عن هشام أن الله تعالى مائية لا يعلمها إلا هو، وحكى عن بعض أرباب المقالات هذا القول عن سفيان بن سحبان (۲) وهو من متكلمي أصحاب أبي حنيفة والمائية عبارة عن المجانسة، وهي معنى وراء مطلق الوجود، والدليل على أن المائية عبارة عن المجانسة أن الناس يقولون: ما هذا الشيء أي (۳) من أي جنس هو وأهل اللغة يقولون : كلمة هل سؤال عن مطلق الوجود، وما سؤال عن جنسه وكم سؤال عن قدره، وكيف سؤال عن صفته، ومتى سؤال عن زمانه وأين سؤال عن مكانه. وأهل المنطق يقولون : في حد اسم الجنس : إنه الاسم الدال على كثيرين مختلفين بالنوع في جواب ما هو، والنوع هو الاسم الدال على كثيرين مختلفين بالشخص في جواب ما هو، فدل أن المائية (٤) عبارة عن الجنس، وكل ذي جنس شبيه بذي جنسه من حيث المجانسة، فكان القول بالمائية قول بالتشبيه. تحقيق قول أبي حنيفة في مائية الله]: رحمه الله غير فنقول وبالله التوفيق : إن هذه الرواية عن أبي حنيفة صحيحة لم يروها عنه أحد من أصحابه الناقلين لعلمه العارفين بحقائق مذهبه، ولا [؟]
For it is said: How do you reject anthropomorphism (tashbīh) when it has been established from the doctrine of your imam Abū Ḥanīfa that he used to say: 'Allah is a mā'iyya (quiddity) that none knows but Him'? This statement was reported from Ḍirār ibn ʿAmr (2), Abū al-Qāsim al-Kaʿbī (4) in al-Maqālāt, and likewise this statement was reported from them by Abū Muḥammad al-Ḥusaynī ibn Mūsā ibn Nawbakht (9) in his book entitled al-Ārāʾ wa al-Diyānāt, and it is the last chapter of his book. Abū Muḥammad said, and Ibn al-Rāwandī (7) narrated it from Ḥafṣ (8) al-Fard, who said: A man from the scholars of the companions of Hishām ibn al-Ḥakam (1) narrated from Hishām that Allah, exalted, is a mā'iyya that none knows but Him. And he narrated this saying from some of the authors of articles about Sufyān ibn Saḥbān (2), and he is among the theologians of the companions of Abū Ḥanīfa. And al-mā'iyya is an expression for homogeneity (al-mujānasa), and it is a meaning beyond absolute existence. And the proof that al-mā'iyya is an expression for homogeneity is that people say: 'What is this thing?' i.e., from which genus is it? And the linguists say: The word 'hal' (هل) is a question about absolute existence, and 'mā' (ما) is a question about its genus, and 'kam' (كم) is a question about its quantity, and 'kayfa' (كيف) is a question about its attribute, and 'matā' (متى) is a question about its time, and 'ayna' (أين) is a question about its place. And the logicians say in the definition of the noun of genus: It is the noun indicating many different in species in answer to 'what is it?' and the species is the noun indicating many different in person in answer to 'what is it?' So it is shown that al-mā'iyya is an expression for genus, and everything that has a genus is similar to that which has its genus in terms of homogeneity. Thus, the assertion of mā'iyya is an assertion of anthropomorphism. [Investigation of Abū Ḥanīfa's statement regarding the mā'iyya of Allah]: May Allah have mercy on him. Not. Then we say, and success is from Allah: Verily this report from Abū Ḥanīfa is authentic; none of his companions who transmitted his knowledge and were knowledgeable of the realities of his doctrine narrated it from him, nor [؟]
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector raises a report that Abu Hanifa said 'Allah is a mā'iyya (quiddity) known only to Him,' which the objector uses to argue that tashbih (anthropomorphism) is part of Abu Hanifa's doctrine, since mā'iyya implies homogeneity (mujānasa). Nasafi first presents the objection, citing multiple sources for the report (Dirar ibn Amr, al-Ka'bi, Ibn Nawbakht, Ibn al-Rawandi, Hafs al-Fard, Hisham ibn al-Hakam, and Sufyan ibn Sahban). Then he explains that mā'iyya means homogeneity, which is a meaning beyond mere existence. He provides two proofs: (1) linguistic: the word 'mā' (what) asks about genus, while 'hal' asks about existence; (2) logical: the definition of a genus noun is that it answers 'what is it?' for many different species. Therefore, mā'iyya indicates genus, and everything with a genus is similar to others of that genus, so claiming mā'iyya for Allah leads to tashbih. Then Nasafi begins his answer: he denies that the report is authentically transmitted from Abu Hanifa, stating that none of his true companions narrated it. The answer is cut off, but the denial is clear. Thus, the passage guards against the misunderstanding that Abu Hanifa held an anthropomorphic view.

Key Terms
المائية (al-mā'iyya)
المجانسة (al-mujānasa)
التشبيه (al-tashbīh)
مطلق الوجود (muṭlaq al-wujūd)
اسم الجنس (ism al-jins)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu Hanifa actually affirmed a quiddity (māhiyya) for Allah, which would mean he held an anthropomorphic view.
Correction: Nasafi argues that the report is not authentically transmitted from Abu Hanifa by his trusted companions, and that the very concept of māhiyya implies tashbih, so it cannot be attributed to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the linguistic argument in the passage, what does the word 'mā' (ما) ask about?
A) Absolute existence
B) Genus of a thing
C) Quantity of a thing
D) Time of a thing

Key Takeaway
The term māhiyya necessarily implies genus and thus similarity, so it cannot be applied to Allah, and the report attributing it to Abu Hanifa lacks authentic transmission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever carelessly described Allah using words or concepts that imply similarity to creation? What terms do you use that might need re-evaluation in light of this lesson?
?
The report attributing mā'iyya to Abu Hanifa is dismissed as inauthentic. Do you accept transmitted religious reports without verifying their chain and content? How can you cultivate a critical attitude towards historical claims?
?
Knowing that even a well-intentioned term like 'mā'iyya' can lead to tashbīh, are you willing to abandon any cherished phrases or concepts about Allah that do not align with the Qur'an and Sunnah, even if they come from respected figures? What is one such phrase you need to let go of today?

Refutation of Circular Reasoning on Divine Greatness
Nasafi refutes a circular definition of God's greatness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi exposes circular reasoning in defining God's attribute of greatness (al-'azama) and why such reasoning is invalid.

Core Idea
Circular reasoning proves nothing — defining greatness by itself is empty.

Nasafi here addresses a specific objection: opponents try to define God's greatness (al-'azama) not as size or rank, but as the ability to meet multiple objects while remaining one. He shows that this definition merely restates the very thing it tries to explain, making it a vicious circle. The passage uses a clear analogy to drive the point home.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: opponents' claim, then Nasafi's response.
The passage uses a conditional 'if they say' construction.
2
Identify the opponents' definition
Find where the opponents define greatness. They say it is 'that He meets more than one while His essence is one.' Underline this.
This definition is the target of refutation.
3
Understand the circularity
See how Nasafi shows that this definition uses the same idea it is supposed to explain. He then gives an analogy (world eternal because eternal) to illustrate the fallacy.
Circular reasoning is also called petitio principii.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذاته فإن قالوا: لا نريد بالعظمة ما ذكرتم من الضخامة أو جلال القدر بل نريد به أنه يلاقي أكثر من واحد مع توحد ذاته قيل لهم : هذا الكلام باطل، لأنكم تقولون : إنه مع توحده يلاقي أكثر من واحد لعظمته ثم تفسرون عظمته وتقولون : إن هذه العظمة أنه يلاقي أكثر من واحد مع توحده فيصير حاصل كلامكم أنه مع توحده يلاقي أكثر من واحد، لأنه مع توحده يلاقي أكثر من واحد ، وهذا جعل الحكم عليه لنفسه، ولو جاز ذا لجاز لقائل أن يقول : العالم قديم لأنه قديم، وللعالم صانعان لأنهما صانعان، وغير ذلك، وهذا كله باطل فكذا هذا.
His essence. So if they say: We do not mean by greatness what you mentioned of bulk or majesty of rank, but we mean by it that He meets more than one while His essence is one, it is said to them: This statement is false, because you say: He, while His essence is one, meets more than one by His greatness, then you interpret His greatness and say: This greatness is that He meets more than one while His essence is one. So the sum of your statement becomes: He, while His essence is one, meets more than one, because He, while His essence is one, meets more than one. And this is making the judgment about itself. And if this were allowed, then a speaker could say: The world is eternal because it is eternal, and the world has two makers because they are two makers, and other such things. And all of this is false, so this is likewise.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage refutes a circular definition of God's attribute of greatness (al-'azama). The author begins by setting up the opponents' claim: they define greatness not as physical size or majestic rank, but as the ability to 'meet' (yulaqi) multiple objects while remaining one in essence. The author then exposes the circularity: they explain greatness by that very same property—meeting many while being one—so their definition merely restates the point to be proved. This is a logical fallacy: making the judgment about itself (جعل الحكم عليه لنفسه). The author draws an analogy: it would be like saying 'the world is eternal because it is eternal' or 'the world has two makers because it has two makers.' Such reasoning is invalid, so the original definition is false.

Key Terms
عظمة
توحد
جعل الحكم على نفسه
باطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the opponents' definition is merely a semantic preference, but Nasafi shows it is logically invalid due to circularity.
Correction: The fallacy is not about word choice but about reasoning: defining a term by the very effect it is supposed to explain is circular and proves nothing.

Checkpoint
What logical fallacy does Nasafi identify in the opponents' definition of greatness?
A) Circular reasoning (petitio principii)
B) False analogy
C) Appeal to authority
D) Straw man

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that defining divine greatness by the very effect it is supposed to explain is circular and logically invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defined something about God using words that merely repeat what you are trying to prove, like 'God is great because He is great'? When you pray or speak about His attributes, do you sometimes fall into empty circular statements without realising it? What will you do to ensure your words about Allah are based on sound reasoning, not just repetition?
?
Nasafi uses analogies to expose circular reasoning: 'The world is eternal because it is eternal.' In your own life, are there beliefs or claims you hold that rely on circular justifications? Are you willing to examine them and discard those that prove empty?
?
The opponents' definition of greatness failed because it did not add new knowledge—it just restated the effect. When you describe Allah’s attributes to yourself or others, are you content with surface-level descriptions, or do you strive to understand deeper, non-circular meanings? Commit to learning one attribute this week from a reliable source with its proper logical basis.

The Absurd Consequence of Unbounded Greatness
Nasafi argues that the opponents' position forces God to be in all places by essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opponents' claim about God's greatness leads to the absurd conclusion that God is in every place by essence.

Core Idea
Unlimited greatness cannot stop at the Throne.

This passage delivers a powerful reductio ad absurdum: if the opponents allow God's greatness to meet countless things, why limit it to only some parts of the world? Nasafi forces them to admit that infinite greatness would require God to be everywhere, even below the world—an absurdity they cannot escape.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the rhetorical question and the author's confident tone in condemning the opponents' view.
Pay attention to the key term 'la tanahi' (infinite).
2
Understand the rhetorical question
The question is: if greatness allows one essence to meet many things, why not all things? The opponents admit no limit to greatness, so it must logically apply everywhere.
This is a classic reductio argument.
3
Grasp the reason: infinite greatness
Nasafi states that greatness is infinite (la tanahi li 'azamatihi), so it cannot be restricted to the area of the Throne. The opponents have no escape from this implication.
Infinite means no boundary—if essence meets by greatness, it meets all.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The author declares that such reasoning leads to impossibilities and nonsense, leaving no doubt about the falsehood of the opponents' doctrine.
Note the strong language: 'muhalat' (impossibilities) and 'turahat' (nonsense).
5
Reflect on divine transcendence
This argument reinforces that God is not confined to space or direction. The absurd consequence warns against any anthropomorphism.
Relate to the broader theme of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: لما جاز أن يلاقي ذات واحد أشياء كثيرة لعظمته لمَ لَمْ يلاق جميع أجزاء العالم فيكون في كل مكان بذاته، ويكون تحت العالم كما كان فوقه؟ وهذا لأنه لا تناهي لعظمته فلا يقتصر على ما بساحة العرش من الأجزاء، وهذا مما لا انفصال لهم عنه. والكلام إذا انتهى إلى مثل هذه المحالات وأفضى المذهب بصاحبه إلى ركوب مثل هذه الترهات لم يبق لذي لب ريبة في بطلانه، ولا لمنصف تردد في فساده، والحمد لله على العصمة.
Then it is said to them: Since it is permissible that one essence meets many things by its greatness, why does it not meet all parts of the world, so that it would be in every place by its essence, and be under the world as it was above it? And this is because His greatness is infinite, so it is not confined to the parts that are in the area of the Throne. And this is something from which they have no escape. And when speech reaches such impossibilities and the doctrine leads its adherent to perpetrate such nonsense, no intelligent person remains in doubt about its falsehood, nor any just person hesitant about its corruption. And praise be to God for protection.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage extends the refutation by asking a further question: if greatness permits meeting many things, why not all things? Since the opponents admit no limit to greatness, it should allow meeting all parts of the world, implying that God is everywhere by His essence—above and below. The author emphasizes that greatness is infinite (لا تناهي لعظمته), so it cannot be confined to the area of the Throne. The opponents have no escape from this consequence. The author then concludes that such reasoning leads to impossibilities (محالات) and nonsense (ترهات), and no rational person should have any doubt about the falsehood of such a doctrine. He ends with praise to God for protection from such errors.

Key Terms
العظمة
كل مكان
لا تناهي
ساحة العرش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think God's greatness allows Him to 'meet' many things but not all, restricting His presence without reason.
Correction: If greatness is infinite, there is no rational basis to limit it to only some parts. Thus, the opponents' view collapses into pantheism or spatial confinement.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why must God meet all parts of the world if greatness is the basis?
A) Because greatness is limited to the Throne area
B) Because greatness is infinite, so it cannot be confined
C) Because God is not in space
D) Because the opponents admit it

Key Takeaway
The opponents' position leads to the absurdity that God must be in every place by essence, which no reasonable person can accept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God being 'above the Throne', do you ever unconsciously restrict His infinite greatness to a specific location? How would you correct that thought right now?
?
The opponents here believed in God's greatness yet still fell into an absurdity. Are there any cherished beliefs you hold about God that, if you followed them to their logical end, would contradict His perfection? Will you examine them today?
?
Nasafi ends with praise for protection from such error. Do you truly feel gratitude that Allah has guided you to sound belief, or do you take it for granted? What would it cost you to lose that clarity?

The Lexical Meanings of al-Istiwa
Lists five lexical meanings of al-istiwa with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five main lexical meanings of the Arabic word al-istiwa and how they inform theological discussion of the divine attribute.

Core Idea
Al-istiwa has multiple meanings—only careful context determines which is intended.

Before theologians apply the word istiwa to Allah, they must first understand its full lexical range. Imam al-Nasafi provides a concise survey of five distinct meanings, each supported by Quranic or poetic evidence. This lexical foundation prevents hasty conclusions about the divine attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the passage once focusing on the flow: each meaning is introduced with 'ويذكر ويراد به' and then illustrated.
Notice the Quranic verses and poetry used as evidence.
2
Identify the five meanings
List each meaning: settlement, straightness, completion, domination, elevation. Check the Arabic terms against the translation.
Elevation is further subdivided into two types.
3
Examine the subdivision of elevation
The passage states elevation splits into physical highness and highness of rank. Reflect on why this distinction matters for divine attributes.
This prepares for the doctrine that Allah's istiwa is of rank, not physical location.
4
Reflect on the theological relevance
Ask yourself: If istiwa can mean domination or elevation of rank, which meaning is appropriate for Allah? The commentary will help connect to tanzih.
This passage lays groundwork for rejecting anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاستواء يذكر ويراد به الاستقرار كقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَاسْتَوَتْ عَلَى الْجُودِي ﴾ ويذكر ويراد به الاستقامة التي هي ضد الاعوجاج - ويذكر ويراد به التمام على ما قال تعالى: ﴿ وَلَمَّا بَلَغَ أَشُدَّهُ وَاسْتَوَى ﴾ ويذكر ويراد به الاستيلاء كما يقال: استوى فلان على بلده كذا قال القائل في بشر بن مروان: قد استوى بشر على العراق من غير سيف ودم مهراق. وقال الآخر: اذكر بلانا بصفين ونصرتنا حتى استوى لأبيك الملك في عدن أي ثبت له الملك فيه. ويذكر ويراد به الارتفاع والعلو كما قال تعالى: ﴿ اسْتَوَيْتَ أَنتَ وَمَن مَّعَكَ ﴾ وهذا النوع ينقسم إلى قسيم يذكر ويراد به العلو من حيث المكان، ويذكر ويراد به العلو من حيث الرتبة.
And al-istiwa is mentioned and intended by it settlement, as His statement the Exalted: 'And it settled upon al-Judi.' And it is mentioned and intended by it straightness, which is opposite of crookedness. And it is mentioned and intended by it completion, as the Exalted said: 'And when he reached his full strength and became complete.' And it is mentioned and intended by it domination, as it is said: So-and-so dominated over his city, as the poet said concerning Bishr ibn Marwan: 'Bishr has dominated over Iraq without sword and bloodshed.' And another said: 'Remember our calamity at Siffin and our victory until the kingdom became established for your father in Aden,' meaning his kingship was established therein. And it is mentioned and intended by it elevation and highness, as the Exalted said: 'You and those with you are elevated.' And this type divides into two divisions: it is mentioned and intended by it highness in terms of place, and it is mentioned and intended by it highness in terms of rank.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage classifies the various lexical meanings of the Arabic word "الاستواء" (al-istiwa). It begins by stating that the word can be used to mean "settlement" (الاستقرار), citing the Quranic verse about the ark settling on Mount Judi. Then it can mean "straightness" (الاستقامة) as opposed to crookedness. It can also mean "completion" (التمام), as in the verse about Prophet Moses reaching his full strength. Another meaning is "domination" (الاستيلاء), illustrated by the poetic line about Bishr ibn Marwan gaining control over Iraq without war, and another line about a king establishing his rule in Aden. Finally, it can mean "elevation" (الارتفاع والعلو), which subdivides into physical elevation and rank elevation. This lexical survey sets the stage for the theological application to God's attribute of istiwa.

Key Terms
الاستواء
الاستقرار
الاستقامة
التمام
الاستيلاء
الارتفاع
العلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think istiwa only means physical sitting or settlement.
Correction: This passage shows istiwa has at least five meanings, including domination and highness of rank.

Checkpoint

B) Straightness
C) Completion
D) Domination

Key Takeaway
The word al-istiwa carries multiple lexical meanings; understanding them prevents misapplication to Allah and supports the doctrine of divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Meaning of Istiwa as Istila and Refuting the Ash'ari Objection
Applies istiwa as istila and refutes Ash'ari objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi scholars interpret the divine attribute of istiwa as istila (domination) over the Throne and how they refute the Ash'ari objection that this implies weakness.

Core Idea
Istila does not mean weakness; custom is not language.

This passage addresses a key theological dispute regarding the meaning of God's 'istiwa' over the Throne. The Maturidi school interprets it as domination (istila), while the Ash'aris reject this because in human experience domination follows weakness. The author clarifies that the association with weakness is merely customary, not linguistic, and defends the interpretation as sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
2
Identify the two justifications
Notice how the author gives two reasons for specifying the Throne: to honor it (like 'she-camel of Allah') and to indicate domination over all else (like 'commander of the town').
These are examples of tashrif and dalala.
3
Understand the Ash'ari objection
The Ash'aris object because istila in human experience occurs after weakness. Find this objection in the passage.
4
Analyze the refutation
The author gives two counterpoints: (1) this interpretation is not exclusive to Mu'tazila, (2) the association with weakness is customary, not linguistic. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
This shows the importance of distinguishing linguistic meaning from customary context.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلى هذا فيحتمل أن يكون المراد منه استولى على العرش الذي هو أعظم المخلوقات، وتخصيصه بالذكر كان تشريفا له إذ إضافة جزئية الأشياء إلى الله تعالى ليتعظم ذلك كما قال: ﴿ نَاقَةَ اللَّهِ ﴾ وَأَنَّ الْمَسَاجِدَ لِلَّهِ ﴾ وَأَنَّهُ لَمَا قَامَ عَبْدُ اللَّهِ ﴾ وغيره. أو كان لدلالة أن ما دونه كان مستولي عليه بالاستيلاء عليه كما يقال : فلان أمير هذه البلدة وإن كان أميرا لأهلها ولرساتيقها وقراها قال الله تعالى : ﴿ وَهُوَ رَبُّ الْعَرْشِ الْعَظِيمِ ﴾ وإن كان ربا لكل شيء، وهذا كله ظاهر. وتزييف الأشعرية هذا التأويل لمكان أن الاستيلاء يكون بعد الضعف لا يتصور في الله تعالى، ونسبتهم هذا التأويل إلى المعتزلة ليس بشيء لأن أصحابنا أولوا هذا التأويل ولم تختص به المعتزلة، وكون الاستيلاء إن كان في الشاهد عقيب الضعف، ولكن لم يكن هذا عبارة عن استيلاء عن ضعف في اللغة بل ذلك ثبت على وفاق العادة. كما يقال : علم فلان وكان ذلك في المخلوقين بعد
Based on this, it is possible that what is meant by it is that He dominated the Throne, which is the greatest of created things, and its specific mention was for its honor, since the attribution of specific things to Allah the Exalted is to magnify them, as He said: 'the she-camel of Allah' and 'the mosques are for Allah' and 'when the servant of Allah stood' and others. Or it is to indicate that what is beneath it is dominated by His domination over it, as it is said: So-and-so is the commander of this town, even though he is commander of its people and its rural districts and its villages. Allah the Exalted said: 'And He is the Lord of the Mighty Throne,' even though He is Lord of everything. And all this is clear. And the Ash'aris' rejection of this interpretation because domination occurs after weakness, which cannot be conceived of Allah the Exalted, and their attribution of this interpretation to the Mu'tazila is nothing, because our companions interpreted it this way and it is not exclusive to the Mu'tazila. And even if domination in the witnessed realm is after weakness, this is not an expression of domination from weakness in the language; rather, it is established according to custom. As it is said: So-and-so knew, and that was in created beings after
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage applies the lexical meaning of istiwa as istila (domination) to God's attribute of being 'above the Throne'. It explains that when God says He 'astawa' upon the Throne, it means He dominates it, since the Throne is the greatest creation. The Throne is singled out for honor, just as God singles out certain things like 'the she-camel of Allah' or 'the mosques of Allah' for veneration. Alternatively, mentioning the Throne implies domination over all lesser things, just as calling someone 'commander of this town' implies command over the whole region, including its people and villages. The verse 'He is the Lord of the Mighty Throne' also indicates His lordship over everything. Then the passage addresses the Ash'ari objection that interpreting istiwa as istila (domination) is problematic because in human experience, domination only occurs after a state of weakness, which cannot apply to God. The author refutes this by noting that (a) this interpretation is not exclusive to the Mu'tazila; the author's own school (the Maturidis) also holds it. (b) Even if in observable reality (al-shahid) domination follows weakness, this is merely a customary occurrence, not a linguistic necessity. The word 'istila' itself does not inherently imply weakness; it simply means gaining control. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
الاستيلاء
العرش
التأويل
الأشعرية
المعتزلة
الشاهد
العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'aris think that istila cannot apply to God because in the world istila occurs after weakness.
Correction: The word 'istila' itself does not imply weakness; the association is merely customary, not part of its lexical meaning.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the Ash'ari objection to interpreting istiwa as istila?
A) Because istila in language does not necessarily imply weakness
B) Because the Mu'tazila are always wrong
C) Because the Throne is not a real object
D) Because God's power is limited

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi interpretation of istiwa as istila is linguistically sound and not invalidated by customary associations of weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Maturidi Interpretation of al-‘Arsh and al-Istiwā
Nasafi reinterprets 'Arsh as dominion and istiwā as completion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi interpretation of al-‘Arsh as dominion and al-istiwā as completion, and how this preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
If 'Arsh means dominion, then istiwā means completion, not physical sitting.

The verse about Allah's istiwā over the Throne (al-‘Arsh) has been a subject of theological interpretation. The Maturidi school, following Imam Nasafi, offers a reading that upholds tawḥīd by interpreting 'Arsh as dominion and istiwā as completion of authority. This lesson unpacks that interpretive move and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if by ‘Arsh is meant dominion, then istiwā is completion.'
2
Identify the key terms and reinterpretation
Pick out the terms 'Arsh, istiwā, and their new meanings: dominion and completion. Understand why this avoids anthropomorphism.
The author cites Ibn ‘Abbās to support the idea of completion after creation.
3
Understand the doctrinal purpose
Ask: What problem does this interpretation solve? It keeps Allah free from physical location or movement, consistent with tawḥīd and preeternity.
The author notes that Sunni imams gave multiple interpretations, all avoiding contradiction.
4
Reflect on the author's brevity
Nasafi says he omits further interpretations to avoid prolongation. Consider why this single explanation is sufficient for the student.

ولو أريد بالعرش الملك لكان الاستواء عبارة عن التمام أي تمام المملوك، كما روي عن ابن عباس - رضي الله تعالى عنهما أنه تعالى : خلق العالم في ستة أيام ثم خلق البشر في اليوم السابع فيهم التمام إذ خلق لهم كل شيء، وقد بالغ أئمة أهل السنة في بيان ما تحتمل هذه الآيات من التأويلات التي لا تنافي التوحيد والقدم. أعرضنا عن ذكرها مخافة التطويل، إذ في هذا القدر كفاية لمن نصح نفسه. وبالله الرشاد.
And if by al-‘Arsh is meant the dominion, then al-istiwā would be an expression of completion, i.e., the completion of what is possessed, as narrated from Ibn ‘Abbās (may Allah be pleased with them both) that He, exalted, created the world in six days, then created human beings on the seventh day, perfecting them by creating everything for them. The imams of Ahl al-Sunna have elaborated on the interpretations these verses can bear that do not contradict tawḥīd and preeternity. We have refrained from mentioning them for fear of prolongation, for in this amount is sufficient for whoever advises himself. And with Allah is the right guidance.

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi interpretation of two key terms: al-‘Arsh (the Throne) and al-istiwā (to establish/ascend). The author states that if ‘Arsh means dominion (al-mulk), then istiwā means completion (al-tamām) — i.e., complete mastery over creation. He then cites Ibn ‘Abbās to support that God created the world in six days and then created humans on the seventh, completing everything for them. The author notes that the Sunni imams have given various interpretations that avoid contradicting tawḥīd and God’s preeternal nature, but he omits them for brevity.

Key Takeaway

Interpreting Verses about God's Location: Sovereignty, Not Essence
Nasafi interprets verses that seem to locate God as referring to His divinity and sovereignty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi interprets Quranic verses that mention God 'in heaven' as referring to His divinity and sovereignty, not His physical location.

Core Idea
God's 'location' in the Quran means His authority, not a place.

When the Quran says God is 'in heaven,' some might think it describes a physical place. But Nasafi explains that such verses actually affirm God's sovereignty and divinity over all creation, not that His essence is contained in space. This lesson unpacks his reasoning using a powerful analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and the literal translation to get the flow of Nasafi's argument.
Notice how he repeatedly uses the same interpretive principle.
2
Analyze the ruler analogy
Nasafi explains that saying 'so-and-so is emir of Bukhara and Samarqand' refers to his authority, not his physical body. Reflect on how this clarifies the Quranic verses.
This analogy is key to understanding divine transcendence.
3
Identify the three Quranic examples
Identify each of the three verses Nasafi references and see how they all point to the same interpretation: divinity in heaven and earth, not essence.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
Consider why this interpretation is necessary for preserving God's transcendence. How does it avoid attributing physical location to God?
This guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا في قوله تعالى: (وَهُوَ الَّذِي فِي السَّمَاءِ الله وَفِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَه) أراد به ثبوت إلَه الألوهية في السماء لا ثبوت ذاته كما تقول : فلان أمير بخاري وسمرقند ويراد به أن أمارته وسلطنته فيهما لا ذاته، وكذا هذا في قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَهُوَ اللَّهُ فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَفِي الأرْضِ أي ألوهيته فيهما لا ذاته. وكذا في قوله تعالى: ﴿ أَمِنْتُم مِّن فِي السماء أي في السماء ألوهيته إلا أنها أضمرت لدلالة ما سبق من الآيات.
And they said concerning His statement, exalted: 'And He is the One who is in heaven and on earth God,' He intended by it the affirmation of divinity in heaven, not the affirmation of His essence, just as you say: So-and-so is the emir of Bukhara and Samarqand, by which is meant his authority and sovereignty in them, not his essence. And similarly in His statement, exalted: 'And He is Allah in the heavens and on the earth,' meaning His divinity in them, not His essence. And similarly in His statement, exalted: 'Do you feel secure from Him who is in heaven?' meaning in heaven His divinity, but it is omitted due to the indication of the preceding verses.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage addresses verses that might be misinterpreted as placing God in a physical location. The author explains that the phrase 'in heaven' or 'in the heavens and earth' refers to God's divinity (ulūhiyya) and sovereignty, not His essence (dhāt). He gives the analogy of a ruler: when we say 'so-and-so is the emir of Bukhara and Samarqand,' we mean his authority extends there, not that his physical body is in both places. The same applies to the three Quranic verses cited: all are understood to mean that His divinity and sovereignty are in heaven and earth, not that His essence is contained there.

Correction: Nasafi explains that it refers to His divinity and sovereignty, not His essence, using a ruler analogy.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi teaches that Quranic references to God being 'in heaven' are about His divine authority, not His physical location.

Interpreting Verses of Divine Nearness and Sovereignty
Nasafi interprets three Qur'anic verses about divine nearness and supremacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi scholars interpret verses that seem to imply divine spatial nearness or superiority as referring to God's knowledge, power, and domination.

Core Idea
God's nearness is a nearness of knowledge and power, not of space.

The Qur'an contains verses that may appear to locate God in space or in close physical proximity. Imam al-Nasafi, following the Maturidi tradition, explains these verses in terms of God's attributes—knowledge, authority, and domination—upholding divine transcendence. In this lesson, we examine his commentary on three key verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic text along with its English translation to grasp Nasafi's concise interpretations.
Pay attention to the key terms: نجوى, قريب, حبل الوريد, فوق, قاهر.
2
Understand the first interpretation
The verse about private counsel (نجوى) is explained as God's knowledge of it—He knows the conversation, not that He is physically present.
3
Understand the second interpretation
'Nearer than the jugular vein' is interpreted as authority (سلطان) and power (قدرة), not spatial closeness.
4
Understand the third interpretation
'Above everything' is interpreted as domination (قهر), supported by the verse 'He is the Dominant over His servants.'
5
Reflect on the pattern
Notice how each verse is given a metaphorical or attribute-based interpretation to preserve Allah's transcendence (tanzih).
This pattern is central to Maturidi theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله تعالى مَا يَكُونُ مِن نَجْوَى ثَلَثَةِ إِلَّا هُوَ رَابِعُهُمْ أي: يعلم ذلك ولا يخفى عليه وقوله تعالى وَنَحنُ أَقْرَبُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ حَبْلِ الْوَرِيدِ  أي: بالسلطان والقدرة. وقوله: فوق كل شيء أي بالقهر على ما قال: ﴿ وَهُوَ الْقَاهِرُ فَوْقَ عِبَادِهِ
And His statement, exalted: 'There is no private conversation of three except that He is the fourth of them' meaning: He knows that and it is not hidden from Him. And His statement, exalted: 'And We are nearer to him than the jugular vein' meaning: by authority and power. And His statement: 'Above everything' meaning: by domination, as He said: 'And He is the Dominant over His servants.'
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage interprets three verses that might suggest physical proximity or spatial superiority. The first verse, 'There is no private conversation of three except that He is the fourth of them,' is understood to mean that God knows their conversation, not that He is physically present. The second, 'We are nearer to him than the jugular vein,' means by His authority (sulṭān) and power (qudra), not spatial closeness. The third, 'Above everything,' means by domination (qahr), as supported by the verse 'He is the Dominant over His servants.'

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Interpreting the Ascension of Good Speech to God
Nasafi explains that the ascension of good speech means the record of deeds is raised to heaven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's interpretation that the ascension of good speech refers to the record of deeds being raised to heaven, not a physical movement to God.

Core Idea
Good speech ascends to God via the record of deeds in heaven.

The verse 'To Him ascends good speech' might be taken literally, but Nasafi explains it refers to the record of deeds placed in heaven. He uses analogies from Abraham's hijra and the Qur'anic emigration verse to show that 'to God' can mean 'to where He commands.' Let's read his explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi structures his argument: a claim followed by two analogies.
2
Identify the key claim
What does Nasafi say about the register of deeds (dīwān)? Where is it located, and what is the implication for the ascension of good speech?
The claim is that the register is in heaven with guardian angels.
3
Understand the analogies
How do the examples of Abraham's journey and the emigration verse support Nasafi's interpretation? Think about what 'to God' means in each case.
Both analogies show 'to God' meaning to a commanded destination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا في تعلقهم بقوله : ( إِلَيْهِ يَصْعَدُ الْكَلِمُ الطَّيِّبُ الآية : إن الله تعالى جعل ديوان أعمال العباد في السماء والحفظة من الملائكة فيها، فيكون ما رفع هناك كأنه رفع إليه لأنه أمر بذلك. كما قال إبراهيم عليه السلام: إنّ ذَاهِب إِلَى رَبِّ أي: إلى الموضع الذي أمرني أن أذهب إليه، وكما قال عز وجل: وَمَن يَخْرُجُ مِنْ بَيْتِهِ ربي مُهَاجِرًا إِلَى اللَّهِ وَرَسُولِهِ . والله أعلم.
And they said concerning their reliance on His statement: 'To Him ascends good speech' — that Allah, exalted, made the register of deeds of the servants in heaven and the guardian angels are in it, so what is raised there is as if it is raised to Him because He commanded that. As Abraham, peace be upon him, said: 'I am going to my Lord' meaning to the place He commanded me to go to, and as He said, mighty and majestic: 'And whoever leaves his house emigrating to Allah and His Messenger.' And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage interprets the verse 'To Him ascends good speech' (35:10). The author says that Allah has placed the record of deeds (dīwān a‘māl al-‘ibād) in heaven, and the guardian angels are there. Therefore, when good deeds are raised there, it is as if they are raised to Allah because He commanded that. He gives two analogies: Abraham saying 'I am going to my Lord' means going to the place he was commanded to go (i.e., for hijra), and the verse about emigrating to Allah and His Messenger, meaning to the place of His religion.

Key Takeaway

Interpreting 'Those Who Are with Your Lord' as Rank, Not Place
Nasafi explains the interpretative meaning of 'with' in divine context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quranic phrase 'with your Lord' refers to honor and station, not physical location, and how Nasafi uses other verses to support this interpretation.

Core Idea
With Allah means honor, not location.

In this lesson, we explore a key principle of Quranic interpretation: when Allah says someone is 'with Him,' it signifies a high spiritual rank, not spatial nearness. Nasafi clarifies this by examining the verse about angels and two other Quranic examples, helping us avoid anthropomorphic misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a claim followed by two supporting verses.
2
Identify the key claim
What does Nasafi say 'with your Lord' means? He explicitly says it means station (manzila), not place (makān).
This is the core of the lesson.
3
Examine the supporting evidence
Look at the two Quranic examples: Moses being 'with Allah a person of standing' and 'the religion with Allah is Islam.' How do they reinforce the meaning of honor rather than location?
These examples show consistent usage in the Quran.
4
Reflect on the importance
Why does this distinction matter for understanding Allah's transcendence? It avoids attributing spatial location to Allah.
Essential for tawheed.

وقالوا في تعلقهم بقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ عِندَ رَبِّكَ لَا يَسْتَكْبِرُونَ ﴾ يعني : الملائكة، أن المراد منه المنزلة لا المكان. كما قال في موسى عليه السلام: وَكَانَ عِندَ اللهِ وَجهًا وقال : في إِنَّ الذِينَ عِندَ الله الإسلام أي: هو الحق الذي : الْإِسْلَامُ
And they said concerning their reliance on His statement, exalted: 'Indeed, those who are with your Lord are not arrogant' meaning the angels, that what is meant is station, not place. As He said concerning Moses, peace be upon him: 'And he was with Allah a person of standing' and He said: 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam' meaning it is the truth, that Islam.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage interprets the verse 'Indeed, those who are with your Lord are not arrogant' (7:206), referring to angels. The author says that 'with your Lord' means rank and honor (al-manzila), not physical location (al-makān). He supports this with two other Quranic examples: Moses being described as 'with Allah a person of standing' (33:69), meaning honored, not physically present, and the verse 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam' (3:19), meaning it is the true religion in His judgment.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'with your Lord' means physical location near Allah.
Correction: It actually means honor and high station, as shown by other Quranic verses.

How does Nasafi interpret 'with your Lord' in the verse about angels?
A) Physical location.
B) Station and honor.
C) Knowledge.
D) Nearness in space.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'with Allah' in the Quran denotes honor and nearness in rank, not spatial proximity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

This lesson teaches that 'with Allah' is about honor and truth. What one habit can you change today to make your belief in Allah's judgment and acceptance the driving force of your actions, not your personal feelings?
?
If the angels, though closest to Allah, are still not physically with Him, how does that recalibrate your understanding of your own relationship with Him? In what area of your life have you been seeking a 'place' with Allah through outward rituals rather than seeking an honored station through sincere submission? Name one specific action you will take this week to prioritize sincerity over show.

The Argument from Eternity for God's Attributes
Proof of divine attributes from the necessity of eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the condition of eternity (qidam) necessarily entails that God possesses attributes of perfection, negating their opposites.

Core Idea
Eternity demands perfection; perfection negates defect.

To establish the attributes of God, the author first appeals to a rational necessity: the eternal cannot be subject to defects. Since eternity requires being free from any deficiency, the opposites of the divine attributes—such as death and ignorance—must be negated. This line of reasoning shows that God must possess the attributes of power, knowledge, hearing, and sight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: premise, condition, conclusion.
2
Identify the logical steps
The author states that if the Maker is eternal, and eternity requires freedom from defects, then He must be powerful, hearing, seeing, knowing. Identify the premise, the condition, and the conclusion.
The reasoning is essentially a contrapositive: if He lacked these attributes, He would have their opposites, which are defects.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Consider why attributing these qualities to God is necessary. What would be the alternative if we did not affirm them?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني الفصل الأول 1 – الكلام في إثبات صفات الله تعالى: حي وإذا ثبت أن صانع العالم قديم، ومن شرط القدم التبرؤ عن النقائص، ثبت أنه قادر سميع بصير عالم إذ لو لم يكن كذلك كان موصوفا بالموت والعجز والجهل والعمى والصمم، إذ هذه الصفات متعاقبة لتلك الصفات. فلو لم تكن هذه الصفات ثابتة الله تعالى لثبت ما يعاقبها وهي صفات نقص، ومن شرط القدم الكمال، فدل أنه موصوف بما بينا لضرورة انتفاء أضدادها التي هي من سمات الحدوث، لكونها نقائص، وإذا ثبت أيضا أنه هو المخترع لهذا العالم مع اختلاف الصنعة، أنواعه، وهو الخالق له على ما هو عليه من الإحكام والإتقان، وبديع وعجيب النظم والترتيب، وتركيب الأفلاك وما فيها من الكواكب السائرة، وما يرى من البدائع من أبدان الحيوانات من الحياة والتمييز، والاهتداء إلى اجتلاب المنافع واتقاء المضار وما فيهن من الحواس، وما في الأجسام الجمادية من البدائع والخاصيات التي أودعت فيها على وجه لو تأمل ذو البصيرة، الموصوف بدقة الفكرة وحدة الخاطر، ورجاحة العقل، وكمال الذهن، وقوة التمييز عمره فيها، لما وقف على كنهها بل ولا على جزء من ألف جزء مما فيها من آثار كمال الحكمة ولطف التدبير، ثبت أنه حي قادر عالم سميع بصير.
The second chapter, the first section: 1 – Discourse on establishing the attributes of God, the Exalted: Living. And when it is established that the Maker of the world is eternal, and from the condition of eternity is being free from defects, it is established that He is powerful, hearing, seeing, knowing — for if He were not thus, He would be described with death, incapacity, ignorance, blindness, and deafness, since these attributes are opposites of those attributes. So if these attributes were not established for God, the Exalted, then their opposites would be established, and they are attributes of deficiency, and from the condition of eternity is perfection. So it indicates that He is described with what we have mentioned due to the necessary negation of their opposites, which are marks of contingency, because they are deficiencies. And when it is also established that He is the inventor of this world with the diversity of craftsmanship, its types, and He is the Creator of it as it is of firmness and perfection, and wonderful and amazing order and arrangement, and the composition of the celestial spheres and what is in them of the moving planets, and what is seen of wonders from the bodies of animals of life and discrimination, and guidance to attracting benefits and avoiding harms, and what is in them of senses, and what is in inanimate bodies of wonders and properties that were placed in them in such a way that if a possessor of insight, described with precision of thought, sharpness of mind, soundness of intellect, completeness of intelligence, and strength of discrimination, were to spend his life reflecting on them, he would not arrive at their essence, nor even at a part of a thousand parts of what is in them of traces of perfect wisdom and subtlety of management. It is established that He is living, powerful, knowing, hearing, seeing.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the divine attributes. The author, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, presents two complementary arguments. First, he argues from the necessary condition of eternity (qidam): the Maker of the world is eternal, and eternity requires being free from defects (nawaqis). If God lacked attributes such as power (qudra), hearing (sam'), sight (basar), and knowledge ('ilm), He would necessarily be characterized by their opposites—death, incapacity, ignorance, blindness, deafness—which are defects. Since eternity demands perfection (kamal), these attributes must be affirmed. The second argument is from the intricate design of creation. The author points to the diversity and precision in the world: the heavens and planets, the bodies of animals with life, discrimination, senses, and the ability to seek benefit and avoid harm, and even inanimate objects with wondrous properties. He emphasizes that no human, regardless of intelligence and insight, can fully comprehend the wisdom embedded in creation. This perfect, ordered, and wise craftsmanship proves that the Creator is living (hayy), powerful (qadir), knowing ('alim), hearing (sami'), and seeing (basir). The passage follows a logical order: premise (eternity), implication (perfection), conclusion (attributes); then second premise (creation's wondrous design), conclusion (attributes). The argument is cumulative, showing that both a priori reasoning (from eternity) and a posteriori reasoning (from creation) converge on the same set of attributes.

Key Terms
قديم (qadim, eternal)
صفات (sifat, attributes)
نقص (naqs, deficiency)
كمال (kamal, perfection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that saying God has attributes like power implies He is composed or limited.
Correction: The passage shows that affirming attributes is necessary to avoid their opposites, which are true limitations. Attributes of perfection do not imply composition; they negate deficiency.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would be the consequence if God were not described with attributes like power and knowledge?
A) He would be described with their opposites, which are defects.
B) He would be purely simple without any attributes.
C) He would still be perfect but unknowable.
D) He would be non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The eternal nature of God requires that He possess all attributes of perfection, else He would have defects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God is eternal and perfect, and that His hearing and seeing are necessary, how would your secret thoughts and actions change? Do you sometimes act as if God might not see or hear you? What would it take for you to live with constant awareness of His attributes?
?
The passage shows that God cannot lack any attribute of perfection—He is necessarily powerful, knowing, seeing, and hearing. Does this truth make you feel more secure in His ability to respond to you, or more accountable for what He sees? Which feeling dominates your heart, and why?

The Argument from Creation for God's Attributes
Proof of divine attributes from the intricate design of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intricate design of the universe proves that the Creator is living, powerful, knowing, hearing, and seeing.

Core Idea
Perfect creation demands a perfect Creator.

After establishing the necessity of attributes from eternity, the author now turns to the observable world. The extraordinary order, composition, and wisdom in the heavens, animals, and even inanimate objects point to a Creator endowed with life, power, knowledge, hearing, and sight. No human intellect can fully grasp the depth of this wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage describing creation
Read the Arabic passage and translation, paying attention to the various examples of creation mentioned.
2
Identify the attributes implied by each aspect
For each example (celestial spheres, animals, inanimate objects), reflect on which attribute of God it demonstrates.
For instance, life and discrimination in animals point to the Creator being living and knowing.
3
Appreciate the cumulative argument
The author emphasizes that no human can fully comprehend the wisdom in creation. This shows that the Creator's knowledge and power are beyond measure.

Divine Attributes
